News
Ottawa lends flu vaccine to Mexico
Canwest News Service
191 words
7 January 2010
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A7
English
Copyright © 2010 Montreal Gazette

Canada is lending 5 million doses from its H1N1 flu vaccine stockpile to Mexico, the federal government announced yesterday.

Shipments will start heading south by the end of this week, but they are not being sent as a donation, federal Health Minister Leona Aglukkaq said in a statement.

Mexico has bought H1N1 vaccine for its population, but most of its order won't be filled by the manufacturers until the end of the month.

Canada started its vaccination program in October and has millions of doses of unused vaccine in storage because demand for the shot did not keep up with supply.

The provincial and territorial governments agreed unanimously to the Mexican government's request for 5 million doses, according to the health minister's statement.

Mexico is to replenish what it takes from Canada's supply by March 31.

Canada ordered 50.4 million doses of the vaccine from GlaxoSmithKline this summer and bought 200,000 doses from Australia. Canada is poised to have more than 20 million unused doses at the end of this flu season in April.

ARTS
A Christmas blessing
By Valerie Hill
Record staff
902 words
7 January 2010
Waterloo Region Record
TKWR
Final
B6
English
Copyright (c) 2010 Kitchener-Waterloo Record.

GUELPH

Baby Julian's grandmother cradled him for a last, loving hug before it was time to say goodbye.

"She handed him to us and said ‘Merry Christmas,' " recalls adoptive mother Joy Pryor of the incredible moment on Nov. 19, 2009 in a Kansas hospital, when her dreams of becoming a mother finally came true.

On a recent winter evening in their Guelph home, Joy and her husband Malcolm Pryor work together to dress little Julian in his best Christmas jammies. They exhibit all the caution of new parents and their constant cooing to this happy and thoroughly loved baby boy is a joy to watch. They waited so long. They tried so hard.

The couple's journey began soon after they married in July 2006. Joy, now 40 and Malcolm, 55, were childless and desperately wanted a family. Both are high school teachers: Joy teaches at Forest Heights Collegiate in Kitchener; Malcolm at Centre Wellington in Fergus. The idea of adoption rattled around in their minds, but not as a preferred option. At least, not at first.

After thousands of dollars and four years of fertility treatments leading to one disappointment after another, the idea of adoption eventually blossomed as Joy researched possibilities, starting with the provincial Ministry of Children and Youth Service (www.adoptontario.ca[http://www.adoptontario.ca]). They knew there would be hurdles, not the least of which was their ages. Joy noted the province has a strong support system for unwed mothers as well as legalized abortion, and few healthy newborns seemed to be available.

"There was so much competition," she said.

So the couple looked further afield, using the ministry's website to locate international adoption agencies, starting in Ukraine, a country where Joy had taught for a few years. Unfortunately, the European nation had tightened its adoption laws. Next was Bulgaria, but the agency there offered only older children. In March 2008, the couple learned that the United States was an option, but they had to hurry because adoption laws were changing in April, effectively eliminating out-of-country adoptions. By applying immediately, they were accepted, launching an incredible chain of events.

"We wanted to be a family," said Malcolm. "We did talk about adoption, if need be, but I didn't know how I'd feel about adopting a baby. But he (Julian) feels like my own baby."

Adoption agencies in Kansas and South Carolina requested they create a scrapbook illustrating their lives, from vacations to family photos, information used by birth parents to choose suitable adoptive parents. Then the Pryors got the call: a young, unmarried couple in Kansas was expecting a child on Joy's birthday, Dec. 11.

"They had another couple in mind, from New York," said Joy. "They'd been waiting (for a baby) for three years."

At the last moment, the couple was offered another child and withdrew their application. This past July, Joy and Malcolm flew to Kansas for an informal meeting with the birth parents at the agency's office. And, like a blind date, both parties feared the other wouldn't approve and wouldn't find them suitable or attractive enough. The young father, a high school drop out, explained his wish was for Julian to have opportunities, to be challenged to excel.

"It was a powerful experience," said Joy. Malcolm added that he knew the father was "extraordinary, bright, I'd say gifted."

After the meeting, there was nothing to do but return to Canada, to their lives and jobs. On Nov. 19, just after midnight, the phone rang: The birth mother was in hospital, nearly a month early. Could it be false labour? Uncertain, Malcolm and Joy went to work as usual. Both had just returned to the classroom after recuperating from two weeks of battling the H1N1 flu.

In the middle of a class, Malcolm got the call that the baby was coming. By noon they were on their way, bound for Wichita, Kan. Then the adoption agency drove them to a small hospital in the town of Wellington, Kan. Oddly, Guelph is in Wellington County and Joy's father and grandfather both bear the name Wellington.

Three hours after his birth, the couple stood in the nursery with baby Julian Wellington Pryor cradled in their arms.

But their ordeal was not over. Healthy but premature, Julian was kept another night by doctors. Then there was the issue of getting a passport, which required an 800-kilometre drive to a quick service Houston passport office, plus hotel stays and finalizing documents to allow them to bring the baby to Canada.

After everything Joy and Malcolm had been through, a last-minute Canadian government bureaucratic roadblock only made them more determined to bring Julian home.

Finally, on Dec. 1, all three settled into their home, a family at last. When Malcolm looks outside at the adjacent park and watches moms and dads and children, it no longer hurts because, at last, Malcolm is a dad, too.

Joy said, "the birth mother said ‘you are meant to be a mother.' It felt perfect. It's a life fulfilled."

Little Julian rarely takes his huge eyes off his mother.

"He's our little Christmas miracle," said Joy.

vhill@therecord.com

Joy and Malcolm Pryor hold their new baby Julian Wellington Pryor whom they adopted last month. David Bebee, Record staff

NEWS
Health board tackles flu threat
Vik Kirsch
Mercury Staff
438 words
7 January 2010
Guelph Mercury
GMER
Final
A1
English
Copyright (c) 2010 Guelph Mercury.

FERGUS

Wellington-Dufferin-Guelph Public Health administered and distributed almost 97,000 vaccine doses against pandemic H1N1 flu in its coverage area last fall, the organization's board of directors was told Wednesday.

Medical officer of health Dr. Nicola Mercer told them she's confident that 60 to 70 per cent of the region's population now has immunities and she doesn't expect a significant outbreak here.

Mass immunity protects against such an eventuality with the flu season continuing into the spring, she noted

"It prevents a large-scale outbreak."

Flu is still in the area, but at a low level, Mercer told board members meeting at the health board's office complex on Wellington Road 18, half way between Fergus and Elora.

Among indicators the level of infection is subsiding in the general population, absenteeism rates at area schools are declining, she said.

The 96,563 doses between late October and December represent about 35 per cent of the population, a higher rate of adoption than at some other Ontario communities, including the Waterloo region, she said.

Still, she conceded some areas have administered or distributed doses to even higher percentages of their populations, like some areas of northern Ontario.

Mercer told board directors she'll provide them with a detailed analysis of the H1N1 threat and the health board's immunization program in February.

The World Health Organization last summer declared a pandemic as H1N1 spread from Mexico and the U.S. to other countries, including Canada.

The local health board reported in November that 16 people had been hospitalized with lab-confirmed flu in its coverage area. Ten of these related to H1N1 and the rest from seasonal flu.

Three people died: two from H1N1 and a third from the seasonal variety.

Mercer's health board held a variety of community clinics throughout the area, most notably Guelph, in recent months.

It also introduced special clinics at area schools to reach school-age children. It continues to encourage immunization and offers flu shots at its offices to those requesting immunization.

Mercer said Wednesday that of the 96,563 doses administered and distributed, 43,230 were given to family physicians for their patients. Community clinics were a close second, with health board staff there administering 39,270 shots to people.

As well, hospitals received 3,590 doses. Long-term and retirement facilities were given 4,813 doses and nursing services were provided with 3,660. The University of Guelph - virtually a city within a city - was given 2,000 doses to immunize the campus community.

News
Doctors defend H1N1 blitz; $82 million spent; Two top epidemiologists back immunization drive
AARON DERFEL
The Gazette
592 words
7 January 2010
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2010 Montreal Gazette

With the second wave of the H1N1 pandemic well behind us - and far fewer deaths than expected - some observers have started to question whether a costly mass vaccination campaign was justified.

But two prominent epidemiologists argued yesterday that the broad campaign was necessary and definitely saved lives.

The Quebec government spent more than $82 million on H1N1 vaccines and other materials, and the final bill could climb to $200 million. After having vaccinated 57 per cent of the population, the province is stuck with up to 2 million doses, and is considering selling or giving away the supply to Mexico.

"As it turned out, the mortality and morbidity (illness rate) were less than what we expected," said David Buckeridge, an assistant professor of epidemiology at McGill University. "But the decision (to vaccinate everyone) was the right one, and not to do that would have been irresponsible."

Preliminary research by Buckeridge has shown that in Montreal, the "incidence curve" of new flu cases started dropping with the mass vaccinations.

In Quebec City today, Health Minister Yves Bolduc and Alain Poirier, the province's chief public health officer, are to give an update on the H1N1 situation. Poirier also is to give details about the start of the seasonal flu vaccine, which had been postponed because of H1N1.

Karine Rivard, Bolduc's press attaché, defended the decision to purchase 7.8 million vaccine doses despite initial calls by some to take a more targeted approach.

"There were a number of benefits associated with the vaccination campaign that are incalculable," Rivard said, adding that the campaign raised awareness about the importance of personal hygiene during a pandemic.

In the fall, well before the start of the vaccination campaign, polls suggested only half the population would get the shots. The polls proved to be only slightly off.

In Quebec, 2,481 people have been hospitalized as a result of H1N1 complications since the beginning of the second wave on Aug. 30, and 81 people have died.

Across Canada, the H1N1 flu was responsible for a total of 401 deaths as of Dec. 19, the latest period for which complete figures are available.

That death toll is considerably lower than estimates for the seasonal flu, which range from 1,500 to 2,000. Very few people - beyond the elderly and people with compromised immune systems - get seasonal flu shots, however, so it's hard to make a comparison.

Still, epidemiologists say the H1N1 strain of 2009 will go down in history as the least deadly of the last four flu pandemics. Marc Lipsitch, a Harvard University epidemiologist, has calculated H1N1's death rate is less than 0.1 per cent.

Quebec's H1N1 statistics also belie the prevailing notion during the pandemic that young children - and not the elderly - were particularly vulnerable to dying of swine flu. In fact, 54 per cent of those who died from H1N1 were at least 60 years old. By comparison, two per cent of all deaths occurred in young children and infants.

Forty per cent of all hospitalizations were for young children and infants, however, compared with 17 per cent for those at least 60 years old.

Despite H1N1's relatively low mortality rate, Lipsitch also endorsed the mass vaccination campaign.

aderfel@thegazette.canwest.com

News
Stigma hampers fight against AIDS in Senegal
Peter O'Neil
Canwest News Service
665 words
7 January 2010
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
F6
English
Copyright © 2010 Edmonton Journal

Three gay Senegalese men huddle just after midnight in the corner of a restaurant, wolfing down chicken dinners after agreeing to have their photographs taken on the condition their faces are hidden from view.

All three, who like two-thirds of gay Senegalese men maintain conjugal relationships with wives or girlfriends as part of their public facade, say they live each day fearing that exposure of their homosexuality would provoke contempt, family rejection and possible violence in this poor, predominantly Muslim country on West Africa's coast.

"We face verbal and physical aggression in the streets if people know we are MSM (men who have sex with men)," says Lamine, secretary general of a 95-member group called Xam Xamle ( "teaching knowledge"), which was organized three years ago to convince men, often unsuccessfully, to come out of the shadows to attend meetings on the importance of safe sex and regular HIV testing.

"They call us perverts, they say we are worms."

Dr. Bara Ndiaye, project leader for the non-governmental organization Enda-Sante, said anti-AIDS initiatives led by government and non-government organizations are often viewed suspiciously.

"This society is very hostile toward homosexuals," said Ndiaye, whose organization works closely with MSM and sex workers on issues like safe sex, testing, treatment and nutrition. They assume it is promoting homosexuality."

In early 2008, five men were arrested, and later released, after their photos appeared in a magazine celebrating a symbolic gay marriage.

Then, last December, police burst into the private residence of an HIV outreach worker and arrested nine men, charging them with violation of the country's law against gay sex prohibiting "unnatural" acts.

They were allegedly beaten in prison and then sentenced to eight years in jail, but were released on appeal in April amid international protests from rights groups. Some fled the country as refugees due to threats from outraged citizens who said in public forums that the men should be beaten or even lynched.

Gay men, and male and female sex workers, are by far the two groups most devastated by the HIV/AIDS epidemic, which has infected 33 million people around the world, two-thirds of whom live in sub-Saharan Africa.

Roughly one in five members of each group is HIV-positive in Senegal, which otherwise has a relatively low infection rate of just under one per cent, according to government estimates.

"We are sometimes rejected by our own kids," said Ndeye, an HIV-positive female prostitute and spokeswoman for a support group called Karlene. "If the community finds out you are a sex worker, your opinion will no longer mean anything. We are not regarded as human beings."

Prostitution is legal in Senegal for women over 21, and those who register get easier access to health care, health information, general support and condoms. But an estimated 80 per cent aren't registered, in many cases because women fear the stigma of being officially labelled a sex worker.

National and international health organizations desperately need to reach out to these groups to ensure they don't spread HIV to sex partners, or in the case of mothers to their children during pregnancy or while breastfeeding, says Michel Kazatchkine, executive director of the $18.7-billion US Global Fund to Fight AIDS, Tuberculosis and Malaria.

He said social stigmatization represents the second-biggest barrier facing national and international bodies waging a multibillion-dollar battle against AIDS, a crisis that has been overtaken in the media by the global financial collapse, war, climate change and the H1N1 flu pandemic.

Colour Photo: Didier Ruef, The Global Fund / These three gay Senegalese men, who requested their faces not be shown in this picture taken in a Dakar restaurant, say stigmatization and discrimination in their predominantly Muslim West African country make it difficult to educate gay men about the importance of safe sex and regular HIV testing.

Venting; /vent-ing/ v.t. to give free play or expression to an emotion or passion
Compiled By Terry McConnell

- - I gave my wife a Slap Chop for Christmas. She gave me a slap in the chops.

- - How typical for city hall: Resur-face the Dawson Bridge, then a few months later tear it up again to do further repairs.

- - The city told us in 1984 when they closed the Dawson Bridge to one lane for four months that the repairs they were doing would be good for 50 years. Did they mean that in dog years?

- - The city should have removed the Dawson Bridge entirely and replaced the main structure with precast beams on the existing concrete supports. They would have had it all finished within a couple of months and the bridge would never need painting or rivets replaced again.

- - According to the city, our house has lost $95,000 in value since 2008 -- yet strangely, our monthly tax payment has increased by almost 10 per cent.

- - Earnings and property values go down and city taxes go up. What a wonderful system.

- - So, Canada's top CEOs make more in three hours than most Canadians make in a year. I can't imagine anyone waking up in the middle of the night thinking, "I'm worth $36.5 million."

- - What to do about the boorish behaviour of the Oilers, both on the ice and off ? I know, let's build them a new arena.

- - If the bill from that Calgary restaurant was actually justified, the restaurateurs wouldn't have knocked off $4,500 when the Oilers complained. They would have called the police.

- - The moment one of the Oilers took a swig out of a bottle of expensive brandy, their credibility went out the window.

- - The Oilers have embarrassed the city beyond belief with their cheapskate attitude in that eatery, and in Calgary of all places.

- - I hope all the people who were quick to judge the Oilers are sorry now that the full story is coming out. With the liquor commission investigating, the restaurant owner will have a choice to make. If he insists the $18,000 bill was correct, he will be admitting he overserved the team. If he says he didn't overserve them, he will have to admit he overcharged them.

- - Voting for the Wildrose Alliance is the equivalent of trading the entire Oilers team for the Maple Leafs.

- - Instead of sending observers to monitor elections in the Third World, Canada should instead send a team to the next Alberta P.C. convention.

- - Based on her track record, if Iris Evans was a meteorologist, she couldn't accurately predict yesterday's weather.

- - If our government wants to economize, install MRI machines in the airports instead of the naked scanners. That way, they can check for weapons and at the same time diagnose ailments more quickly and efficiently than Alberta Health.

- - So children under 18 will be exempt from the full-body scan. That's understandable, but aren't a lot of these suicide bombers children?

- - If you're against the idea of being scanned at the airport, there is a simple solution: stay home.

- - Why has Parliament been pro-rogued? For the amount of money these people are being paid, they should get back to work.

- - We clearly don't get the message behind the story of the boy who cried wolf. How else can you explain our collective gasps at Y2K, WMDs, H1N1 and now the "Oh no, it's going to cost 10 times more to care for twice as many Alzheimer's patients."

- - Shame on my son's ex-wife, who defied a court order and hid the children from their financially responsible and loving father over the holidays.

- - Those weren't robins in your bird bath at -16 C. They were sparrows with chapped breasts.

Real Life
Top 10 Health Stories; The Year In Review
Evra Taylor Levy And Eddy Lang
For Canwest News Service
809 words
7 January 2010
Calgary Herald
CALH
Final
E1
English
Copyright © 2010 Calgary Herald

Who would have imagined that a viral infection in a young Mexican boy would lead to a global pandemic with huge public health implications? But there was more to learn about health in 2009 than the impact of the H1N1 virus.

This edition of HealthWatch recaps the top 10 stories from a year filled with important scientific developments.

- Preventing falls in the elderly. Falls in the elderly can result in a downward spiral in health, so prevention is key. Research is showing that specific exercises designed to improve reflexes and stability have important benefits when it comes to preventing falls. General exercise helps, as well, but not as much as a program that targets equilibrium.

- Exercise benefits cancer survivors. Fighting cancer takes a lot out of a person and while survivors can claim victory, their ability to function does not always return to normal. Fortunately, recovery is aided considerably by a regular exercise program that restores energy and allows the disrupted parts of a cancer survivor's life to fall back into place.

- Good news for dieters. Mediterranean and low-carbohydrate diets are viable alternatives to -- and may even be superior to -- the low-fat, calorie-restricted diet that has traditionally been advised for people needing to shed weight and who may have risk factors for heart attack and stroke. A study showed only small differences between the three approaches, allowing dieters to individualize their choice based on their health profile and personal preferences, increasing their chance of success.

- Not all cancer screening is good for you. Evidence continues to mount that, sometimes, getting screened for cancer can cause more harm than good. Tests lead to more tests and treatments, some of which can be unpleasant and harmful, resulting in treating some slow-growing cancers that would never have threatened one's health. In some cases, the decision to get screened becomes an informed personal choice rather than a strong health recommendation.

- Eating less may make you smarter. With the "silver tsunami" approaching, the need to find ways to head off declines in mental functioning becomes a top priority. In addition to the convincing benefit of exercise, new research this year also supported the notion that reducing calories can improve performance on skill-testing questions that are a struggle for the aging brain. Is it time to drastically reduce your caloric intake? Not just yet, but the benefits for your heart and waistline may be other reasons to consider it.

- Post-traumatic stress disorder: we need to talk. As many as one in 10 may develop PTSD in their lifetime, yet the vast majority of cases are undiagnosed and untreated. This leaves PTSD victims unable to fully function, but there is hope. Certain types of talk therapy seem to be the most effective, along with group therapy and peer group support. When it comes to getting help for PTSD, earlier is better.

- Should tanning beds be put to rest? Scientists from the World Health Organization recently declared tanning beds carcinogenic for humans. The most ominous warning related to those under age 30, raising their risk of melanoma, a common and deadly type of skin cancer, by 75 per cent. The bottom line? Tanning beds should be discouraged for those most at risk, especially pale-skinned youths.

- Not all antidepressants are created equal. Most research indicates that depression results from an imbalance in brain chemicals that affect mood and motivation. But everyone's biology is different and no two people will respond alike to treatment. An individualized approach always trumps the best research evidence, and a good dose of patience may be just what the doctor ordered when trying to find the medication that works best for you.

- Growth and the ADHD medication Ritalin. Placing a child on ADHD medication can be an agonizing decision, especially in light of the concern that these stimulant drugs may inhibit children's growth and weight gain. Researchers found that while there was a measurable impact on growth, the effect was small and lessened over time. There are no definitive answers, yet, so decisions must be made with imperfect information. ADHD medications are meant to be used when non-medical approaches are not effective, and should be used at the lowest possible dose to have their desired effect.

- New guide targets addiction. Substance abuse exacts an enormous human toll and is directly linked to more than 47,000 deaths annually, yet, health-care providers are often at a loss to detect problems and offer help. A newly published guide from the U.S. National Institutes of Health offers information and advice, examines the root causes, and outlines 13 core principles of treatment, addressing both the psychological and physical issues.

lifeMain
H1N1 infection a bigger risk to fliers in economy class; A study has found that airline passengers in economy class are much more likely to contract H1N1 from an infected passenger than those in first class
Dave McGinn
603 words
7 January 2010
The Globe and Mail (Breaking News)
GMBN
English
2010 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

There’s one more reason to hate flying economy class.

In a new study of how the H1N1 flu virus spreads through commercial air travel, researchers have found that not only can the number of in-flight infections be predicted based on just one fellow passenger carrying the virus, but also that people travelling in economy class are much more likely to become infected than are people flying first class.

The study, published in the current online edition of the journal BMC Medicine, was conducted by Sally Blower, director of the Center for Biomedical Modeling at the Semel Institute for Neuroscience and Human Behaviour at (too long-winded, jg)the University of California in Los Angeles, along with Bradley Wagner and Brian Coburn, postdoctoral fellows in Dr. Blower’s research group.

“Importantly, we found that the number of infections that would occur on a flight was very dependant on which cabin the infected individual was sitting in,” Dr. Blower said in a release. “We found that many infections could occur if the infected individual was travelling in economy class, but relatively few if the individual was travelling in first class.”

Two things explain the greater likelihood of catching the virus in economy class, the researchers said.

“Primarily, it’s the more crowded conditions in economy,” Dr. Coburn said. “And unfortunately, there is a very high probability – 75 per cent – that if an infected person is on board, they will be in the economy cabin.”

Using Mathematical modelling techniques enabledallowed the researchers to predict in-flight transmission rates. A five-hour flight would likely result in two to five infections, according to the study. Five to 10 people would be infected during an 11-hour flight, while seven to 17 infections would occur during a 17-hour flight.

To make these predictions, researchers used the Wells-Riley equation, which is designed used (repetitive) to predict the size of outbreaks within buildings and other enclosed environments. The equation is based on the number of individuals exposed to an infectious pathogen, the respiratory rate of the infected person, the length of exposure to the infectious droplets and the concentration of infectious viral particles over time.

While previous research has shown that smallpox, measles, tuberculosis, seasonal influenza and severe acute respiratory syndrome can be transmitted during commercial flights, this is the first study to predict the number of H1N1 flu infections, that could occur during a commercial flight, the researchers said.

“Clearly it was air travel, by transporting infectious individuals from the epicentre in Mexico to other geographical locations, that significantly affected the spread of H1N1 during the outbreak last spring,” Dr. Coburn said. “However, until our study, it hadn’t been determined how important in-flight transmission could be.”

Given the results of the study, researchers said that air-travel restrictions could be one potential (redundant)means of controlling influenza pandemics.

“Our results imply that one individual travelling by plane, by infecting other travellers on the same flight, could cause multiple simultaneous outbreaks in different geographical locations rather than causing only one outbreak,” Dr. Wagner said. “For that reason, quarantining passengers who travel in economy class on long-haul flights could potentially be an important control strategy this winter.”

But for those people with extra money to spend, fear not.

“There is no point in quarantining passengers in first class,” Dr. Wagner said.

OTTAWA -- Prime Minister Stephen Harper says virtually all Canadian soldiers will leave Afghanistan by the end of 2011, making some of his most definitive statements yet on his vision of Canada's future role there in an interview with Canwest News Service.

Parliament has already decided that the combat mission involving about 2,500 troops in southern Afghanistan centred around Kandahar will end in 2011. The Department of National Defence has already started preparing detailed plans to move troops and material home.

NAIROBI, Kenya -- A lone elephant charged out of the brush as an American family was hiking near Mount Kenya and trampled to death a mother and the one-year-old daughter she held in her arms, officials said Wednesday.

Four adults and the baby were walking with an unarmed guide just outside Mount Kenya National Park on Monday morning when the elephant charged, said Kenya Wildlife Service official Michael Kipkeu.

The family was hiking on a nature trail about two kilometres from the Castle Forest Lodge, where the family was vacationing, said the owner, Melia van Laar. TORONTO -- Canada, which has a large surplus of H1N1 vaccine on its hands, announced Wednesday it will lend some to Mexico.

Canada's NAFTA partner has ordered vaccine from manufacturers Sanofi Pasteur and GlaxoSmithKline, but won't take possession of the bulk of its supply until the end of this month.

So this week GSK Canada will ship five million doses of adjuvanted vaccine from Canada's stockpile to Mexico. Later, GSK Canada will put five million doses of adjuvanted vaccine from Mexico's order into the reserve the company is holding for Canada.

The move was announced in a press release, which stipulated that the vaccine will be replaced by the end of March. The loan was triggered by a request from Mexico, the Public Health Agency of Canada said.

Barbara Yaffe
Editorial/Opinion
No shortage of problems
Barbara Yaffe
Vancouver Sun
618 words
7 January 2010
Windsor Star
WINSTR
Final
A6
English
Copyright © 2010 Windsor Star

How will we remember the political year that was 2009? During the past dozen months Canada's two main parties experienced see-saw swings in support, while offering few solutions to the country's most daunting policy dilemmas.

As at the beginning of 2009, Canadians were left wondering at the end of the year how Ottawa will go about ridding itself of a bulging federal deficit that has ensconced itself on the Canadian body politic.

Wondering, too, how the country will reconcile Alberta oilsands expansion with the imperatives of reducing greenhouse gas emissions.

And what Ottawa will do as a NATO member to address the mayhem and murder in Afghanistan post-2011 as a deadline for the Canadian Forces' military withdrawal fast approaches. While these hot potatoes simmered, the political parties were on full boil with their own problems.

Liberals, last January, were raring to go with their new leader, Michael Ignatieff. Polls had them tied with the governing Conservatives by the time the tulips bloomed in Ottawa. Then -- whoosh -- all the petals fell off in the cool autumn air as Liberal support cratered.

The Conservatives, meanwhile, who were in the dumpster in early January following Stephen Harper's desperate appeal for a parliamentary recess last Christmas, spent 2009 climbing steadily in the polls.

The party's support reached majority territory by October when the PM dared to play the piano for voters at a National Arts Centre gala and, in doing so, looked -- ye gods -- human.

It was a year when New Democrats had some success, finding gut-punching issues to champion -- the harmonized sales tax adoption in B.C. and Ontario, and the pensions plight. But the party remains no closer to convincing voters it's a realistic governing alternative at the federal level.

The election everyone widely anticipated throughout the year never did materialize. But at least the notion has been put to bed for awhile, given the Grits' faltering condition. Meanwhile, a Nanos poll announced in December that Canadians are in a better mood than a year ago, with 64 per cent thinking Canada is moving in the right direction, up from 53 per cent in 2008.

Some fun stuff happened in 2009 as well. Gov. Gen. Michaelle Jean got herself a new Afro hairdo. Defence Minister Peter MacKay finally got himself a would-be wife in Jana Juginovic. And U.S. President Barack Obama paid a visit to Ottawa, for a few hours.

Prince Charles and Camilla, the Duchess of Cornwall, also dropped in and stayed longer, but, in terms of excitement and crowds, there was no contest between the two visits.

Canada got nine new Conservative senators in 2009, to join 18 others appointed last December. The tally currently is 46 Conservative senators and 51 Liberals. Any guesses about which party will lay claim to the six additional seats in the Red Chamber that will need filling by the end of 2010?

This was a year when Canadians finally got the truth, or as near as they'll ever get, in two separate government inquiries: the Braidwood Inquiry into the 2007 Vancouver International Airport death of Robert Dziekanski after he was repeatedly Tasered by four Mounties ,and the Oliphant Inquiry into business dealings between Brian Mulroney and Karlheinz Schreiber. Testimony at both events was nauseating.

It was also a year for hand-wringing about climate change and how Canada's petro-powered economy is going to adapt to a new world order.

A year, too, for hand-washing as people got the willies about the H1N1 virus even if they didn't get the flu itself.

byaffe@vancouversun.com

The Economist
Editorial / Op-Ed
The dirty lowdown on our obsession with filth; The war on dirt might be hurting our children
The Economist
910 words
6 January 2010
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A13
English
Copyright © 2010 Montreal Gazette

Grub, filth, grime, muck, gunk, slag, grit, grunge, smut, dross, dust, sludge. Insulted, hounded, and despised, dirt these days has nowhere to hide.

A constant shower of advertising and health warnings orders you to scrub, cleanse or purify every corner of the body, office or home. Bugs lurk at every turn. Skin, as much as household surfaces, must be scoured, sterilized and sprayed. The latest scare is the computer keyboard: Supposedly it contains nearly 70 times more microbes than the average toilet seat.

The effort to remove dirt, and imbue bodies and bathrooms with the scent of tangerine, mint, or almond instead, is big business.

Each year, the world spends $24 billion on soap bars or liquid gels and wash, according to Euromonitor International, a research firm. Another $106 billion goes on cleaning laundry, dishes, bathrooms, and other surfaces, including the baths and showers the bodies themselves get scrubbed in.

Shock studies periodically expose and deplore sloppy habits. Fully 76 per cent of kitchen-sink cloths are infested with germs. One in three American men does not wash his hands after using a public lavatory. Worries about the spread of swine flu are currently doing wonders for the market in pocket-sized antimicrobial hand wash.

There is nothing fixed, however, about Western fascination with dirt - or terror of it. As recently as 1965 only half of British women wore an underarm deodorant. Back in 1940 just over half of American households had a proper bathroom. In 1951 nearly two-fifths of English households lacked a bath - and not only for reasons of postwar poverty.

The English language demonizes dirt. It is packed with phrases such as "to do the dirty," "to dish the dirt" or "dirty money" or "dirty word." In England, police officers are denounced as "the filth." Those of humble origins were born "dirt poor," and the wealthy are often "filthy rich."

The act of washing, whether of a corpse in Jewish culture or of the hands and feet of the happy couple in a Hindu wedding ceremony, carries ritual symbolism in many cultures and faiths. Many religions link ablution to absolution.

Just as public-health reform in 19th-century industrial England and North America concentrated on the need for basic hygiene, so do today's efforts at curbing disease in the poorest parts of the world. Pumping clean water into villages and slums, and encouraging hand washing, are considered two of the most efficient means of reducing infant mortality.

Each year, according to the United Nations Children's Fund, 3.5 million children die from diarrhea or related diseases that could be prevented by simple hygiene.

Yet, in the West, standards of cleanliness seem to have moved beyond science into the realm of fashion and fad. Certainly, the array of sprays, gels and lotions, to be applied in vast shrine-like bathtubs or under today's power showers, would have appalled much of Europe for most of the past millennium.

These days, marketing has turned to the need to get rid not just of dirt but of stress, too. Bathrooms have become temples of relaxation. Mosaic tiles and sunken baths nod to Greek and Roman inspiration; organic bath essences promise spa-like regeneration.

North Americans still buy more bars of soap than Europeans each year. But, if liquid washes are included, Europeans today only just trail us in spending per person.

Today, the message is not just to disinfect surfaces but to avoid contact with them at all. There are automatic-flushing toilets, foot-operated taps and strange-looking devices that enable you to open door handles by applying pressure with the forearm.

"With all the advances in restroom hygiene, it's hard to grasp why the door handle has been so universally ignored," declares a maker of such products. Japanese high-tech lavatories have a built-in temperature-controlled nozzle that squirts water to wash your bottom, then dries it with warm air, delivering a "hands-free clean."

Has the persecution of dirt, however, gone too far? Some immunologists believe that children now growing up in hyperclean, sterile environments are failing to develop immune systems properly because of inadequate exposure to bacteria.

This idea, the hygiene hypothesis, is a possible explanation for growing rich-country incidences of eczema and other allergic diseases, which are rare in poorer lands.

First-time parents, writes Mary Ruebush, an American immunologist and author of Why Dirt is Good, frantically try to keep their babies away from dirt. "When that pacifier falls on the floor, the parents cannot throw themselves on it quickly enough to wash it off, soak it in bleach, run it through the dishwasher, the microwave, you name it."

The trouble is, she adds, such a baby will not have the contact with germs that are needed to build up a strong immune system. Children rather should be encouraged to play in the dirt.

The idea is gaining some ground. Unilever, for instance, has an advertising campaign for OMO, its laundry-cleaning brand, titled Dirt is good! The firm's market research showed that mothers were frustrated by the message that dirty clothes are bad. "We wanted to reposition dirt as an expression of freedom," explains Weed. Let children get dirty, goes the message, safe in the knowledge that OMO will clean up afterward.

Seasonal flu shots available soon
The Hamilton Spectator
503 words
6 January 2010
The Hamilton Spectator
HMSP
Final
A04
English
Copyright (c) 2010 The Hamilton Spectator.

Seasonal flu clinics are coming to Hamilton next Monday.

The city's public health department has posted its clinic schedule on its website at hamilton.ca/influenza.

The clinics are a chance to get a regular flu shot, but also to get an H1N1 vaccination for those that still haven't received one.

There are no known cases yet of seasonal influenza in Hamilton or Ontario, said Dr. Elizabeth Richardson, Hamilton's medical officer of health -- something that suggests H1N1 may be becoming the new seasonal strain of flu.

But it can't be known until later whether seasonal flu has been fully replaced by H1N1.

"You can only ever determine in retrospect," said Richardson, adding the H1N1 pandemic in Hamilton has definitely tapered off.

She said it's best for people to get both shots so that they are prepared for the presence of both types.

Anyone over six months of age who lives, works or attends school in Ontario is eligible. Please bring Ontario health cards to speed the process.

Clinics are at 10 locations and run from Jan. 11 to 22.

Canada to donate excess supply of H1N1 shots?
The Canadian Press
149 words
6 January 2010
The Toronto Star

ONT

English
Copyright (c) 2010 The Toronto Star

Canada will announce plans for some of its excess supply of the H1N1 vaccine Wednesday, government sources said.

The move comes as the World Health Organization prepares to make the first deliveries of donated vaccine to developing countries this week. Though a number of developed countries have donated vaccine to the WHO for redistribution to poorer nations, so far Canada has made no such offer.

Government sources told The Canadian Press on Tuesday that an announcement would be made about a portion of the oversupply.

The lack of generosity has puzzled many who are familiar with the country's long-standing support for the WHO.

Canada ordered 50.4 million doses of vaccine in August, when it was assumed people would need two doses of vaccine apiece. Studies later showed one dose was adequate.

Document TOR0000020100106e6160002m


Bronwyn Eyre
News
Information best route to understanding
Bronwyn Eyre
Special to The StarPhoenix
753 words
6 January 2010
Saskatoon Star Phoenix
SSP
Final
A2
English
Copyright © 2010 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

Call it the clash of the weather forecasters.

David Phillips, senior climatologist for Environment Canada, was all over the news last week talking about the terrible year we experienced weather-wise in 2009. Except for B.C. and the North, most parts of Canada were below average -- in Saskatchewan, for example, for nine full months.

Nevertheless, CBC's senior meteorologist, Claire Martin, begged to differ. Standing in front of a chart which read "Hot" in huge red letters, she implied Canadians shouldn't trust their own senses. She wasn't buying the "bummer summer" theory.

And she had a four-point chart to prove it: "2009 was 0.5 C warmer than average. It was the 13th warmest year in a row. The North had its warmest summer ever. And 2000 to 2009 was the warmest decade in Canada and globally-speaking."

How did Martin arrive at her figures? Was it by adding up all of the temperatures in Canada, dividing them by 350, standing on one foot and pulling numbers out of a hat?

And how can she, as a professional meteorologist, ignore the recent "Climate-gate" figures, which indicate that in 2005 -- halfway through the decade -- global temperatures had been "cooling" for seven years?

Martin didn't say. It was basically a matter of, trust me, I'm the expert.

Which is why many of us have grown cynical about what experts say.

It's like the reports that claim a certain percentage of Canadians believe X, based on this survey or that poll. But why can't we be told, even in the course of a short newscast, how the questions were worded, who sponsored the findings and whether other findings indicate something different?

More information would enable us to better assess what we're presented with. And, as a result, we'd be better, more informed citizens.

If confusion were the only byproduct of unsubstantiated information, that would be one thing. But the negative effects of presenting facts and figures in isolation can be far-reaching.

In his book, Denialism: How Irrational Thinking Hinders Scientific Progress, Harms the Planet, and Threatens Our Lives, for example, the New Yorker's Michael Specter documents some of the dangers of putting scientific and "expert" information too simplistically.

"We don't trust authority as we used to, and there are some good reasons for that," he said in a recent TV interview. Because many people now view science as a "political constituency" and distrust scientific claims, "they go on the Internet where they can find support for anything."

And because many experts don't effectively make their case -- and often seem unresponsive to public concerns -- the now-debunked theory, for example, that vaccination leads to autism gained wide credence and caused many people not to have their children inoculated.

The H1N1 semi-debacle is another case in point. Specter said he was amazed that 40 per cent of Americans said from the outset they didn't intend to get vaccinated. (Just over one-third of Canadians eventually did).

But because of conflicting reports -- and little contextualization or debate -- people were unsure what to do. There was little discussion about how, or why, Poland, for example, didn't vaccinate for H1N1; or about what death and illness rates were in Australia, which went through the flu season just before we did.

Specter also discusses the often heated and ideologically blinkered debate over organic food. While we in the West can afford the luxury of organic, locally grown food, he says, most in developing countries aren't so lucky.

One-sided reports from the West don't help. If cassava, for example, could be genetically modified to contain proteins and vitamins, this now nutrient-deficient staple could -- for starters -- prevent widespread blindness. But fuelled by the "fundamentalism of fear," many African leaders "prefer to let their citizens starve to death rather than import genetically modified grains."

As Specter says, "We should talk about these things."

What harm would more widespread discussion of such issues do? And while we're at it, whatever happened to proving your point?

As my favourite detective, Sherlock Holmes, says, "It is a capital mistake to theorize before one has data. Insensibly, one begins to twist facts to suit theories, instead of theories to suit facts."

Tell that to the "it's never been hotter" crowd.

bronwyn.eyre@sasktel.net

About 250 pharmacies in Alberta, including 20 in Calgary, are able to immunize everyone except children under 10 years, which meant a separate trip to the Avenida clinic to have seven-year-old Graham vaccinated. "It hurt," he noted.

Dr. Judy MacDonald, deputy medical officer of health, said the clinics are scheduled to operate daily through Saturday at Avenida Village, Brentwood Village Mall and EMS Whitehorn, where the H1N1 vaccine is available to everyone over the age of six months.

"We will at the end of this week reassess what the demand is and determine at that point whether we will continue to have those mass clinics or look at continuing to provide vaccine through our community health centre clinics," said MacDonald.

She said there is continual monitoring for flu outbreaks in schools, extended care facilities and physicians' offices.

"It's been fairly quiet, but just a reminder: people have been together for the holidays and people have travelled extensively. There's more (influenza) activity in other parts of the world that can be brought back. It's still early in influenza season, and I anticipate we're going to see more activity.

"Your best protection is to get vaccinated."

Meanwhile, seasonal influenza appears to have subsided, said MacDonald.

"Again, that can turn around in a day. That is why we also have seasonal influenza vaccine available at our clinics."

Approximately 500,000 doses of H1N1 vaccine were given to Calgarians before the mass clinics closed last month.

According to Alberta Health Services, as of Dec. 30, H1N1 has been linked to the deaths of 69 people in Alberta, the most recent figure available.

News
Tourists told to stay alert; Quebecer dies; But killing isn't expected to reduce travel
RENE BRUEMMER; Jason Magder
The Gazette

Final
A1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2010 Montreal Gazette

The killing of a Quebec tourist in his Dominican Republic hotel room over the holidays was a tragic event but will do little to alter the vacation plans of Canadian sun-seekers, travel officials said yesterday.

But while the violent death of a Canadian abroad is rare, travellers are advised not to let the atmosphere of an island paradise lull them into a false sense of security.

Canadians should take the same precautions while travelling as they would anywhere, advised Canadian Foreign Affairs spokesperson André Lemay.

Trips to Mexico, Canada's top international travel destination after the United States, were up 10 per cent over 2007 to 1.1 million visits in 2008.

The rise came despite the highly publicized murder of an Ontario couple at a Mexican resort in 2006 and the deaths in Mexico of two young Canadians in 2007, which authorities attributed to a car accident and a fall from a balcony, but the families said were slayings.

Canada's Department of Foreign Affairs confirmed yesterday that a Canadian was killed in the Dominican Republic, but would not release details for "privacy reasons."

Media reports identified the victim as Jean Gagné, 41, of Quebec City.

Gagné was killed at a private villa in Sosua on the north coast, near Puerto Plata.

In response to suggestions from some online comments saying travel to developing countries is dangerous and should be avoided, Lemay noted that the murder of Canadian tourists, who took 6 million international vacations in 2008, is a rarity.

"There are millions of Canadians travelling to all four corners of the world," Lemay said.

"It is very rare, Canadians are very seldom targeted. It's just something that happens."

The Canadian Foreign Affairs Department says Canadian travellers should:

- Inform themselves of the country's security situation beforehand on the Foreign Affairs website, which lists everything from violent crime to political upheaval to flooding. For the Dominican Republic, the website recommends that travellers "exercise a high degree of caution," then notes that while violence has affected "a few foreigners," such petty crimes as pickpocketing are common.

- Inform themselves about which neighbourhoods are safe to walk in and those that should be avoided, especially after dark.

- Not flash money around or show signs of affluence.

Basically, use common sense, Lemay said.

Yesterday, a Montreal travel agent said she didn't expect the news of a Quebecer being killed in the Dominican Republic would reduce the number of Quebecers booking trips there, much like the Ontario couple's deaths in Mexico failed to put a dent in travel to that country.

"We didn't see much of a change in bookings to Mexico after that," said Nancy Parsa, a travel agent at the Uniglobe agency in downtown Montreal. "But after H1N1, then we saw a huge drop."

Le Journal de Montréal reported that Gagné and a friend went on a last-minute trip to the 12-room hotel, surrounded by a high fence and protected by a security guard. On the night of Dec. 30, two men broke into Gagné's friend's room at 2 a.m., tied him up, stole his credit cards, cash and cellphone, and pistol-whipped him. They then went to Gagné's room, and the friend said he heard two gunshots.

The friend, who requested anonymity, told reporters the security guard has been arrested for giving information to the robbers on the whereabouts of the two Quebecers. One other man has been arrested.

Canadians took 2.2 million trips to the Caribbean and Bermuda in 2008, according to Statistics Canada. Half a million of those vacations were to the Dominican Republic.

There is always a bit of upheaval after a violent event in a country abroad, said Donavon Gaudette, communications manager for the Association of Canadian Travel Agencies.

"Then people will soon chalk it up to wrong place, wrong time," he said.

"Most of the time, we find Canadians are a cautious bunch - and a trusting bunch, too."

To check on the status of a foreign country, visit www.international.gc.ca[http://www.international.gc.ca.login.ezproxy.library.ualberta.ca]

Cityplus
U of A adopts honour system for sick students; Doctor's note will no longer be required to defer exam or assignment
Elise Stolte
Edmonton Journal
725 words
5 January 2010
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
B1
English
Copyright © 2010 Edmonton Journal

The University of Alberta has permanently done away with doctor-signed sick notes, asking students to self-declare instead when they are too ill to complete assignments or write exams.

As for students wanting to take an 'flu vacation,' or those who 'forgot' to study and need a convenient excuse to postpone the inevitable: "we trust the students," said university registrar Gerry Kendal. "We saw that in high school. We're just not expecting that's going to happen to any significant degree whatsoever (now)."

The university first suspended doctor's note requirements for flu-like symptoms last October, but expanded the policy to include all illnesses and made it permanent at a recent Academic Standards Committee meeting.

The policy takes effect immediately.

Kendal said as far as he knows, the U of A is the first major Canadian university to institute a no-sick-note-necessary policy for all illnesses. Several other registrars have contacted him already, asking to be kept informed about how it goes.

Students will be required to submit a form to their faculty declaring that they were sick, and in some faculties, that declaration may even be witnessed by a commissioner of oaths.

The form will be kept in the students' file.

"Deferring an exam, deferring an assignment rarely pays off anyway," said Kendal. "We have excellent students here and they are not the type of students who would take a shortsighted view."

On the first day back at school Monday, several students disagreed.

"Buying your time is everything. It's very tempting," said Mark Bugiak, a fourth year accounting major. "I definitely think (the policy) will be taken advantage of. People aren't any different in high school than in university. Well, not very different."

But at least those who keep using the same excuse will raise suspicions because their records are on file, said education student Samantha Semchuk.

Matthew Glass, in his second year of political science, said if students are sick enough to miss an exam, they should be going to see a doctor anyway and a sick note helps instructors make a more objective decision about rescheduling.

"You should be at least required to prove it," he said. "Otherwise what about students who worked hard and put in the time?"

"It's unfair," said education student Anna Christensen. "I can see a lot of people taking advantage of that," she said. When she was sick with the flu last semester, she went to the doctor anyway, even though she felt guilty leaving her house to come on campus.

Staff at the University Health Centre have voiced their support for this policy change for years, said Kevin Friese, administrator. They used to have lineups of sick students out the door and down the hall at exam time.

"Ethically speaking, it just doesn't make sense," he said. "You should be home. You should be resting, not dragging yourself out of bed to try to get a sick note."

The requirement for doctor's notes puts unnecessary demands on the health system, he said. And during the H1N1 wave last semester, when the number of students donning masks in the waiting room with flu symptoms rose 54 per cent over the year before, it was a relief to forget about the paperwork. "That made a huge difference."

Meanwhile, officials at NAIT said they require sick notes for anything other than the flu and will continue to. They had no major problems with attendance during the flu outbreaks during the fall semester.

Grant MacEwan University has an online system for students to self-declare when they have the flu and more than 800 students did so in the fall, said spokesman David Beharry.

Administrators there have not considered a permanent or expanded policy for waiving all doctor's notes, but "it's something our senior academic leadership will be looking at."

H1N1 vaccination clinics reopened to the general public in Edmonton on Monday.

Rob Breakenridge
The Editorial Page
2010 may be worse than 2009 for premier
Rob Breakenridge
For The Calgary Herald
690 words
5 January 2010
Calgary Herald
CALH
Final
A10
English
Copyright © 2010 Calgary Herald

Among those happiest to tear down old calendars, I suspect, were members of the Alberta government.

By any political standards, it was a grim year for Alberta's Tories. By their own political standards, it was nothing short of a disaster.

Having finally reached the end of a torturous 12 months, does the government now face the horror having to relive it all?

Not that Premier Ed Stelmach is likely to find himself in a Groundhog Day-like scenario where he awakes to find it is Jan. 1, 2009 all over again.

Rather, the likelihood that the political missteps, intransigence and hubris that made 2009 such an unbearable year will be ever-present again in 2010.

Moreover, it would seem as though most of 2009's political headaches -- energy royalties, the deficit, spending cuts, health care -- will carry over into the new year. Those largely self-inflicted headaches remain as such because the Tories seem incapable of devising a coherent strategy for dealing with them. Some of these issues are new or ongoing challenges, but the question of energy royalties was one that the government had ostensibly dealt with.

Now, as 2010 begins, we find ourselves awaiting (yet another) set of changes to the royalty structure.

Of course, the raging populist battle cry of "our fair share" has been replaced by the cold reality of the damage done by the government's first round of changes. As noted in 2009's Global Petroleum Survey from the Fraser Institute, Alberta had become the least attractive Canadian province for oil and gas investment. The one firestorm that might not carry over into 2010 is the H1N1 flu situation, as the abating of the outbreak has led to an abating of sorts of the backlash against the handling of the vaccination campaign.

However, just as people were perhaps ready to put that controversy behind them, an apparent hypersensitivity to criticism on the government's part has helped thrust the matter back into the spotlight.

Following the vaccination fiasco, the opposition Liberals called for the resignation of Health Minister Ron Liepert. The Liberals subsequently laid out their case at a website they set up: liepertmustgo.ca.

Last week, however, the Liberals were informed the website had to go. None other that the Speaker of the legislature himself, Ken Kowalski, has determined that the website is an inappropriate use of public funds since it "singles out" one MLA. Of course, the Liberals could simply use party funds to pay for the site, but it's an absurd demand from the Speaker's office -- one that also sets a disturbing precedent. Should opposition parties refrain from criticizing individual ministers -- or the premier himself -- and simply offer vague critiques of the government?

The order also reveals a tendency on the government's part to attack the messenger -- much like the premier's comments at November's Tory convention where he blamed the media for his woes.

With the same controversies and same attitudes from this government, will 2010 be any different? Other than the names on the cabinet office doors, given the expected cabinet shuffle this morning, little will change.

As the government found itself plunging in public support -- to the point it trailed the Wildrose Alliance by 15 points, and the premier's personal approval rating hit 14 per cent -- it showed little indication that a change in course was in the offing.

That much must have seemed obvious to now-former Tory MLAs Rob Anderson and Heather Forsyth. Their defections to the Wilrose Alliance must now confirm for disgruntled Tories that change is to be found elsewhere.

Having now dug themselves a deep political hole and having entrenched a deep desire for change in Albertans of all political stripes, it's hard to see how 2010 is going to be much merrier for the Alberta government than was 2009. If all we get is more of the same, it could actually be worse.

The Rob Breakenridge Show airs weeknights on AM770. rob. breakenridge@corusent.com

Censorship must go
Calgary Herald

Ordering the Alberta Liberals to remove a controversial website which seeks anecdotal evidence of health-care chaos is a blow to democracy and an abuse of power.

Speaker of the legislative assembly, Ken Kowalski, has been around long enough to know better than to muzzle the opposition, but did so anyway by deeming the Lipertmustgo.calink on the Alberta Liberals web page to be an inappropriate use of caucus funds. The party says it will now appeal to the Members' Services Committee, as it should.

It's the right and duty of opposition to evaluate the government's handling of significant public policies such as health care. Assessing Health Minister Ron Liepert's record is also fair game in politics.

There's not one personal attack on the website or anything more controversial than the Liberals' request for first-hand stories directly from health-care professionals and patients hurt by health-care reform. Free speech is free speech, even when using online communication tools.

The minister of health is ultimately responsible for the mishandling of the H1N1 vaccination rollout, bed closures and the ludicrous policy reversed only when it became public, that would have seen toiletries and snacks taken away from mental health patients to save $70,000 a year.

What else is happening behind closed doors and where are the cracks in the system? The Liberals are rightly seeking those answers. Instead of trying to silence their critics, the Tories should open their ears to the answers, and get out of the way of what should otherwise be a healthy public debate.

Guest Column
Editorial/Opinion
Trends of the decade
Andrew Cohen
Special to The Windsor Star
769 words
5 January 2010
Windsor Star
WINSTR
Final
A6
English
Copyright © 2010 Windsor Star

And so the first decade of the 21st century fades into the folds of history, keen to classify and catalogue it, which is what history does. As they gave monikers to the "roaring" twenties and the "dirty" thirties, future chroniclers will no doubt find a catchy byword for the 2000s. We await history's shorthand.

In the meantime, what were the trends of the last decade? What defined our world and convulsed our lives?

Consider, for a start, terrorism, technology and temptation. Each was a force unto itself, drawing strength from the other. While there is nothing new about using violence to advance political ends, terrorism arrived like a thunderclap in North America on Sept. 11, 2001. That the attacks came in the United States, against the pillars of American financial and military power, assured them maximum exposure in the breathless media age.

Suddenly, the American summer -- that long season of affluence and insouciance that characterized the Clinton presidency in the 1990s -- came to an unceremonious end. At home, America had a seizure -- constricting civil liberties, creating colour-coded levels of alert, constructing the apparatus of national security. Abroad, it went to war in Afghanistan, fitfully, and Iraq, disastrously.

In Canada, we followed. We introduced new laws, too. We fought in Afghanistan but not Iraq. The threat of terrorism, in all forms, darkens our lives. Its spectre hangs over this generation as nuclear war did the last.

Technology, another great force of the decade, has helped terrorism. Sept. 11 was managed with box-cutters, yes, but terrorism is now advancing into cyberspace.

The immediate impact of technology is personal and commercial. Technology insinuates itself into our lives, changing the culture. We are tethered to each other now; the expectation is that one is available anytime and anywhere. Always. In the feverish 24-hour news cycle, we fear falling out of touch. We must talk and we must e-mail (as well as play music, watch movies and read maps).

Privacy is the first casualty. It no longer exists. A generation is growing up in public -- sharing their lives on Facebook and YouTube, texting secrets, posting racy images.

Technology has created a reign of rudeness. Today, people answer phones at formal meals, talk loudly on buses, reply to e-mail in business meetings.

In the unregulated, unfiltered Internet, bloggers traffic with impunity in rumour, innuendo and gossip. Technology has accelerated the coarsening of culture. Expletives forbidden in public conversation a decade ago appear in titles of best-selling books and on prime-time television. No one cares.

In speech, in dress, in manners, we are less formal than we were 10 years ago. So what if the twentysomething medical office receptionist calls an octogenarian patient by his or her first name? They don't know any better.

The third horseman of the decade is temptation. This is largely the rise of greed and sexual licence. Indulgence and infidelity didn't begin yesterday, but both are enjoying a jolly good run.

The new Bill Clinton is Silvio Berlusconi, not to mention the philandering David Letterman, Tiger Woods and Jon Gosselin (minus Kate). The sins of Bernie Madoff, Martha Stewart and Conrad Black were venal rather than carnal, but they, too, simply couldn't resist.

We are now awash in a depth of scandal, celebrity and sex, unthinkable 10 years ago. While the planet warms, models of vanity and insanity strut down the public catwalk. Experience is devalued. Civic, moral and religious authority fades. Dilettantes enter politics. Athletes cheat.

Kids cannot fail in primary school because everyone is a winner. Universities credential rather than educate. Amid ebbing standards, we sigh and shrug. Whatever.

Still, it isn't all bad. We are wealthier and healthier. Knowledge is spreading and travel is exploding. Both deepen international understanding. Neither the swine flu nor the recession were as bad as we thought. Americans elected their first black president, an epochal event.

In Canada, we've had a decade of constitutional peace. As we become more diverse, the threat to our unity is no longer an independent Quebec. It is managing the great migration (immigration) and the great devolution (the rise of the provinces).

The good news in 2010 is that the survival of Canada, long the uncertain country, is no longer seriously in question. We endure in this funny, feverish world -- and will.

Andrew Cohen is president of The Historica-Dominion Institute. These views are his own. E-mail: andrewzcohen@yahoo.ca. This column first appeared in the Ottawa Citizen.

Body & Health
A nail-biter's guide to breaking a bad habit; Stress, insecurity and psychological trauma all blamed for ragged tips
Tara Kruger
Calgary Herald; Canwest News Service
1333 words
4 January 2010
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
C3
English
Copyright © 2010 Edmonton Journal

When you're a chronic nail-biter, everyone's always sticking their fingers in your business. My elementary school teacher once asked me, "Did you know your nails are made of the same substance as a beetle's exoskeleton?" thinking that factoid would gross me out enough so that I'd quit. It didn't. I didn't. I still nibble, gnaw and chew at my fingernails like a rabid squirrel going after Mr. Peanut.

My mom tried everything to nip the problem in the bud early on, to no avail. I've had my hand slapped away from my mouth by my boss, co-workers, friends, boyfriend and complete strangers. I've been a biter as long as I can remember. After a lifetime of daily chewing, my poor nails have been bitten to the quick and beyond.

I don't need anyone to tell me why I should stop. Nail-biting (the medical term is onychophagia) isn't pretty; it can cause infections; it's interpreted as a sign of insecurity by others; and with H1N1 lurking on every door handle, constantly putting your fingers in your mouth is just asking for trouble. I've even developed a phobia of pointing. The last thing I want is for anybody to have a reason to look at the end of my finger. It's downright embarrassing.

"Lots of people bite their nails. It's nothing to be ashamed of," I tell myself, but sharing a body-focused repetitive behaviour with Britney Spears, Lindsay Lohan and Paris Hilton provides little comfort. Then again, even Princess Di balled her hand into a fist when it came time to display her engagement ring, lest her raggedy nails be exposed. It's estimated that 30 per cent of children and 45 per cent of teenagers bite their nails. Most simply outgrow it once they reach adulthood, but not everyone does.

With unshakable resolve and a few tricks, many kick the habit. Rubberband bracelets are flattering -- wear red and you're in style-step with Kabbalah-practising Madonna -- but you have to remember to punish yourself with a snap on the wrist when you catch yourself biting.

Nasty-flavoured nail polish can be effective, too. There is never a drink or mint close enough when that foul taste hits your tongue. It's like licking the inside of the cap of a bottle of nail-polish remover and chasing it with a shot of lye. Tasty!

Band-Aids around the fingertips invite awkward questions, as do gloves in the middle of summer, but you can't bite what you can't get at.

Fake nails seem to work. Press-ons do the trick for me, but they only last a week or so. I've never had gel tips, but I hear they are tough enough to break a tooth if you try to chow down on them. Eventually, though, the falsies must come off and you're back where you started.

I have tried all of these methods and have succeeded in quitting three times. But then my fingers make their way into my mouth again within a few months and all that hard work and will power are for naught.

Wally Muller, who owns Advance Hypnosis in Calgary, says my compulsion to chew is all in my head. Some experience in my past gave rise to an emotion that makes me want to bite, he maintains. He uses regression hypnosis to help people identify the root cause of their bad habit and overcome it. "They don't know where it came from, but they can't get rid of it," Muller says. "They know it's there, but they don't know what it is. It's called anxiety."

Regression therapy is only one type of hypnosis. Direct suggestion -- you've seen the shows where some unfortunate soul clucks around the stage with his arms flapping -- involves the hypnotist reading a script to influence behaviour while the subject is in a trance. You can buy MP3 recordings and CDs online, and do direct-suggestion hypnosis at home. Muller says this method does work, but only temporarily. "Direct suggestion in a lot of situations is a Band-Aid. It'll fix it for a while, but then it comes back because you never really got rid of the cause."

Psychologists and hypnotists get along like doctors and chiropractors, but they do agree on some things: Most psychologists also think nail-biting is stress-induced. So if anxiety is to blame for the problem, then all I have to do is rid my life of stress and presto! Nails to die for. I'd also like a unicorn for Christmas and Albertans to vote for an NDP government in the next provincial election.

So I'm contemplating getting what could be the final nail in the coffin of my bad habit: a mouthpiece. Custom-moulded clear covers that fit over either your top or bottom teeth are an option, as is a device primarily used to discourage thumb-sucking: it has sharp bits that jab any fingers that wander into your mouth. As I also have TMJ, I'm thinking a clear cover could help me overcome two of my oral obstacles.

If you do manage to quit nibbling -- I'm crossing my fingers for you -- the hard part is to keep it up. The battle may be won, but the war is never over. Once you've stopped, buy a handful of nail files and never let yourself be caught without one. Seriously. The slightest sign of a ragged edge can undo all your hard work in a matter of minutes. Invest in some cuticle cream and a few new shades of nail polish. Flawless tips are the best defence against a relapse.

---------

CHEW CAN DO IT!

- - Mavala Stop: Although it looks like a regular clear topcoat, this product has a foul taste that lasts for days, which is especially important during flu-fighting season when we're all washing our hands like Lady Macbeth on steroids. And there's a 33-day program ( "Day 1 tip: Paint the product on all fingernails except one pinky") that holds your hand through the process of transforming from a nail-nibbler into someone with tip-top fingertips. $9.99 at Shoppers Drug Mart (not available at all stores). Online at shoppersdrugmart.caor mavala usa.com.

- - Artificial nails: Acrylic, gel, solar, wrap: These options and more are available at salons all over town, with prices starting in the $40 range. Be warned: If your own nails are in really rough shape (stripped, bitten cuticles, scabbed on the side), you may be turned away and ordered to leave them alone for a few weeks before returning. Then there's the maintenance cost. Artificial nails have to be filled every three weeks or so, starting at about $25 a shot. Referrals are a good way to select a salon, and always be on the lookout for an ultraclean environment in which the reusable tools are sterilized between clients.

- - Rubber band: Associate a given behaviour with a painful consequence enough times, and presto, chango, you won't be engaging in said destructive action quite as often. (Although why this theory doesn't hold when it comes to overindulging in tequila, I don't know.) Take a rubber band and place it loosely around one of your wrists. The second your finger hits your mouth for a good chew, give the band a healthy snap. Yes, it's supposed to hurt. Keep it up for a week or two. Look in a junk drawer near you.

Airport debate, new arena some of my favourite things; Columnist McKeen looks back at the year that was and what he said about it
Scott McKeen
Edmonton Journal

4 January 2010
