Some of the mistakes I made in 2009 will definitely be repeated.

I learn from history. But I am not always a quick study.

I grew up a bit in 2009. I lived a bit less in my head. I lost weight. I reduced all my vices, by one.

Still, I procrastinated like there was no today. I spent too much time on my computer. I didn't exercise enough.

I swore at rude drivers. I was ticketed by the karma police. My feelings were too easily hurt. Other people were just too sensitive.

I wrote more than 100 columns in 2009 and was asked recently to pick my favourites.

Here's the deal. I'm neurotic. I'm never happy with my work. Yet I can get hissy -- how dare you! -- when someone else craps on it, Did I mention my neuroses? So, talking about my favourite columns of 2009 is going to be painful. And enjoyable.

I did a number of columns last year on the City Centre Airport debate. Like the city itself, I'd visited and revisited this topic far too many times over the years. Council finally made a decision. I wrote about it. Like exercise, it felt good to stop.

My columns on plans for a downtown arena went from positive to negative. Something flipped for me in 2009. I lost some of my boyhood crush on the Oilers and found my inner skeptic.

When the arena proposal is made public in 2010, city council must engage citizens in an objective cost-benefit analysis of the project, as well as the NHL team's contribution to civic pride and quality of life.

If Daryl Katz and company bring forward an arena proposal that respects the aspirations of Edmonton, not just the Oilers, then I might jump on board again. Maybe the team could win a couple of games, too. That might help.

My column in defence of hemp was one of my favourites. It again pointed out our hypocrisy in regards to marijuana and alcohol. No one wrote or called afterwards to call me immoral or stupid. Perhaps we're nearing the end of our reefer madness. Or maybe you're just all smoking something.

I wrote a column in defence of men. It ticked off my daughters. They understood the essential argument, but accused me of being shrill and insensitive. Me? Insensitive?

Man, did I learn a lesson in 2009. I stood in line on the first day for the H1N1 shot. I naively thought I was doing the right thing. First, by eliminating the possibility of being a carrier. Second, by writing a column promoting vaccination as a civic duty. Then the province ran short of the vaccine. Then the e-mails and calls started coming in, accusing me of jumping in the lifeboat ahead of the frail, the ill, the young and the elderly.

I do have a chronic health condition that puts me at risk of pneumonia with every flu. So I make sure I get a seasonal flu shot as early as possible.

But there were definitely people at greater risk who were forced to wait for the H1N1 vaccine. To them, I apologize.

I wrote a column about Kerstin's Chocolate as a substitute for sex. I ate a lot of chocolate in 2009.

I wrote about the new Halloween festival, dEdmonton. I also wrote about the festival's sexy Miss dEdmonton contest. And ate more chocolate.

I wrote a tongue-in-cheek piece against gargantuan baby strollers. Then I came to my senses and wrote a followup column in defence of stroller moms. I don't fear bikers or billionaires. But sleep-deprived, hormonal women are a personal safety concern.

I wrote about local photographer Cindy Gannon's sexy boudoir photo sessions. Yup, more chocolate.

I wrote about the civic budget, local architecture and numerous city-council shenanigans.

Perhaps my favourite column of the year was the saddest one of all. It was about the suicide of 17-year-old Alex Wedman.

His parents, Don and Betty, as well as siblings Eric and Janel, talked bravely about their pain in hope that it would lift the veil of shame and inspire other troubled young people to seek help.

Alex's suicide ended years of bullying at school. So many of you wanted the bullies punished. The Wedmans know that feeling, too, but are trying to move past it.

Finally, just before Christmas I wrote a piece about gratitude. It was one of my favourites, too. The column, in essence, was a published version of my personal gratitude list.

You e-mailed me in droves. People suffering loss and great sorrow said they were inspired to write gratitude lists of their own. In doing so, their spirits were lifted.

The gratitude list was not my idea. I'd heard about it elsewhere and merely channelled it in my column.

So my ego can't take the credit. In fact, it was forced onto the sidelines as I wrote of my good fortune in family, fellows and community.

Thinking of others instead of myself made me truly happy.

Not to worry. It won't last.

Opinion
An unhealthy year in health
Lee Prokaska
The Hamilton Spectator
725 words
4 January 2010
The Hamilton Spectator
HMSP
Final
A10
English
Copyright (c) 2010 The Hamilton Spectator.

Pandemic, scandal and shortage were among the most common health-related terms over the past year.

The H1N1 flu virus shocked us when its victims tended to be younger and healthier than the victims of most flu bugs. And it angered many when high-profile sports figures and members of hospital boards jumped the queue for shots.

The billion-dollar eHealth Ontario scandal shook us in a different way when we learned how much money was squandered during these recessionary times. Anger flared again at the way the health ministry buried the salaries of some of its highest-paid bureaucrats in the budgets of several of the province's hospitals.

As well, a breakdown at the Chalk River nuclear reactor led to a worldwide shortage of medical isotopes, critical in diagnosing cancer and heart ailments.

The potential severity of the H1N1 virus caused a spike in ER visits and multi-hour lineups at flu shot clinics across southern Ontario. In some ways, the public awareness campaign launched in advance of the clinics' opening worked almost too well. The clinics for specific high-risk groups saw a large proportion of vaccine-seekers who didn't fall into any of the groups. And the death of Evan Frustaglio, an otherwise healthy 13-year-old Mississauga hockey player, led to a greater clamour for shots.

The target was to vaccinate 70 per cent of the population, but by the time the clinics were closing earlier this month, only about 30 per cent of the province's population had been vaccinated against H1N1. More than 300 Canadians had died of H1N1 by early this month.

The eHealth scandal cost David Caplan his job as health minister and resulted in revamped leadership for the beleaguered agency mandated to create electronic medical records for Ontario residents.

A scathing auditor general's report estimated that about $1 billion had been ploughed into eHealth, with little of value to show for the substantial spending. Hundreds of millions of dollars in untendered contracts were awarded to consultants, some of whom demonstrated an outrageously cavalier attitude toward taxpayer-funded expense accounts. All this was happening during the recessionary period in which many taxpayers lost their jobs or had to tighten their spending to keep afloat; it's no wonder taxpayers were angry over the profligate and irresponsible spending.

The provincial government has wisely decided to press on with the mandate of the eHealth agency. Earlier this month, Ray Hession was nominated by Health Minister Deb Matthews to head the agency's board of directors. Three years ago, Hession warned the lack of electronic health records in Ontario was making as many as 32,000 sick people worse every year because doctors don't have access to files of patients they haven't seen before. Sounds like Matthews has found the right man for the job.

The federal government came under fire this year over the shutdown at the Atomic Energy of Canada Limited nuclear reactor at Chalk River, which produced about a third of the world's medical isotopes -- used in diagnostic nuclear imaging and disease treatment. The shutdown, resulting from a heavy water leak in May, was expected to last at least three months; at this point, the aging reactor is not expected to be back in production before spring.

The shutdown has been a substantial political liability to the Stephen Harper government, showing so clearly there is no backup plan in the event of problems at Chalk River despite earlier problems with the reactor. Canada's reputation as a supplier of medical isotopes has been seriously tarnished by the situation. Worse, patients are worried because their physicians must rely on erratic supplies of the critical isotopes.

As always, our health-care system has been under acute pressure this year, trying to make every dollar stretch as far as it can. Hospitals have been warned that's not likely to change; in fact, a funding freeze is possible in the coming year as the province faces a period of multi-year, multibillion-dollar deficits. There's no doubt 2010 will be a rocky and challenging year in the health-care sector.

Editorials are written by members of the editorial board. They represent the position of the newspaper, not necessarily the individual author.

H1N1
Editorial
Children's place in the queue
469 words
4 January 2010
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
A14
English
2010 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Children were twice as susceptible as adults to becoming ill with the H1N1 influenza, a new study published in the New England Journal of Medicine shows. If one person in a household had the disease, a child was much more likely to sponge up the germs than an adult. It's more reason for Canada to rethink its strange vaccination strategy, in which children were an afterthought.

Anyone five and older was shunted to the back of the line for the flu vaccine in most provinces. By contrast, the United States allowed young people up to the age of 24 to join the line early on.

When the Public Health Agency of Canada, after consultations with the provinces, published its list of at-risk groups, the main criterion was not who was most likely to get the disease but how likely they were to suffer severe illness, or die from it. That's a reasonable approach, especially in light of shortages of the vaccine.

But after the initial priority groups, including children under five, aboriginals, pregnant women and health-care workers, received their chance at vaccinations, other at-risk groups popped up, which in some cases were at no great risk of dying (police and firefighters, who on the initial PHAC priority list were side by side with children five and over), and in other cases were at reduced risk of contracting H1N1, such as elderly people. The elderly, though not given priority for the vaccine in the U.S., were to be given prompt treatment with Tamiflu, under the strategy adopted by the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.

By the time the vaccine reached Canadian children, it was next to useless. Many had already had the influenza, or had been exposed to it. In effect, public health authorities in much of the country (Saskatchewan and New Brunswick being notable exceptions) left children unprotected. Children were shoved so far down the list that three weeks passed from the first vaccinations and most Canadian children had not been given a chance to protect themselves. This was the very group most likely to get the disease: children have made up about 60 per cent of swine-flu cases.

No, they didn't die in great numbers. But if H1N1 had been deadlier than it turned out to be, they might have. And no one knew with any degree of certainty how severe the illness would be. Canada cited supposed epidemiologic evidence showing they were not at elevated risk of death; the U.S. cited other evidence showing that they were. Far better to err on the side of caution with children's health.

There may be other flus, other pandemics. Canada should never take such chances with its children again.

Huskies v-ball team schooled by masters
Scott Connell

HUSKIES ALUMNI 3, HUSKIES 1

A blast from the past blew threw the PAC during the weekend.

The 2004 Huskies alumni dusted off their volleyball gear and won the Brian Mallard and Associates Invitational final against the host University of Saskatchewan Huskies -- the 2009-10 edition -- 3-1 (23-25, 25-16, 25-19, 25-22) on Sunday.

The alumni team, comprised of mostly the 2004 Canadian Interuniversity Sport championship team, plus the 2009-10 Huskies redshirts, had a 2-1 record in round-robin play at the three-day tournament.

Adam Ens of the alumni was tournament MVP and was part of the last Huskies team to win the annual tournament, in 2004. He said winning the tournament was extra sweet for the players who don't play as much anymore.

"It was fun getting back together with the guys," Ens said. "We were really relaxed and played a little bit like we used to."

Ens, who was a CIS champion and CIS male athlete of the year for all university sports in 2003-04, is now a teacher and senior boys volleyball coach at Rosthern Junior College. He said the alumni get together for senior men's tournaments, but not often.

The alumni looked out of place in the warmup, clad in multi-coloured, non-matching clothes, as if they were going to the Y for a workout. But once the warmups ended, they put on the familiar Huskie white uniforms.

After they dropped a tough first set, they showed they still have what it takes.

"We're a little bit out of shape," Ens said. "We definitely finished the tournament with more legs than we started with. A little bit surprising we came back into form that quick."

Huskies coach Brian Gavlas, who coached all of the players on the alumni team, hoped the tournament would serve as more than a tune-up for the resumption of Canada West conference play.

"(The alumni's winning reputation) was another reason to bring them out, to get them together for the camaraderie and a little bit of nostalgia, but also to get them to interact with our guys," Gavlas said.

The University of Regina finished third, defeating the Guelph Gryphons 3-2 (12-25, 25-18, 20-25, 25-15, 16-14).

Regina's Colin Finch, Guelph's Andrew Revie, Huskies Cole Hintz and Geoffrey Zerr and alumni players Joel Ens and Dan Roth were named to the all-star team.

The Huskies, 5-3 in Canada West, are in Calgary this weekend for a conference series against the Dinos. Their next home game is Jan. 15 against the Winnipeg Wesmen.

HOCKEY

The Huskies women's hockey team recorded a 3-2 road victory over the Lethbridge Pronghorns on Saturday in a make-up game rescheduled from October due to H1N1 concerns.

The Huskies also played the Pronghorns on Sunday. The score was not available at press time.

Breanne George, with two power-play markers, and Shaye Christiansen were the goal scorers for the Huskies. Kendal Tremblay and Brittney Kaye, a native of Saskatoon, replied for the Pronghorns.

The Huskies improved their record to 7-3-3, three points back of Manitoba for second place. The 'Horns drop to 3-6-2.

Ron Petrie's Saskatchewan
News
Looking back on the decade past; This Week: New Decade
Ron Petrie
The Leader-Post
1117 words
4 January 2010
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A4
English
Copyright © 2010 Regina Leader Post

So I heard a funny thing come up in conversation again the other day -- not funny nyuk-nyuk, but funny in that curious way of being both an expression that's jarring to the ears and yet perfectly natural.

Twenty-ten.

For 50 years 2010 has been best known in Saskatchewan as a model of durable John Deere tractor, or, at the optometrist's office, some pretty decent eyesight.

Now it's a year.

What's also funny is how we managed to stumble through a whole decade without a common vocabulary for the passing years. At the turn of the millennium, the question was whether we'd borrow from the early 1900s and refer to the upcoming years as "aught-one," "aught-two" and so forth.

No. Not without a rocking chair, knitting needles and an ear trumpet, we wouldn't. Instead we spoke the cumbersome "two thousand and three" and "two thousand and four" as if counting off seconds before rushing the passer in a backyard game of touch football, or we abbreviated to "zero-five" and "oh-six."

Now we've returned to form. Before long -- all too soon, actually -- our recollections will be prefaced by "back in '11" or "the summer of '12," as effortlessly as '67 or '82 is shorthand for the 1900s series.

This twenty-ten deal arrived in a flick, though, didn't it?

Seems like only a few months ago, not 10 years, we were fretting about whether our idiot computer contraptions would flip from 1999 to 2000 without inadvertently blowing up the planet; now we're all sneezing into our elbows to stop a worldwide flu outbreak. From Y2K to H1N1, a decade has lapsed.

So what do we call this past decade? The tens? The aughties? And how will it be remembered by Saskatchewan?

Institutions fell by the wayside and others arose. The Saskatchewan Wheat Pool, the grain-growing co-operative whose trademark once towered on the sides of elevators above virtually every town and village, is now the international corporate entity Viterra. Likewise, imagine the curious disbelief of any listener in 1999, the explanations and translations we'd need to make sense for him of such 21st-century Saskatchewan advents as "Mosaic Stadium"; those white plastic grain storage bags fast replacing steel bins as the farm landmarks of the countryside; Corner Gas; the provincial ban on something called texting, or young waitresses in cafes baffled by a beverage order for "Vi-Co."

Anybody else remember, back in 1999, the "information superhighway," so mysterious and unexplored? What a quaint notion. Today we Google and Facebook and even Twitter each other, no shame whatsoever. We send messages halfway around the world in mere seconds without the cost of a postage stamp, and fast disappearing is the vehicle with an antenna on its front fender for radio reception, much less on its trunk lid for -- what did we call it? -- a "car phone"?

Saskatchewan entered this decade with Premier Roy Romanow, Rider quarterback Reggie Slack and an outrageous gasoline price of 70 cents a litre for regular unleaded; it emerges with Premier Brad Wall, quarterback Darian Durant and the mystery of why the cruel beggars simply don't call it a buck even at the pumps.

We partied until dawn after a Grey Cup victory in which the Roughriders played poorly against a pathetic opponent, and we gawked in stunned disbelief two years later when the football team was in top form against the league's best, led on the scoreboard until the clock ticked down to zero, and yet somehow lost the game.

Sadly, in the past decade, Saskatchewan has had to make room for names on its cenotaphs. Any hopes that the new century would bring international peace were dashed early on. Once again young men and women from the province risk their lives for others in faraway lands.

Saskatchewan celebrated its first century in 2005, still a new land, but now, and for the first time, older than any of its people. The living memory in this province of settlement and optimism, of drought and despair, and of two world wars is fast fading.

It would seem a time of change.

And it is.

During the decade's last five years, in particular, Saskatchewan has charged forward. In cities where not long ago construction cranes on the skyline were novelties, new roads are paved to reach housing developments that push the urban outskirts ever farther. Small towns, even those that once offered land for $1 to anybody willing to relocate and build, are now running out of serviced lots and are rolling out maps to pick and choose where the next houses will be. The T-shirt "Friends don't let friends move to Alberta" is still funny, but has lost much of what once made it downright hilarious, being now passe, so two-thousand and two.

It has become fashionable in certain circles to attribute all of the above, Saskatchewan's recent growth, to something called the new winning attitude. People do need their things to say, of course, but that new winning attitude by definition dismisses previous generations as ... what? content losers? Hardly. No one ever built a road with pick-axe and spade, or taught the children of a one-room classroom, in pursuit of defeat. Without the work of the past 100 years, Saskatchewan would not be in the position to seize upon what it now has.

Economics is a science desperately short on fact, but one of the discipline's few truths is that to wherever prosperity is most promising, people will move. The world's insatiable appetite for energy delivers that promise here, but so, too, does a crowded planet's need for grains, for food, and for potash, for the fertilizer to grow food. Feeding the world is what Saskatchewan has always done best, so well in fact that during times of surplus and bad politics, farmers of this province shouldered the brunt.

Two-thousand and nine, oh-nine, aught-nine, has already been recorded as the year a recession finally caught up with Saskatchewan. But that will be quickly forgotten, just as all economic markers come and go.

Twenty-ten will write itself. Year-end predictions are for chumps. None of us has a crystal ball, but all of us are allowed our hopes.

Here's hoping for the wisdom to make the most of the wealth that appears imminent, and the humility to know that we are a people, today in Saskatchewan, blessed by place and time.

Tory uprising in Riverbend challenges health-care cuts; Longtime Hancock supporter helps form citizen's group opposed to government policy
Jodie Sinnema

English
Copyright © 2010 Edmonton Journal

For many years, Frank Horvath was a loyal supporter of MLA Dave Hancock, helping the current education minister during his 2006 run for the premier's job.

Times have changed for the retired teacher.

"Loyalty was one of my weaknesses because I tend to be loyal to people who don't deserve it for far too long," said Horvath, 63.

"I was supporting him because I liked his ideas and I also felt he was closer to having a social conscience than perhaps some of the others running for leadership."

But Horvath now says Hancock hasn't been listening to his concerns or those of his neighbours about the radical changes to Alberta's health system, even after two meetings in June and last December.

Horvath and others formed the Whitemud Citizens for Public Health, a grassroots group in Hancock's southwest Edmonton riding that aims to either convince the minister and the Stelmach government to reverse changes made to the health system -- bed closures at Alberta Hospital, a near-freeze on hiring nurses, a new pharmaceutical strategy that increases drug costs for some seniors while reducing it for others -- or to replace the government altogether.

"What choice do we have?" Horvath asked. The Whitemud Citizens group expects 200 people at its inaugural community meeting Jan. 11 at Riverbend United Church. "We must act. If we don't take action, who will?"

Horvath's group isn't alone. Growing discontent is sparking movements such as Reboot Alberta, in which unhappy Red Tories and Liberals are trying to engage people in politics, and the Courage Project in Calgary, which supports people with independent voices.

Bill Moore-Kilgannon, executive director of a non-partisan but left-leaning advocacy group, Public Interest Alberta, said dissent is increasing. "Organizing at the community level is where people feel most empowered."

He said people are taking action on the health system after experiencing the 1990s cuts, when thousands of nurses were fired, hospital beds and hospitals closed.

"They want to head it off before we go down that road (again)," he said. Moore-Kilgannon believes people are particularly disgruntled with health budget cuts, postponed elective surgeries and the decision to build privately run assisted-living facilities rather than more long-term-care beds because, despite a $1.2-billion health deficit, the province is flush with money in its reserve fund.

"What I'm hearing from people is they think the decisions the government is making around the cuts, around their long-term care policy, etc. are really unnecessary and so they're trying to organize at the community level to challenge that notion," Moore-Kilgannon said.

"Politics is definitely changing in this province and the government ignores these community voices at its peril. There's a strong underground movement that's growing, that is really looking for ways to change the direction this government is going and those voices are across the political spectrum and need to be listened to and respected."

Hancock, out of the country for holidays, wasn't available for comment, but spokeswoman Sharron Theuerkauf expressed surprise Horvath and his group were dissatisfied with their meetings with Hancock, since they hadn't expressed that.

"Dave considers representing his constituents the most important role that he has and therefore welcomes both personal contact and public discussion of public policy issues," Theuerkauf wrote in an e-mail to The Journal. Last April, Hancock held a community focus meeting on health to get input on the government's pharmaceutical strategy. She said Hancock also plans to attend the Jan. 11 meeting of the Whitemud Citizens. "Meetings with constituents are very important to Dave to inform his advocacy on behalf of the constituency."

Dan McKinley, president of the Edmonton Whitemud Progressive Conservative Association, said he hasn't heard many concerns about the health file in his riding, although there were some rumblings about the way the H1N1 vaccine campaign was rolled out. He said Hancock is liked and respected.

"Dave (Hancock) has historically shown a high degree of interest in listening to people," said McKinley, who has worked on Hancock's campaigns for more than 18 years. "At our general meetings, he actively says, 'Look, I need to hear criticism. I need to hear what people are thinking out there because otherwise I don't know what's going on.' "

McKinley said while Hancock may not publicly voice dissenting views on his government -- as did MLA Guy Boutilier who was booted out of the PC caucus by Premier Ed Stelmach when he criticized the government for failing to build a long-term-care centre in Fort McMurray as promised -- McKinley believes Hancock speaks openly about his constituents' concerns during caucus meetings.

"Over the years I've given him bits of criticism I see and I don't tend to get shut down," McKinley said. "I tend to get asked more information about it and I tend to feel it makes an influence on him. That's why I support him."

McKinley said the provincial economy is in transition.

"It creates an awful lot of dissatisfaction, an awful lot of adjustment going on, an awful lot of introspection so I think as a result there's probably a lot of dissatisfaction out there in a lot of respects," he said. He supports the Whitemud residents forming a group on public health. "If they feel strongly, I would really encourage them to continue to voice their opinion. I think there's no question things could be done better and I think public dissent keeps us going."

McKinley believes Hancock will listen.

Horvath said he decided to form Whitemud Citizens for Public Health rather than join groups like Public Interest Alberta or Friends of Medicare in part because he doesn't believe the government takes such groups seriously and aligns them with left-leaning politics.

"A democracy is based on listening to the electorate," Horvath said. "When you have so much power, you should come from that position of power and be open and transparent and engage people in the process.

"Somehow, we've been disenfranchised. Either we haven't been fulfilling our responsibilities as citizens or we've been cut out in some way and it's time for us to take that back, to ensure we don't lose our democratic rights."

jsinnema@thejournal.canwest.com

"The Oilers can't finish a check nor pay one now either."

That is how Journal online reader "ABC123" responded to Saturday's front-page story describing a New Year's Eve skirmish between Edmonton Oilers players and an upscale Calgary restaurant.

The story sent hundreds of Journal readers scrambling to their keyboards to sound off on edmontonjournal.comall day, and responses were still pouring in at press time late Saturday.

The story, from the Calgary Herald, detailed a dispute that erupted after Oilers players, their wives and girlfriends -- 45 people in all -- wined and dined and then whined over the bill at Osteria de Medici, an Italian restaurant in Calgary, after the Oilers lost 2-1 to the Calgary Flames on Thursday night.

Restaurant owner Maurizio Terrigno complained to the media that the Oilers refused to pay their bill, which totalled nearly $18,000. Terrigno said the players were particularly upset with the $8,000 liquor tab, which included shooters, wine, tequila, rum, Crown Royal rye, Grey Goose vodka, Coors Light beer and two $500 magnums of Dom Perignon champagne.

The players "went ballistic" when they received the bill, Terrigno said. He said they demanded a discount on the alcohol portion because they expected to be charged for bottles of liquor rather than each individual drink.

Oilers spokesman Allan Watt later said the matter was resolved after the bill was reduced by $6,000.

The players paid the adjusted bill and left a tip of about $1,800, and have the receipt to prove it, Watt said.

Books
Foer's manifesto; Let's face the chilling truth, author says in exposé on eating animals
Susan Salter Reynolds, with files from Nicholas Pashley, Canwest News Service
The Los Angeles Times; with files from Canwest News Service
1621 words
3 January 2010
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
B1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2010 Ottawa Citizen

Eating Animals

By Jonathan Safran Foer

Little, Brown, $31.99

- - -

It's time, argues Jonathan Safran Foer, to stop lying to ourselves.

With all the studies on animal agriculture, pollution, toxic chemicals in factory-farmed animals and exposés of the appalling cruelty to animals in that industry, he writes in Eating Animals, "We can't plead ignorance, only indifference. Those alive today are the generations that came to know better. We have the burden and the opportunity of living in the moment when the critique of factory farming broke into the popular consciousness. We are the ones of whom it will be fairly asked, 'What did you do when you learned the truth about eating animals?'"

Some of our finest journalists (Michael Pollan, Eric Schlosser) and animal-rights activists (Peter Singer, Temple Grandin) -- not to mention Gandhi, Jesus, Pythagoras, Aristotle, Thomas Aquinas, John Locke and Immanuel Kant (and so many others) -- have hurled themselves against the question of eating meat and the moral issues inherent in killing animals for food.

Foer, 32, in this, his first work of nonfiction, intrepidly joins their ranks, inspired by fatherhood, the memory of his grandmother (who survived the Holocaust by scavenging her way to freedom) and something else.

This something else is what made critics of Foer's fiction, the novels Everything Is Illuminated (2002) and Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close (2005), fall over themselves to praise him. It is a kind of fearless modernity: one part "whatever," one part descendant of Holocaust survivor (we've only got this one life, if that, to get things right) and one part soaringly beautiful, annoyingly entitled liberalism.

What did you do when you learned the truth about eating animals? There's no hiding behind culture in Foer's postwar, post-9/11, square-framed modernism: "Food is not rational. Food is culture, habit, and identity. For some, that irrationality leads to a kind of resignation."

Think your way through it, Foer warns. Define the terms. Choose your priorities. You have that luxury.

You have that luxury because doctors and health advocates agree that vegetarianism is more than adequate (many say more healthful) than a meat-eating diet, especially meat tainted with toxic pesticides, hormones and other chemicals. You have that luxury because so many foods are now available. You have that luxury because there is now so much information on how to live and care for your loved ones without supporting animal agriculture and factory farming.

That said, this book, its author warns, is not a case for vegetarianism. It's a case for being informed and taking responsibility. In the process of asking questions, Foer "came face-to-face with realities that as a citizen I couldn't ignore, and as a writer I couldn't keep to myself."

After a brief, Swiftian modest proposal on our relationship to animals (including a "classic

Filipino recipe" for stewed dog), Foer launches into the various sectors of the animal agriculture industry: chickens, fish, pigs and cattle. Some sections are so overwhelmingly violent as to be unreadable.

Readers of the work of Grandin, a designer of livestock handling facilities and author of several fascinating, enduring, groundbreaking books on autism and animal rights, will have read passages like this before. For those who haven't, especially those who eat meat, the feeling is not unlike gagging, like being the poor sop in a horrible Greek fable who has just unknowingly eaten his family members.

The principal villain in Foer's book is modern industrial farming, if "farming" is really the term for it. If we don't know what factory farming is like, it's no accident. Foer wrote to big industrial farming companies asking for a tour. He wasn't turned down, simply ignored. The only way to see these facilities is to break in at night, and Foer joins a group of animal activists to do just that.

The reader learns a lot from Eating Animals. The world of large-scale chicken farming has been reduced to two breeds: layers and broilers. The lives of neither of these are pleasant, but at least the broilers endure theirs for only 39 to 42 days. (Male layer chicks get far less time, for obvious reasons.) Broilers are bred to fatten up quickly, frequently leaving them unable to walk. On the plus side, at less than a square foot of space each, they have nowhere to go. Strolling around the barnyard is a luxury unknown to these creatures.

One of the chicken "farms" Foer visited (illegally) contained 20 sheds, each 45 feet wide by 490 feet long, each holding about 33,000 birds.

Even, for example, if you thought you were right in buying free-range chickens, you can stop patting yourself on the back. "To be considered free-range, chickens raised for meat must have 'access to outdoors,' which, if you take those words literally, means nothing. Imagine a shed containing 30,000 chickens, with a small door at one end that opens to a five-by-five dirt patch -- and the door is closed all but occasionally."

Foer has a raft of statistics to dazzle us. Industrial farming in America accounts for "99.9% of chickens raised for meat, 97% of laying hens, 99% of turkeys, 95% of pigs and 78% of cattle." And don't think you're getting off by eating fish instead. The fishing industry uses the devious word "bycatch" to describe seagoing animals caught while fishermen are looking for something else, perhaps shrimp or tuna. The rest of it -- 80 to 90 per cent of shrimp-trawling catches, for example -- is merely discarded.

Most of Foer's numbers are American, so we can try to fool ourselves that things are vastly better in Canada. After all, we don't send people to be tortured, do we?

Foer does give us credit for using, at least to some extent, air-chilling systems rather than the spectacularly gross water-immersion systems to bathe chickens before packaging. Don't read this book near mealtime.

We eat much more meat than we used to (Americans, according to Foer, eat 150 times more chicken than they did 80 years ago), and we want it cheap. The only way to produce that much meat that cheaply leads to some very nasty practices. (I'll spare you the worst of it.)

Growing numbers of squeamish animal lovers have chosen to go vegetarian. Fine, you say, but vegetarianism is a personal choice.

Not so fast, says Foer, who sees modern farming as horribly corrupt and savagely cruel. He also raises the spectre of flu pandemics caused by farming -- think of "avian" flu and "swine" flu -- though he also suggests that many of the brief flus we catch are in fact food poisoning from contaminated factory-raised fowl. Apparently, the USDA regulations have been altered to dismiss feces in chicken meat as a "cosmetic blemish."

And then there's climate change: "According to the UN, the livestock sector is responsible for 18% of greenhouse gas emissions, around 40% more than the entire transport sector -- cars, trucks, planes, trains, and ships -- combined." And you probably don't want to know about the pollution from pig farming. A 1995 spill of pig waste in North Carolina represented an environmental disaster twice the scale of Exxon Valdez.

A reader is reminded of the debate over the first photographs from the Vietnam War. We don't want to look. Should we or shouldn't we?

Can one person make a difference? Certainly, he writes. Our food choices matter: "We eat as sons and daughters, as families, as communities, as generations, as nations, and increasingly as a globe. We can't stop our eating from radiating influence even if we want to." Or else he says: "Cruel and destructive food products should be illegal." These are Foer's conclusions.

You might not like being lectured on this topic, and Foer is not a philosopher or a logician -- so his arguments now and then veer into the shrill zone. For some readers, this will be the final straw, the book that finally makes you forgo the Thanksgiving turkey in favour of 10,000 other available dishes.

Oddly, the strongest parts of the book are those in which Foer recalls his grandmother, for whom hoarding food was a vestigial effect of the war.

"The worst it got was near the end," she recalls, "and I didn't know if I could make it another day. A farmer, a Russian, God bless him, he saw my condition, and he went into his house and came out with a piece of meat for me."

"He saved your life."

"I didn't eat it."

"You didn't eat it?"

"It was pork. I wouldn't eat pork."

"Why?"

"What do you mean why?"

"What, because it wasn't kosher?"

"Of course."

"But not even to save your life?"

"If nothing matters, there's nothing to save."

This is the kind of wisdom Foer hopes to pass on. It is

the kind of wisdom that, in all its humanity and clarity, deserves a place at the table with our greatest philosophers.

News
Economy beats flu as top story of the year, editors say; H1N1 in second place after claiming 400 Canadian lives
Glenn Johnson
Canwest News Service
491 words
3 January 2010
Ottawa Citizen

English
Copyright © 2010 Ottawa Citizen

An ailing economy edged out the H1N1 flu as the top news story of 2009 among Canwest editors.

A survey of senior editors at Canwest papers and Global Television stations gave the economic crisis the majority of votes at 53 per cent. The economy and recession beat out H1N1 (36 per cent) as story of the year in the survey, compiled in November by Canwest News Service.

"The biggest story of the year was the recession. It touched every aspect of our daily lives, our consumption habits, holiday plans, our leisure times and sadly, in many, many cases, the employment and well-being of our readers," wrote Gerry Nott, the Citizen's editor-in-chief.

"Many Canadians went through 2009 fearing for their jobs, if they kept them. ... We shopped less, saved more, took less flashy vacations, put off buying a car, cooked more," wrote Paul Harvey, assistant managing editor at the Calgary Herald.

Although there were signs of improvement later in the year, many Canadians lost their jobs, homes and savings. After reaching a peak of employment in late 2008, Canada's economy lost more than 400,000 jobs. Household bankruptcies skyrocketed. But the recession was milder and shorter than those of the early 1980s and '90s, ending with mild growth in the fall. The economic crisis also saw a crash in the auto sector in Canada and the U.S., with two major automakers filing for bankruptcy protection and taking billions in handouts from governments, while cutting their workforces and production lines.

"Although it is recovering, the economy was the lens through which most everything else in the news was viewed -- much as the 'war on terror' dominated the previous years," said Rob Roberts, Toronto editor of the National Post.

"Ontario shed much of its manufacturing base in 2009, suggesting some of these economic changes are permanent. And Toronto itself hit 10-per-cent unemployment, signalling its future as the country's economic powerhouse is very much at risk."

Dave Obee, editorial page editor for the Victoria Times Colonist, said the impact of the recession will be felt in reduced services and higher taxes for some time.

The H1N1 pandemic, which also dominated headlines in 2009, was the second choice among editors. It killed nearly 400 people in Canada and more than 11,000 worldwide. But a predicted explosion of deaths didn't happen, even though at times H1N1 clogged clinics, emergency rooms and hospital beds, putting a big strain on the health-care system.

On the positive side, H1N1 -- which appeared in Canada in April -- saw an unprecedented collaboration among the medical and scientific community.

While the economy and H1N1 received the most votes from editors, some also chose social networking, Afghan detainees and the death of Michael Jackson as top stories.

City
City still on pandemic alert; Public health officials remain poised for potential H1N1 resurgence
Pauline Tam
The Ottawa Citizen
701 words
3 January 2010
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
A4
English
Copyright © 2010 Ottawa Citizen

The crowds for the H1N1 vaccine have thinned, and the worst of the pandemic appears to be over. But since the number of swine-flu cases peaked in early November, the city's public-health officials have not let down their guard.

Of the 475 employees at Ottawa Public Health, 300 are still involved in pandemic response, with many reassigned to the vaccination campaign through much of this month, when the H1N1 virus is expected to re-surface.

"A lot of people say to us, 'Well, it's over, isn't it?' But for us, it's far from over," says Dr. Isra Levy, Ottawa's medical officer of health. "We haven't de-activated our emergency response structure, and I don't anticipate doing so until well into January, if not after."

For the city's point man on pandemic response, 2009 will be remembered as the year his department faced a number of stress tests.

First, the city embarked on its largest-ever vaccination campaign in an effort to beat back a new and highly infectious disease. The year also marked the first real test of a pandemic plan that had been in the works since the 2003 SARS outbreak. Then, on July 24, an intense rainstorm caused flooding in 1,200 Kanata homes, prompting 40 public-health inspectors to be dispatched to assess the health risks. The emergency took place as the spring H1N1 outbreak was receding, and plans were being drawn to cope with the virus's return in the fall.

Levy said the extra pressure from the flood was a reminder of how quickly multiple disasters could push the city's emergency-response systems to the brink.

"It was a very significant stress," said Levy. "If there is a second event happening concurrently with an event that's already consuming close to 100 per cent or more of your capacity, then how vulnerable is the community?"

To date, more than 40 per cent of Ottawa residents have been vaccinated against swine flu, with 300,000 doses given through city-run or hospital clinics. Another 119,000 have already been given by doctors and other health-care providers, or are in those providers' hands. A further 120,000 doses are sitting in storage, waiting to be used.

While demand for the vaccine is waning, in late October, when the first shipments were released to high-risk groups, the unexpected surge in demand pushed public-health workers to the limit. Even with 90 per cent of the department's employees reassigned to the vaccination effort, the clinics were still perpetually short-staffed.

On Oct. 26, the first day the pandemic vaccine was offered to Ottawa residents, the lineups were so long that within hours of opening their doors, the city's six vaccination clinics were turning people away.

That evening, during a midnight conference call with Mayor Larry O'Brien and city manager Kent Kirkpatrick, Levy pondered ways to speed the queues.

After that call, 140 city employees -- from departments such as parks and recreation, public works, information technology and human resources -- were reassigned daily, freeing up more public-health staff to perform triage and give shots.

While Levy and other planners had always counted on tapping city resources, much of their discussion had focused on where the vaccination clinics would be located, rather than how many people would be needed to manage crowds.

"In retrospect, maybe a lot of discussions were imbalanced toward physical assets rather than human ones," said Levy. "There were a few days when we were learning as we were going."

To get the vaccine into arms quickly, Levy and his deputies were forced to scale back on routine public-health programs and redirect, on any given day, about 100 of the 150 staff whose duties involved food inspections, health hazard assessments, sexual health clinics and health clinics for mothers and newborns. The tradeoffs were necessary because, like many public-health units across the country, Levy's department did not have enough staff and funds to manage its core programs and a pandemic response.

Editorial
Schools perfect for flu clinics
265 words
3 January 2010
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
A18
English
Copyright (c) 2010 The Toronto Star

Post mortem on H1N1 flu,

Editorial, Dec. 30

Now that the H1N1 frenzy has subsided and the general public has forgotten to be afraid, the focus will be (I hope!) on analyzing how things can be done better in terms of delivery of immunizations and other pandemic preparedness strategies.

One thing I have never seen mentioned and which has greatly mystified me, especially with the tremendous push to get people immunized, is the absence of any consideration to hold immunization clinics for children in schools. This would have been an ideal and efficient way to address immunizing a sizable portion of the population without creating a great deal of inconvenience.

I would bet that many children were not immunized because parents could not take time off work, had no transportation options, could not manage multiple children being removed from school and transported at the same time, and/or had no family doctor offering the immunization.

An in-school immunization clinic would allow the maximum treatment of children, could be done class-by-class, and remove the impact entirely on parents. The clinic providers would know exactly how many people they would be dealing with in advance, and parents would have to deal only with signing and returning a consent form.

Such clinics have been done before in schools: TB testing used to be routine.

Why was such a simple, efficient option never once considered, let alone implemented?

Tammy MacKenzie, Perth

Travel
Oasis of the Seas steals the show
Phil Reimer
Canwest News Service
572 words
3 January 2010
Vancouver Province
VANPRO
Final
B8
English
Copyright © 2010 Vancouver Province

It could be the end of the decade, or maybe I'm getting older, but it seemed that 2009 found a new gear and raced by more quickly than the years before.

The year started off with sales by all the cruise lines. January through March is the traditional "wave season," when all the cruise lines put their products on sale. This year, the cuts were deeper and the perks were better.

The wave season this year again will provide the industry with an indication of sales for 2010. It's only a first indication, as cruise lines are now offering sales throughout the year.

Consumers now either book early to guarantee they get a specific cabin, adjoining cabins, or to lock in at what they believe is a good air and cruise price, or they simply don't want to miss the chance to sail on a particular cruise. When the Queen Elizabeth offered cabins for its maiden voyage, it sold out in 29 minutes. Other travellers have a gambling instinct, and wait until the cruise date approaches in the hopes there will be further discounts.

Alaska had no sooner popped the champagne corks to usher in 2009 and celebrate its 50th year of statehood, when several cruise lines announced that many of their ships were not coming back to that state in 2010. Several reasons were given. The cost of doing business was too high in Alaska, and with so many ships in the market, the routes weren't nearly as profitable. Unfortunately for Vancouver, four of the ships not returning were based in our city's port. Some of those are heading to Europe in 2010, and some are relocating to Seattle, where operating costs are lower, mostly because it's cheaper to fly there.

The H1N1 vir us hurt Mexican tourism in 2009, and when the outbreak was at its worst, cruise ships would no longer sail there. On the east coast, those ships were quickly re-routed to Caribbean islands, while ships based on the west coast had more of a problem, as Mexico was the only destination for ships sailing the Mexican Riviera out of Los Angeles and San Diego. Those ships wound up sailing north and west until they were allowed back in to Mexico.

But all the attention was on Oasis of the Seas. This was to be the game-changer in cruising, and there's no doubt this floating resort is the high-water launch for the year. One report I saw indicated that of all the cruise lines stories written in 2009, 24 per cent of them mentioned Oasis of the Seas.

What to expect for the cruise industry in 2010? I'll write about that next week. Until then, best wishes for the New Year to you all.

I haven't done this for a while, so send me your questions, and I will answer the most interesting ones in a future column in a few week's time. Send them to preimer@canwest.com

preimer@canwest.com.

The year it DIDN'T HAPPEN - 2009 was fraught with predictions of disasters that failed to materialize
Gwynne Dyer
2102 words
2 January 2010
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
H11
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

The year 2009 was most notable for the three bad things that didn't happen. Firstly, the financial melt-down of late 2008 did not plunge us all into a 1930s-style Depression, although there were plenty of pundits predicting that less than a year ago.

As Stephen Schwarzman, head of the private equity company Blackstone Group, said last March: "Between 40 and 45 per cent of the world's wealth has been destroyed in little more than a year and a half." But all he was really saying was that a very specific kind of financial bubble has burst. All the land and houses are still there and so are most of the factories and jobs.

There has certainly been a deep recession in the developed countries and the current slow recovery may be a false dawn: A "double-dip" recession is still entirely possible. Moreover, the vast amounts of money spent by Western governments to save the banks has left them with a staggering burden of debt. But the worst has been avoided and in the developing countries there was scarcely even a recession.

The second predicted disaster that did not come to pass was a killer global pandemic like the 1918 strain of influenza. Something of that order is probably still lying in wait for us down the road, but H1N1 (also known as swine flu) turned out to be much less lethal than was initially feared. Considerable credit should go to those who made a vaccine available much faster than was thought possible, but basically we just got lucky.

And the third bad thing that didn't happen? The same bad thing that hasn't happened every year since 2001. There was no mass loss of life (by which I mean more than 1,000 people murdered in a single incident) due to terrorist action in any Western country.

Even a relatively large death toll like that should not be a reason for any government to go berserk and start invading foreign countries. A thousand people is a lot to lose, but it really isn't the end of the world. A thousand people die of natural and accidental causes in the United States (to pick a country not entirely at random) about every three hours. Terrorism is different, of course, but a rational and measured response is still required. You know very well it would be neither rational nor measured. So we go from year to year waiting for the terrorists to succeed again on the scale of 9/11, knowing Western countries will go crazy again if they do. But it didn't happen in 2009.

What did finally happen at the beginning of 2009 was the long-overdue departure of the Bush administration. Almost everybody outside the United States, and many people within it, were profoundly relieved by that, but it imposed a huge burden of expectation on the shoulders of his successor as president, Barack Obama.

It has been a difficult first year for Obama, who presumably expected to have both his health-care reforms and a climate change bill through Congress by now. His problems with Afghanistan, however, are largely of his own making.

Calling Afghanistan the "good war" (in contrast to Iraq) during the election campaign was a useful tactic to deflect accusations he was too peace-loving, but now he's stuck with it. He has already ordered a doubling of U.S. troop numbers in Afghanistan and he is now on a very slippery slope. This war is unwinnable and it could destroy him politically.

In the meantime, Obama does small but useful things that do not require Congressional assent, like cancelling the Bush plan to put a missile defence system into eastern Europe. Or at least that do not need Congressional assent in advance, like a new and better Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty with Russia (although that is still being negotiated). He even tried to launch a new Middle East peace initiative, although that was doomed from the start.

The Middle East, with only a tenth of the world's people, continued to generate more than its fair share of the news. The Israeli punishment attacks on the Gaza Strip that began on Dec. 27, 2008, continued through most of January, leaving more than 1,300 Palestinians dead. More than half of them, according to all estimates except the Israeli military's own, were civilians. Israeli fatalities from all causes, including friendly fire, were 13.

The Israeli election in February delivered Benjamin Netanyahu, the leader who had buried the Oslo accords in the late 1990s, back into the prime minister's office and any remaining hope for an Israeli-Palestinian peace deal evaporated. Netanyahu is politically dependent on right-wing Jewish settlers in the occupied territories and would be most unlikely to compromise on their demands even if he were personally so inclined (which he is not).

So the 25-year dream of a "two-state" solution gradually fades and the prospect of a third intifada grows. It didn't happen this year and it probably won't happen next year either. But the Israeli military occupation has entered its fifth decade and another generation of Palestinians is growing up so full of rage they will confront Israeli power despite the obvious fact they cannot win. At the other end of the Middle East, Iran was hardly ever out of the news in 2009. The old question of whether or not it is seeking nuclear weapons stayed high on the international agenda, but it was overtaken by the new question of whether the present leadership could stay in power. President Mahmoud Ahmedinejad's implausibly high level of voter support in the June election ignited protests that have shaken the regime's hold on power.

If it is a revolution, it's a very slow-motion one: The protesters are not out on the streets every day, or even every month. Most of them do not even want to overthrow the Islamic system; just to reform it. But they keep coming out, most recently this week, and they are not deterred by mass arrests and systematic rape of detainees, nor by Revolutionary Guards shooting to kill in the streets.

This is the way the Shah was overthrown: by slow degrees, over a period of many months. It may not end the same way this time, but it looks like the same pattern -- and the best thing everybody else can do is not to meddle. Iranian protesters do not need foreign support and they certainly do not need foreign trade sanctions to be applied right now, because that makes them look like foreigners' puppets.

In Iraq, Prime Minister Nouri al-Maliki emerged as a genuine strongman, centralizing power in his own office, staffed by his own party and clan. In Afghanistan, another American nominee, President Hamid Karzai, embarrassed his patrons by rigging his re-election last August too blatantly, but they had to accept him in the end. And in Fantasyland-on-the-Gulf, a.k.a. Dubai, they could not make interest payments on some $50 billion in loans until Abu Dhabi bailed them out.

Europe had a quieter year (as it generally does). Chancellor Angela Merkel of Germany swept back into a second term in September with a "dream coalition" that freed her from having to compromise with the left. France became the largest economy to impose a carbon tax on individuals and businesses using coal, gas or oil, with the explicit intention of changing people's patterns of energy use. The tax is US$24 per tonne of emissions now, but it will rise over the years.

At one extremity of Europe, Northern Ireland was threatened with a slide back into chronic violence, as radical Catholic groups who reject the IRA's commitment to power-sharing tried to lure the Protestants and the British army back into the fight by committing random acts of terrorism. At the continent's other extremity, the Georgian government was found guilty of starting last year's war with Russia by an investigating commission of the European Union.

The EU as a whole finally ratified the Treaty of Lisbon, which streamlines the operation of the organization to cope with a membership that has now expanded to 27 countries. It took eight years, two Irish referendums (they got the answer wrong the first time) and some face-saving concessions to the Czech president to get it through. The EU, it would appear, is still not ready for prime time.

The principal political events in Asia were the Indian election in May, which gave the ruling Congress Party a resounding vote of confidence, and the August election in Japan, which brought the opposition Democratic party to power after 52 years of almost uninterrupted rule by the Liberal Democratic Party. But there was almost no connection between these two elections in anybody's mind: Asia is still little more than a geographical expression.

Elsewhere in Asia, the most important military events were the Sri Lankan government's decisive victory over the Tamil Tiger insurgency in May, which brought a 26-year civil war to an end, and the North Korean nuclear weapons test in the same month. The North Korean regime was mainly using the test as a way to blackmail the major powers into guaranteeing its future and those powers are playing along as usual. They have no choice.

Thailand is caught up in a deepening struggle between the poor majority and the old royalist anti-democratic elites, incarnated in the recurrent street clashes between the Red Shirts and the Yellow Shirts. In Myanmar, pro-democracy leader Aung Sang Suu Kyi, though still under house arrest, has supported efforts by the United States to open up channels of communication with the military junta. A military mutiny was successfully put down in Bangladesh in February.

In Pakistan, an amnesty this month for corruption charges that had protected hundreds of politicians including Prime Minister Asif Ali Zardari was overruled by the Supreme Court and the country was plunged into fresh political turmoil. The Maldives held a full cabinet meeting underwater in scuba gear to dramatize the threat posed to the low-lying island country by rising sea levels. The Nepalese cabinet, not to be outdone, held a meeting on the slopes of Mount Everest to dramatize the threat posed to the country by melting glaciers.

China's emergence as a world player continued, with attention focused in particular on its expanding investments in Africa. The ex-imperial powers saw this as neo-imperialism, but the ex-colonies themselves mostly took a different view. They understood China was trying to secure long-term supplies of food, fuel and minerals for a future in which it believed all those commodities would be scarcer, but at least the Chinese paid well and didn't subject their suppliers to hypocritical lectures on human rights.

A blow-by-blow list of all the things that went wrong in Africa in 2009 is depressing: a military massacre in Guinea, a coup in Madagascar, blood-drenched anarchy in Somalia, mini-wars between the police and extreme religious sects in northern Nigeria and much more in the same vein. But it feels less hopeless if you recall that most of Africa's 52 countries are at peace -- and that African economies have been growing at an average of five per cent a year since 2000, compared to only one per cent in the previous decades since independence.

Much the same observation applies, in a minor key, to Latin America. What little news makes it out of the continent tends to be bad: the brutal war between the state and the drug cartels in Mexico, the alleged threat of war between Venezuela and Colombia, the messy sort-of-coup in Honduras, and so on.

Finally, Copenhagen. The vast, 192-country conference on climate change in December was a total failure in terms of its declared objectives. There is no new treaty to replace the Kyoto accord. Even modest Kyoto-style national targets for cutting greenhouse gas emissions were omitted from the vague declaration that was cobbled together by the two biggest emitters, the United States and China, on the last day. Neither are there any deadlines for further action.

If there is one thing 2009 is remembered for, it may be for the historic failure at Copenhagen.

Gwynne Dyer's most recent book is entitled Climate Wars

In 2009, Journal photographers created about 100 audio slide shows and 175 videos to complement news stories.

Sound and moving pictures give photojournalists even more tools to work with, says Bruce Edwards, who captured all the thrills and spills from the 2009 Canadian Finals Rodeo on video. He chose a rodeo clip as his favourite multimedia element of the year.

"With multimedia, one of the things I like best about it is that you can put the subject's own voice into it," he says.

Read on to find out more about our photographers' favourite productions from the past year.

Toddler came back after H1N1 stronger than ever -
By Carol Sanders
576 words
2 January 2010
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
B1
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

THE flaxen-haired toddler with a pink crocheted blanket was unconscious, alone and hooked up to tubes that helped her tiny, damaged lungs to breathe.

Family photos were taped to the glass of her isolation room window. Her parents, who'd taken her to the emergency room three times before she was admitted to the pediatric intensive care unit and diagnosed with H1N1, waited and hoped she'd recover. She had respiratory problems earlier and got off to a shaky start, health-wise, from the time she was born.

No one knew if she she'd make it.

Never mind ending up in better health than ever.

In the spirit of the season, call it The Miracle on Second Avenue.

In Stonewall, Michelle and Scott Myers saw their little Rhea recover from the deadly virus, then back at home on Second Avenue in Stonewall witnessed the healing of her previously damaged lungs.

When the H1N1 outbreak hit Manitoba, I saw the ferocity of the flu pandemic and the people fighting it first-hand.

A day at Health Sciences Centre touring the intensive care units with Free Press photographer Wayne Glowacki was an unnerving experience.

We saw a jam-packed ICU full of previously healthy people our own age with tubes down their throats, hooked up to ventilators in isolation rooms.

When I asked what the severe H1N1 patients were feeling, chief medical officer Dr. Perry Gray said it was the sensation of drowning.

Your lungs fill up and it's a struggle to take a breath.

Patients had to be heavily sedated and hooked up to the breathing machines. At times, some panicked and tried to pull out the tubes, one nurse said.

Hospital staff and administrators were planning for an onslaught of more H1N1 cases. People were working overtime, some were run off their feet.

The concern was, would there be enough health-care workers and ventilators to handle what might happen?

In the pediatric ICU, a dark-haired preschooler was only mildly sedated. She kicked off her blanket in her isolation room. "Sometimes she gets sad," said the nurse keeping an eye on her.

The unit was full of kids with H1N1 and was jarringly quiet.

We learned later that a child had died there earlier in the day.

It was not Rhea Myers.

Later, that summer, she was happily toddling around home with her older brother Alex and enjoying her swing set but still on supplemental oxygen.

In September, the oxygen tubes were gone, and she was breathing better than ever.

Her mom Michelle said one of the specialists told them the time she had spent on the ventilator had given her growing lungs a break, and a chance to heal.

"It truly is amazing to see her and realize what a strong little girl she is," Michelle said.

"The reality is that the outcome of her recent illness could've been tragic. If there is a silver lining to a child being that sick, perhaps being in the coma gave her body the rest it needed to heal and she has somehow beaten H1N1 and is now healthier than ever."

After weeks of bad news, there was a headline about hope and some very good news.

Thanks again, Rhea. Have a happy, healthy new year.

carol.sanders@freepress.mb.ca

SWINE FLU
National News
How Canada's H1N1 plan delivered for mothers; Pregnant women got advance prescriptions
LISA PRIEST
914 words
2 January 2010
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
A1
English
2010 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Jordana Simms woke in the middle of the night with the feeling her chest was being crushed. She was two months pregnant, suspected she had H1N1 – and her doctor's advice to get antiviral drugs was ringing in her ears.

When her breathing became more strained, she went to a hospital emergency room in Red Deer, Alta. There, she was given a prescription for oseltamivir phosphate, or Tamiflu, nipping H1N1 in the bud and putting her on the road to a happy ending – a pregnancy now in its 16th week.

“We're just really thankful for everything that happened,” Ms. Simms said in a telephone interview from her home in Sylvan Lake, west of Red Deer. “You can't ask for a whole lot more than good health.”

The 23-year-old is the quintessential non-story of the H1N1 pandemic: She is a health disaster averted.

That's because Ms. Simms received prompt medical treatment, encouraged by an initiative of the Society of Obstetricians and Gynecologists of Canada. It recommended to doctors in October something so bold even their American counterparts had not suggested it: to provide advance prescriptions of antiviral drugs to all pregnant women.

“One thing that's good about Canada is the health system is different and the numbers are different as well,” said Vyta Senikas, associate executive vice-president of the Society of Obstetricians and Gynecologists of Canada, which received help from the Public Health Agency of Canada. “Certainly we're smaller so we were able to react.”

Pregnant patients are particularly vulnerable to severe disease due to H1N1. With obstetrical appointments typically being once a month, doctors were urged to write advance prescriptions, knowing antiviral drugs work best when taken within 48 hours of the onset of symptoms such as fever, cough and sore throat.

In Canada, 250 pregnant women have been hospitalized with H1N1, including 30 who were in intensive care and four who died, according to Public Health Agency of Canada figures from April 12, 2009, to Dec. 19, the latest available.

“They are at a tremendously higher risk for getting severe disease,” said Anand Kumar, a Winnipeg-based intensive care and infectious diseases specialist. “… The mothers overall tend to have a worse outcome and the fetuses are lost at a fairly high rate.”

That was certainly the experience in Manitoba. There, 27 pregnant women had H1N1, including six who were in intensive care; two of the six died. And the fetuses fared even worse: One did well, but another suffered severe brain damage due to lack of oxygen, and the remaining four died, according to Stephen Lapinsky, site director of the intensive care unit at Mount Sinai Hospital in Toronto, who, along with Dr. Kumar, is writing a paper on caring for pregnant H1N1 patients.

Treating pregnant patients can be akin to a medical high-wire act: While adults can tolerate low oxygen levels while on a ventilator, fetuses do not. Drugs to support a mother's blood pressure can divert blood away from the uterus and harm a fetus.

“It's a choice between supporting a mother or supporting a fetus,” Dr. Lapinsky said. “And the general sort of approach is to do what you can for the mother, because without a mother, the fetus is not going to survive.”

As for the SOGC initiative, it is difficult to know, as with any pro-active measure, how many pregnant women's lives were saved.

Some women, such as Tasia Coolen, who was given the prescription but never had to use it, felt better just having it in her wallet. When the vaccination became available, she got inoculated. And on Dec. 30, she gave birth to daughter Gabriella at B.C. Women's Hospital and Health Centre.

“I was happy to go see my family doctor, who talked to me about what the pros and cons are [of antiviral medication], and for me, it wasn't something I thought twice about,” said Ms. Coolen, 35, an infant development consultant in Vancouver.

For Ms. Simms, however, it was a lifesaver.

When she came down with flu symptoms in early November, she saw her obstetrician. At the time, her doctor was on the fence about whether to prescribe it, as it wasn't clear she had H1N1. If her symptoms worsened, her doctor told her to return or go to emergency as she would require antiviral medication.

Ms. Simms's symptoms quickly became more severe. In the middle of the night, her husband Tom Simms woke up the children – Kenyon, 3, and Nate, 14 months – bundled them in the car, and they all drove to the emergency department in Red Deer.

“You feel completely helpless,” said Ms. Simms, a retail store manager. “You are terrified for the life of the baby.”

At the hospital, Ms. Simms was given a prescription for Tamiflu. Since the pharmacy wasn't yet open, she contacted her obstetrician early the next morning, wondering if it was safe to take while pregnant. Her doctor told her to fill it. She did and, two days later, she was on the mend.

“Even waiting a few hours can make a huge difference on whether you can be … treated or not,” Ms. Simms said. “I'm glad we didn't wait.”

Now, the only waiting Ms. Simms is doing is for her third child, due in June.
