News
Defined by terrorism, remembered in tears; The first decade of the 21st century was not the best of times -- it's been dubbed everything from The Uh-Oh Decade to the Decade from Hell. Sheldon Alberts reviews the tumultuous times.
Sheldon Alberts
Canwest News Service
3483 words
31 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
A10
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

The 620th day of the new decade began like most others for Kyle Conley, with all the familiar routines of life unfolding as usual.

The 32-year-old financial systems analyst dropped his daughter off at daycare near his home in Arlington, Virginia, then started the stop-and-go commute to his job in downtown Washington.

A new father who would soon learn his wife was expecting their second child, Conley had a comfortable home, a promising career and -- like so many millions of others in the western world -- every reason to believe the relative peace and prosperity of the time would continue uninterrupted.

Then, just around 9:40 a.m., as he was crossing the Theodore Roosevelt Bridge over the Potomac River, Conley glanced up to see the brilliant blue September sky blighted by a plume of black smoke rising above the Pentagon.

In lower Manhattan, the twin towers were already engulfed in flames and nearing collapse.

Although he didn't know it at the time, Conley's future had been forever altered, and along with it the arc of a suddenly tumultuous decade.

"Looking back, I don't remember having any particular concerns about what was going on in the world at the time, or being concerned with things outside my own personal life," Conley says now.

"That all changed on Sept. 11. I realized for the first time that we are vulnerable to attack from overseas, that even close to home, awful things can happen."

- - -

Conley's reaction rings true for anyone who experienced terrorism up close in the past decade, whether in London or Madrid, Bali or Baghdad, Mumbai or Moscow, Kandahar or Kabul.

As 2009 draws to an unsettling close, what's surprising is that no one agrees on what to name the 10-year period that is passing into history. It has been called "The Aughts," the "Naughty Aughties" and even the "Uh-Oh Decade," a favourite among cheekier historians. Time magazine took a dimmer view in November by dubbing it "The Decade from Hell."

This much is certain. It was a decade marked by a demarcation line so clear and jarring -- before and after the 9/11 hijackings -- that one strains to recall the world as it was when the calendar flipped from 1999 to 2000.

"The emergence of stateless terrorism as a major international threat will go down as the defining event, without question," says Julian Zelizer, a history professor at Princeton University. "It cemented terrorism as the No. 1 national security issue, not just for the United States but for so many countries. It's not a threat that will dissipate anytime soon."

With 10 years of memory providing a kaleidoscope of perspective, it seems almost comical that the decade opened with the planet gripped by anxiety about impending technological chaos.

The dreaded Y2K computer threat came and went with a whimper, not a bang. Personal computers did not crash. Banking systems did not fail. Satellites did not fall from their orbits. It was the Chicken Little of international crises, a millennial false alarm.

Before 9/11, it was conventional wisdom this would be the decade of American empire, with the world's lone 'hyperpower' becoming even more omnipotent in its global influence.

America had moments of triumph, none more powerful than Barack Obama's election as the first black president of a nation whose history was forged by slavery. But the Sept. 11 attacks eclipsed Obama's election in their sheer world-shaking magnitude, ushering in a decade of American vulnerability that created ripple effects worldwide.

It was in some ways a decade when fear -- of suitcase nukes, of melting polar ice caps, of pandemic viruses and nail clippers on airplanes -- held firm grip over entire populations. And in the way most of us experienced the 9/11 attacks -- first on live TV, then repeated in an endless digital loop -- they presaged an era when technology would make the world, and all of its tragedy, hope and despair, a much smaller place.

- - -

As an American, Kyle Conley was so angered by 9/11 that he put his civilian life on hold to join the U.S. Marine Corps reserves. He left his job and his pregnant wife in January 2002 for boot camp in Parris Island, South Carolina, thinking he would eventually be deployed to Afghanistan.

"I recognized the need for people to go out on the front lines and risk our lives to get the bad guys, and be part of a larger effort to create a homeland where this wouldn't occur again," he says.

Whatever sense -- or hope -- of global security that emerged at the end of the Cold War was lost on 9/11.

"I was in my 20s when the Cold War faded way. Things looked a little rosier," Conley says.

"There weren't really major conflicts on the horizon. It is kind of naive in hindsight. I was like the average person who saw 9/11 as sort of out of the blue."

By the time Conley's unit was activated, the Taliban had fallen and then U.S. president George W. Bush's attention shifted from Afghanistan. Full of presidential hubris and armed with faulty intelligence, the U.S. invaded Iraq in March 2003.

Conley's light armoured battalion was at the tip of the spear, leading reconnaissance patrols as U.S. forces advanced toward Baghdad.

He returned safely to the U.S. in August 2003, only to be deployed again in 2006. Conley spent a second Iraq tour in a riverine boat patrolling the Euphrates River from Ramadi to the Syrian border.

- - -

From the start of the war on terror to the end of the Great Recession, much of the past decade's history can be written through the prism of an America thrown off kilter.

"It was the decade when the United States lost its confidence, militarily and economically," says Zelizer.

More than eight years after toppling the Taliban, the insurgents continue fighting and the war in Afghanistan is about to escalate.

In Iraq, U.S. combat forces will leave only in August, more than seven years after Bush first declared an end to major combat operations.

"Whether you were for the Iraq war or against it, it really raised questions about what the U.S. is capable of doing to combat the threats that the country faces now," Zelizer says. "We can get bogged down now just as easily as in the 1960s."

There were other consequences to America's shattered sense of security after 9/11. For Canadians, the world's longest undefended border is a line you can no longer cross without a passport. Unmanned aerial drones patrol the frontier.

Extraordinary rendition and Guantanamo were also the fallout of post-9/11 American insecurity, producing stress fractures in the relationship between America, Europe and the Arab world.

On the economic front, the past decade revealed an underlying U.S. weakness that caught other nations by surprise. Wall Street's indulgence in risky mortgage securities led to the near-collapse of the nation's banking industry in the fall of 2008, sparking a global recession. The once-mighty U.S. dollar has beaten a steady retreat, while the national debt has surged above $12 trillion.

The bankruptcies of General Motors and Chrysler highlighted U.S. industrial decline.

"America went into the decade as the great victor from the Cold War. There was a combination of self-righteousness and a sense of power. Both of those have been lost," says Gil Troy, a history professor at McGill University.

"The two biggest traumas of the decade -- 9/11 and the economic meltdown -- hit America very personally, where it hurts. You have a country that has been knocked down a peg or two."

Even American democracy was showing wear and tear. The presidential vote of 2000 introduced "dimpled chads" into the global democratic lexicon and put George W. Bush in the White House, but only after 36 days of indecision that exposed significant flaws in the U.S. electoral system.

At no moment did America seem more uncertain of itself than in the aftermath of Hurricane Katrina and the flooding of New Orleans.

"Katrina was very traumatic. It totally exposed the limits of what public policy could accomplish in this country," says Zelizer.

"You had this city devastated and essentially all levels of government failed."

As America suffered, China rose. Now the world's third-largest and still fastest-growing economy, China survived the global recession in far better health than nations in the West. China endured its own national disaster -- the Sichuan earthquake, which killed tens of thousands -- but it also threw its international coming-out party with the 2008 Beijing Olympics.

"The rise of China didn't start in 2000 or 2001, but this was the decade that it became clearer we are dealing with a very formidable economic force," says Zelizer. "They have a kind of economic power that all countries, including the United States, will have to reckon with in the next few years."

- - -

Did we mention it was the decade of fear?

In 2006, bird flu was going to spread across oceans from Hong Kong and sicken millions. The H1N1 virus prompted the largest immunization program in history, and the lineups for vaccines in Canada and the United States underscored the collective international angst.

North Americans learned their seaports were so porous that nuclear weapons could be smuggled in container ships without detection. In the U.S., the new normal meant deciphering colour-coded terror alerts and worrying about anthrax in the mail. The U.S. got Saddam Hussein, but Osama bin Laden remains a phantom threat.

The fear "started in some ways with the Y2K scare," says Troy. "In the era of 24/7 media, there is this chain of transmission that leads to hysteria and, in some ways, our worst expectations keep on not being met."

Except when it was justified. Terrorism aside, there was little in the past decade to ease anxiety about the broader state of global security. In Darfur, genocide claimed the lives of several hundred thousand people. AIDS was the health crisis of the decade, sweeping silently across Africa and killing millions.

North Korea test fired nuclear devices in 2006 and 2009, and Iran remained defiant in the development of its own atomic weapons program. The Israeli-Palestinian situation continues to fester. In each case, international diplomacy mostly failed to lower the threat.

- - -

The Sept. 11 attacks were the most traumatic act of violence ever broadcast on live TV. But the Internet quickly brought other calamities closer to home. The 2004 tsunami in the Indian Ocean killed more than 230,000 people, horror enough. But handheld YouTube videos showing the giant waves crashing ashore in Thailand and Indonesia created a near-instant global awareness of nature's force and its human toll.

When NBC journalist Tim Russert died of a heart attack at his office in 2008, the story broke on Wikipedia before being announced on his own network. Bad news never travelled faster.

But neither did celebratory news. People attending Obama's historic inauguration shared their feelings about the experience 140 characters at a time on a revolutionary site called Twitter.

The advances in Internet technology democratized communication in a way that may have profound geopolitical consequences, Zelizer says.

Twitter, for example, became the technology of choice for Iranians to tell the world about street demonstrations following last June's election. The shooting of Iranian protester Neda Soltan in 2009 was captured on video and broadcast on YouTube, generating global awareness of the regime's crackdown. "It was in this decade that the total sweep of the Internet really changed peoples' lives," Zelizer says. "It is having a dramatic effect around the spread of proto-democratic sentiment emerging in authoritarian countries. A government can try to control it but it will be very hard to do so."

- - -

The decade closes with a bookend to Sept. 11, 2001, as 30,000 American troops prepare for deployment to the war launched in response to those attacks. Ominously, passengers and crew of Northwest Airlines flight 253 with almost 300 people aboard had to overpower a passenger after he ignited an explosive device attached to his body as the aircraft as it approached Detroit from Amsterdam on Christmas Day.

Conley, now 40 and the father of three, is on inactive reserve and working in Washington with the same company that employed him on 9/11. His old Marine unit is back in Afghanistan.

Deciding to enlist was "one of the best decisions I ever made," he says, and part of him wishes he were in Afghanistan with his buddies.

For all that has happened since then, Conley believes the U.S. and the world are more secure today, even though unfinished business remains.

"In 2001, did I think in 2009 that we wouldn't get bin Laden? No," he says. "I thought there was no chance he'd still be out there."

- - -

2000

May: Survivor, a reality television program using real people in unscripted contests or artificial situations in exotic or bizarre locations, debuts and spawns a hugely popular new genre.

March 26: Vladimir Putin wins the presidential election in Russia.

June 13: North and South Korea sign a peace accord.

Dec. 12: Five weeks after the vote, Al Gore finally concedes and George W. Bush is declared the winner of the American presidential election.

Dec. 9: The United States invades Afghanistan in retaliation for the terror attacks.

2009

Nov. 25: The National Enquirer tabloid breaks a story about golf superstar Tiger Woods's marital infidelity.

EDITORIAL
2009: Really bad things didn't happen
Gwynne Dyer
737 words
31 December 2009
Waterloo Region Record
TKWR
Final
A13
English
Copyright (c) 2009 Kitchener-Waterloo Record.

The year 2009 was most notable for the three bad things that didn't happen. First, the financial melt-down of late 2008 did not plunge us all into a 1930s-style depression, although there were plenty of pundits predicting that less than a year ago.

As Stephen Schwarzman, head of the private equity company Blackstone Group, said last March: "Between 40 and 45 per cent of the world's wealth has been destroyed in little more than a year and a half." But all he was really saying was that a very specific kind of financial bubble has burst. All the land and houses are still there, and so are most of the factories and jobs.

There has certainly been a deep recession in the developed countries, and the current slow recovery may be a false dawn: a "double-dip" recession is still entirely possible.

Moreover, the vast amounts of money spent by Western governments to save the banks has left them with a staggering burden of debt. But the worst has been avoided, and in the developing countries there was scarcely even a recession.

The second predicted disaster that did not come to pass was a killer global pandemic like the 1918 strain of influenza. Something of that order is probably still lying in wait for us down the road, but the H1N1 flu turned out to be much less lethal than was initially feared. Considerable credit should go to those who made a vaccine available much faster than was thought possible, but basically we just got lucky.

And the third bad thing that didn't happen? The same bad thing that hasn't happened every year since 2001. There was no mass loss of life (by which I mean more than a thousand people murdered in a single incident) due to terrorist action in any Western country.

Even a relatively large death toll like that should not be a reason for any government to go berserk and start invading foreign countries. A thousand people is a lot to lose, but it really isn't the end of the world. A thousand people die of natural and accidental causes in the United States (to pick a country not entirely at random) about every three hours.

Terrorism is different, of course, but a rational and measured response is still required.

You know very well that it would be neither rational nor measured. Although an over-reaction is precisely what the terrorists are seeking to provoke, domestic political realities in the target country still make it likely that the response would be hugely stupid and violent.

So we go from year to year waiting for the terrorists to succeed again on the scale of 9/11, knowing that Western countries will go crazy again if they do. But it didn't happen in 2009.

What did finally happen at the beginning of 2009 was the long-overdue departure of the George W. Bush administration. Almost everybody outside the United States, and many people within it, were profoundly relieved by that, but it imposed a huge burden of expectation on the shoulders of his successor as president, Barack Obama.

It's been a difficult first year for Obama, who presumably expected to have both his health-care reforms and a climate change bill through the U.S. Congress by now. His problems with Afghanistan, however, are largely of his own making.

Then there was Copenhagen. The vast, 192-country conference on climate change in December was a total failure in terms of its declared objectives. There is no new treaty to replace the Kyoto accord. Even modest Kyoto-style national targets for cutting greenhouse gas emissions were omitted from the vague declaration that was cobbled together by the two biggest emitters, the United States and China, on the last day. Neither are there any deadlines for further action.

"The Chinese hate numbers," as one participant put it – and the United States was quite happy to let China get the blame for killing a deal that Washington couldn't have delivered on either. The problem is that nobody really knows where we go from here, and time is running short. If there is one thing that 2009 is remembered for, it may be for the historic failure at Copenhagen.

Gwynne Dyer is an independent journalist based in London, England.

Odds of catching H1N1 are 1 in 8

How contagious is swine flu? New research suggests less than the novel viruses that have caused big world outbreaks in the past.

If someone in your home has swine flu, your odds of catching it are about one in eight, although the study found children are twice as susceptible as adults.

It is one of the first big scientific attempts to find out how much the illness spreads in homes versus at work or school, and who is most at risk.

The study appears in today's New England Journal of Medicine.

Researchers studied infection patterns in 216 people with swine flu in the U.S. (half of them children) and 600 people living with them. Respiratory illnesses that researchers assumed were swine flu developed in 78 of the 600 household members or 13 per cent. However, 10 per cent had symptoms more specific to flu. That's less than the "spread" rate during earlier flu pandemics in 1957 and 1968, when 14 per cent to 20 per cent of household members were infected.

Ginkgo biloba not helpful

The popular herbal supplement ginkgo biloba may not be everything it's claimed to be.

A study says it does not help slow mental decline in older adults.

The study examined whether a twice-daily 120-milligram dose of the supplement affected the rate of cognitive change over time in older adults.

U.S. researchers found no evidence it slows mental decline in healthy, aging individuals, or in people already showing the first signs of cognitive impairment.

The study was published in the Journal of the American Medical Association.

STEEL SHUTDOWN

Hamilton was rocked in March by the U.S. Steel announcement it was shutting down all operations at the former Stelco, throwing 1,500 employees in the city and Nanticoke out of work indefinitely.

The news was the most shocking ripple of the economic crisis that had been battering Hamilton and its struggling manufacturing sector.

"This is just going to devastate Hamilton," said Rolf Gerstenberger, president of the United Steelworkers union in Hamilton.

A portion of the laid-off workers returned a few months later.

THE TIGER-CATS

The return to respectability of the Hamilton Tiger-Cats. After winning just 12 Canadian Football League contests in the previous three seasons, the Ticats, under the tutelage of head coach Marcel Bellefeuille, finished the 2009 campaign at 9-9. That was good enough to earn the Tabbies their first home playoff contest in eight seasons.

SUMMER OF FLOODS

The flood in mid-summer won't soon be forgotten, especially by homeowners in the city's east end. In some areas of the city more rain fell in two hours than is normally received in a month.

The flood prompted city council to offer up to $11 million to help cover the losses suffered by thousands. The city has also made flood prevention a priority with $30 million in sewer upgrades planned for this year and $200 million budgeted over the next five years.

PAN AM GAMES

The awarding of the 2015 Pan Am Games to Toronto proved a huge story for Hamilton. And for good reason. Hamilton will be the site of a 15,000-seat stadium of which the city will pay $45 million. Private funding for stadium expansion is expected to turn the facility into the new home for the Tiger-Cats.

The games will also deliver a new state-of-the-art pool for McMaster University.

Ontario will spend $1.4 billion for facilities and operations with $170 million flowing into Hamilton and Burlington.

The Pan Ams also helped soothe the sting from the city's two previous failed bids for the Commonwealth Games.

H1N1 OUTBREAK

The H1N1 outbreak made headlines in October and November across the province and the flu hit Hamilton harder than most communities. Hamilton schools were especially hit hard by the pandemic. And the situation wasn't helped by incidents of queue-jumping for vaccinations and by a shortage of the vaccine.

Hamilton opened up two flu assessment centres and Hamilton public health was on the front lines doing its best to keep a worried public calm.

CITY HALL

The ongoing Hamilton City Hall renovation project caught the public attention as municipal politicians squabbled over whether the new cladding should be marble or cheaper concrete. The latter won out. While the renovation went on quietly for the most part, the work was visible, with the former City Hall resembling a bombed-out building for most of the summer.

Do you remember... 2009? -
By Iyvan Michalchyshyn and Bill Patterson
1557 words
31 December 2009

This news quiz was compiled from stories that appeared on the front pages of the Winnipeg Free Press in 2009

Do you remember...

2009?

1. Jan. 6 Canada's junior hockey players win a fifth straight gold medal. Who is their opponent in the championship final?

2. Jan. 16 US Airways flight No. 1549 is forced to land in a river following a collision with a flock of geese. Which river?

3. Jan. 26 A large bear is sighted in northern Manitoba. What is unusual about this sighting?

4. Feb. 2 An Olympic swimming superstar is spotted at a party smoking dope. Who is he?

5. Feb. 8 Australians flee for their lives in the outback. Why?

6. Feb. 10 Winnipeggers experience a bizarre winter weather phenomenon. What has happened?

7. Feb. 15 Demonstrators protest a historical re-enactment planned for the summer in Quebec City. What's the historical event?

SMALL DOSES / KEY OUTCOMES / HEALTHCARE
Globe Life Column
The Obama effect and other lessons from the year in medicine
PAUL TAYOR
1933 words
31 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
L1
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

ptaylor@globeandmail.com

When Barack Obama was sworn in as U.S. President in January, many top Canadian scientists feared they would soon be hearing a great sucking sound as research resources and talent were drawn back to the United States.

After all, Canada had indirectly benefited from some of the restrictive science policies of the George W. Bush administration, such as federal funding curbs on stem-cell research. Canada, by contrast, seemed like a friendlier place to do science. Better still, many Canadian scientists had enjoyed hefty increases in their research grants in the early part of the decade.

Now the tables have turned. Mr. Obama declared that science is “essential” for America's continued health, security and prosperity. His economic-stimulus package included $10-billion (U.S.) for medical research.

At about the same time, Prime Minister Stephen Harper's government trimmed the budgets of three granting agencies that fund university-based research – the type of work that can potentially generate major discoveries and groundbreaking innovations.

A lot of researchers are skeptical of the Canadian government's commitment to science, even though Ottawa has pledged more money for university infrastructure and equipment. The money, some researchers argue, should be going to “brains not bricks.”

The government's credibility took another hit when Gary Goodyear, the federal minister responsible for science and technology, declined to answer a question about whether he believes in evolution, during an interview with The Globe and Mail's science reporter Anne McIlroy. Mr. Goodyear, a chiropractor by training, later said he did believe in evolution but the remarks he made to clarify his position were so ambiguous that experts in developmental biology wondered whether he understood the concept.

Some high-profile scientists – including one of Canada's leading AIDS researchers, Rafick-Pierre Sékaly of the University of Montreal – have already begun to shift their research operations south of the border.

But it's far from an exodus, and Canada continues to attract U.S. investment. Californian and Canadian officials recently announced a $40-million joint venture in stem-cell research.

As this year draws to a close, it's not yet clear that the United States has regained its competitive edge.

“Obama is certainly pro-research and pro-science,” said Gordon Keller, a stem-cell biologist and director of Toronto's McEwen Centre for Regenerative Medicine.

“But he is facing trillion-plus-dollar deficits and trying to get health care off the ground. I am not sure how much money there will be for … increased long-term funding for National Institutes of Health,” which finances a substantial chunk of medical research in the United States.

Another factor in Canada's continued favour is government funding for the health-care system and universities, which provides many researchers with their core salaries, said John Dick, a senior scientist at the University Health Network in Toronto. “It's not a life and death situation if you lose your grant – you're are not out of a job the next month,” he explained. And that's not necessarily the case in the United States.

The following is a wrap-up of some of the year's medical highlights, low points and emerging trends to watch in 2010.

Lessons from a pandemic

The H1N1 influenza pandemic turned out to be not as bad as some were expecting.

It's impossible to calculate the severity of the outbreak, because the vast majority of cases never come to the attention of public health officials. But using the best available evidence from the United States, researchers at Harvard's School of Public Health and the U.K.'s Medical Research Council, have come up with two estimates.

Under one set of data and assumptions, they concluded 1.44 per cent of people with H1N1 symptoms were hospitalized and 0.048 per cent died. Under another set, 0.16 per cent were hospitalized and 0.007 per cent died, according to their study published earlier this month in the online journal PLoS Medicine.

That works out to a potential range of 7,800 to 29,000 deaths in the United States from H1N1, compared to the annual flu, which kills 36,000 in that country.

Using the same approach, that would mean between 780 and 2,900 H1N1 deaths in Canada. That's significantly fewer than the roughly 4,000 or more Canadians who die in a “normal” flu season. (The estimate from the Public Health Agency of Canada stood at 336 in mid-December – but this figure represents only confirmed deaths directly linked to H1N1.)

“Our work shows that the severity of H1N1 may be less than initially feared,” said Marc Lipsitch, who led the research team at Harvard.

Does that mean public health officials over-reacted to the perceived threat? No, says Dr. Lipsitch. “It's a sign of good judgment in public health to prepare for something worse than actually happens, because you can't know until it happens how bad it's going to be.”

He also noted that H1N1 hit some younger people especially hard, while the seasonal flu tends to take its biggest toll among the elderly.

“It's still bad. It's still putting people in hospital and it's killing more children than normal seasonal flu,” said Dr. Lipsitch. “So it's not harmless, but it's milder than other pandemics have been.”

While public health officials may have acted prudently, there were some snags and lessons to be learned for the next pandemic. H1N1 infections started to peak in Canada well before ample supplies of the vaccine could be produced and distributed across the country. Some experts say Canada may be better served by using multiple vaccine suppliers, rather than relying on just one company.

Folic acid folly?

For more than a decade, food manufacturers in Canada and the United States have been required by law to add folic acid to bread, pasta, flour and other grain products. The fortification effort is meant to ensure that women receive adequate levels of folic acid in case they become pregnant. The B vitamin is critical in the early stages of fetal development.

And, by all accounts, the mandatory program has been highly successful in reducing certain birth defects such as spina bifida, which has plunged by 50 per cent to about 110 cases a year in Canada.

However, other research has hinted that there might be a dark side to fortification – fuelling cancers in the general population.

Two studies, published earlier this year, suggested folic acid supplements may boost the odds of getting prostate and colon cancers.

Then, in November, yet another study showed an overall increase in cancer cases and deaths among Norwegians taking folic acid pills.

Although these findings are worrisome, experts generally agree there's not enough evidence to justify pulling the plug on folic acid fortification.

Still, it's certainly a research field worth watching. Policy makers may eventually be compelled to revisit a program that essentially treats everyone the same way – rather than targeting just those who are supposed to benefit from it.

New hope for MS?

An Italian doctor has captured international attention with a new theory – and possible treatment – for multiple sclerosis.

Paolo Zamboni, a professor of medicine at the University of Ferrara, has published a series of papers suggesting that a narrowing of blood vessels in the neck could contribute to the development of MS. According to Dr. Zamboni, veins draining blood from the brain are malformed, or become blocked, leading to a buildup of iron in the brain which, he thinks, causes the neurological symptoms of MS.

By increasing blood flow, through a relatively simple operation, he believes symptoms can be reversed or minimized. The procedure has been performed on 65 MS patients, including Dr. Zamboni's wife, with mixed results.

The medical community looks upon Dr. Zamboni's work with some degree of skepticism. “We have seen similar claims of potential causes and cures in the past,” the University of British Columbia MS Clinic said in a recent statement.

Most previous research has indicated that MS is an autoimmune disease in which the body's own immune system attacks the protective myelin coating on nerves. The disease can affect balance, mobility, vision, hearing and memory. Dr. Zamboni acknowledges his findings are preliminary and need to be confirmed by other research teams. And even if the treatment works in some patients, MS may still be an autoimmune disorder. Poor blood drainage could simply be a trigger that sets the disease in motion, according to Dr. Zamboni.

The MS Society of Canada, with an annual research budget of about $10-million, has urged scientists to submit proposals to investigate the theory. UBC has requested funds to begin a study involving 100 patients.

Perplexing rise of celiac disease

Celiac disease was once considered to be a fairly rare disorder. But an increasing number of people are being stricken with the digestive ailment. And there is good reason to be concerned – it can lead to an early death.

A study by researchers at the Mayo Clinic in Rochester, Minn., revealed the disease is 4.5 times more common today than it was about 50 years ago. “It now affects about one in 100 people,” said Joseph Murray, lead author of the study published in the journal Gastroenterology.

Celiac disease is an autoimmune disorder that damages the small intestine, interfering with the absorption of nutrients. It is triggered by gluten, a protein found in wheat, barley and rye.

Dr. Murray says something must have changed in the environment to make the condition more common. He speculated that modern food production and processing could be to blame. But, he readily acknowledges, “it could be something entirely different.”

Faux journals Pharmaceutical companies will often use favourable studies and review articles published in peer-reviewed journals as part of their sale pitches to doctors.

“See, look how well our drug works,” the drug reps will say as they hand physicians reprints of the seemingly objective reports.

But what if there is a lack of published material? Or, more to the point, no articles with a positive spin?

Well, if you're especially audacious, it seems you just create a journal out of thin air.

Pharmaceutical giant Merck paid an undisclosed sum to publishing firm Elsevier to produce several editions of what looked like peer-reviewed medical journals, The Scientist magazine revealed earlier this year.

The pseudo-publications, part of a Merck marketing effort to raise the profile of the company's drugs among Australian physicians, came to light during court proceedings.

One of the publications was titled The Australasian Journal of Bone and Joint Medicine and included glowing reviews of Merck's osteoporosis drug Fosamax and painkiller Vioxx, which has since been pulled from the market.

Rush to judgment

If there is a lesson to be learned from 2009 it's this: Don't jump to conclusions.

Take the case of a British schoolgirl who died within hours of receiving a vaccination that provides protection against cervical cancer.

Many parents – including some in Canada – began to question the safety of the shots.

However, an autopsy later revealed that Natalie Morton's chest cavity was filled with cancer.

It now appears the timing of her death, so close to getting the vaccine jab, was just a coincidence. The multiple tumours were so advanced, “death could have arisen at any point,” pathologist Alexander Kolar told an inquest.

OK, here's what's happening... - Pundits make predictions for the year ahead
1785 words
31 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A6
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

The last week of December often provokes people to think back over the year coming to an end.

Today, however, the Free Press looks ahead at the year about to begin. We asked three of Winnipeg's brightest commentators 10 questions about 2010.

THE PANEL:

CHRIS ADAMS, Probe Research vice-president, adjunct professor, University of Winnipeg / University of Manitoba

DAVID

BARNARD, University of Manitoba president and vice-chancellor

SHANNON

SAMPERT,

University of Winnipeg, political studies professor and pundit

THE ECONOMY

1. WILL THE SLOWDOWN LEAVE MANITOBA IN THE RED AND PUSH FEDERAL FINANCES DEEPER INTO DEFICIT?

ADAMS: Probe Research's recent Winnipeg Free Press/Jory Capital poll shows that 80 per cent of Manitobans are confident about the local economy. Yet provincial Finance Minister Rosann Wowchuk announced just before Christmas that there will be a shortfall of $592 million for the 2009 budget year. Compared to the federal government's growing debt load, and what is happening in Ontario, this is peanuts.

BARNARD: Experts at the University's Asper School of Business agree with others that in 2010 federal and provincial governments will be managing deficits created by the economic downturn and needed investment in economic stimulus. But Manitoba's diversified economy, resilient citizens and major economic contributors like the University of Manitoba ($1.8 billion annual economic impact) will soon bring about recovery.

SAMPERT: The decision by the Harper government to implement stimulus programs as a way of dealing with the economy has resulted in a record-breaking deficit situation in Canada. This could have an effect on Manitoba's economy and in particular in Winnipeg, which houses a large number of federal-government employees should the government decide to trim its public service.

NATIONAL NEWS

2. WILL CANADIAN VOTERS HEAD TO THE POLLS AGAIN FOR A FEDERAL ELECTION AND IF SO, WHO WILL WIN?

ADAMS: The odds are that we will have an election, simply because the Conservatives need support from at least one other party to pass any bills in the House of Commons. The outcome of a 2010 election depends on where the Liberals are riding on the roller-coaster of public support. Probe's polling data show that there would be few changes here in Manitoba.

BARNARD: I cannot predict if an election will be called, but if it is, the party with the majority of seats in the House of Commons will win.

SAMPERT: While I don't think we're going to see a record number turnout at the polls, Harper is likely going to see himself leading a majority for the first time. Ignatieff just can't bring in the polling numbers in the face of an effective and well-financed perpetual campaign being staged by the hungry Conservatives.

WINNIPEG

3. WILL SAM KATZ STILL BE MAYOR AFTER THE CIVIC ELECTION IN OCTOBER?

ADAMS: Probably. To unseat a sitting mayor there needs to be a strong challenger, without other contenders to divide the opposition vote. A June Probe Research/Winnipeg Free Press poll revealed that 74 per cent of Winnipeggers thought that Mayor Katz is performing well. However, this is soft: Only 16 per cent said he is doing it "very well." A few might jump the Sammy ship.

BARNARD: I hope that the civic election will result in a broad engagement of citizens in choosing our leaders. I also expect that following the election University of Manitoba alumni will again be in many leadership roles in civic government.

SAMPERT: Katz is a pretty average mayor, and being average will likely work to his advantage. He hasn't incensed voters enough to oust him; there doesn't seem to be a concerted bid to run a campaign against him. His incumbency will work to his advantage.

HEALTH

4. WILL THERE BE A THIRD WAVE OF H1N1?

ADAMS: Not wanting to pretend to be an expert on this topic (pollsters don't know everything, you know), there appears to be a lot of speculation out there in media reports. Worrisome is that there is a lot of winter left to go and it might be returning for years to come. Most of the Probe Research staff got the shot. Have you?

BARNARD: If there is, I'm reassured by the fact that University of Manitoba researchers will take the lead in identifying, tracking and advising on how to deal with the problem, as has been the case thus far. But I hope not. I was incapacitated for over two weeks with what was likely H1N1 and I don't want others to share that experience.

SAMPERT: Most experts suggest that H1N1 will be an issue for up to two years and the next three months are the months in which we tend to become more prone to illness. What is particularly scary is that so few people have been inoculated in Manitoba -- only about a third of the population has received the H1N1 shot.

THE WORLD

5. WILL THE TROOP SURGE IN AFGHANISTAN RESULT IN FEWER DEATHS FOR CANADIAN AND ALLIED SOLDIERS?

ADAMS: We can only hope that there will be fewer deaths for Canadians, our allies and others living in Afghanistan. Who could have predicted at the beginning of this decade that such questions would be asked? Certainly not me, nor most of our fellow panellists.

BARNARD: Eventually, and we can all pray that it will be soon.

SAMPERT: There are still considerable issues with corruption in governance in Afghanistan and many see a troop surge built on an alliance with a corrupt government as doomed. Recent polls suggest that Canadians aren't sold on the U.S. troop surge, either. Bottom line: We'll see more young men and women coming home in body bags in what is becoming another Vietnam.

6. THE U.S. SAYS IT WILL BE DOWN TO 50,000 SOLDIERS IN IRAQ BY AUGUST 2010 -- THERE ARE ABOUT 115,000 NOW. WILL IT MAKE THE CUT?

ADAMS: According to my better-informed friends who do research and teach about such matters, it looks like there is a real probability that this will happen. Despite recent "high-profile attacks," violence has declined to pre-2003 levels. Obama wants out, as do an increasing number of Americans. Trimming to 50,000 is likely.

BARNARD: President Obama's commitment to peace was certainly enough to impress the Nobel committee. That commitment will motivate him to at least come very close to making this cut.

SAMPERT: This looks promising. There seems to be a transition from a focus on Iraq to a focus on Afghanistan and Pakistan and there seems to be an interest in decreasing its numbers in Iraq to focus on these fronts. That could all change should there be a resurgence of a Sunni backlash. Much still could go wrong. But for now, it looks good.

7. WILL THE U.S. LIFT ITS EMBARGO ON CUBA?

ADAMS: Florida is a key electoral state, and with many Cuban-Americans located in Florida you can bet the Democrats are examining how such a decision would influence their party's fortunes in the mid-term congressional elections and the next presidential election. Because they are juggling too many hot-potato issues now, I predict that the embargo will stay in 2010.

BARNARD: Eventually, but it is unlikely to be a priority in the coming year. In the meantime, the University of Manitoba's faculty of nursing will continue training Cuban nurses and working to improve that country's health-care system.

SAMPERT: This seems to be the last vestige of the Cold War and now in a time of hyper-globalization, it may be a good time to resume trade with Cuba. Maybe Obama could stem his falling polling numbers by offering Americans Cuba as a cheap tropical vacation location.

SPORTS

8. WILL THERE BE A SEVENTH CANADIAN NHL TEAM TO START THE 2010-11 SEASON? IF SO, WILL IT BE IN WINNIPEG?

ADAMS: It's funny how our NHL fortunes keep pace with those of Quebec City, whose beloved Nordiques entered the NHL along with the Jets and Oilers, left to the U.S. at the same time, and now the Nordiques look to be coming home again soon. A June 2009 Probe Research/Jory Capital poll revealed that a majority of Winnipeggers want the same for our city.

BARNARD: That would be a great addition to the city.

SAMPERT: The loss of the Jets has caused such psychic injury to Winnipeg, it's still an emotional topic more than a decade later. No. We're not going to get an NHL team. We can't afford it. And the league would be foolish to expand at a time when the average consumer is having a hard time putting food on the table.

9. WILL CANADA 'OWN THE PODIUM' (WIN THE MOST MEDALS) AT THE VANCOUVER OLYMPICS?

ADAMS: According to a recent Winnipeg Free Press article (Dec. 19), Canada ranks seventh in the all-time record for medals, behind such countries as Norway, Germany and the U.S.A. What is so different for 2010? While cheering for all of our athletes, especially (speedskater) Cindy (Klassen), we want gold medals for curling and hockey.

BARNARD: We're all hoping for great things from our athletes, including Manitobans Cindy Klassen and Clara Hughes (honorary graduates from the University of Manitoba).

SAMPERT: We'll do better than in years in which we're not the host country, but we won't win the most medals. We don't spend the money we need to train these athletes like they do in other countries. Regardless, we'll still be incredibly proud of those who sport the Canadian flag when they wave from the podium.

10. WHICH IS MORE LIKELY TO HAPPEN IN 2010: THE BOMBERS MAKE THE PLAYOFFS OR SOD IS TURNED ON THE LONG-PROMISED NEW FOOTBALL STADIUM?

ADAMS: When polling 1,000 Manitobans 18 months ago about the stadium, approximately half told us they supported the use of public funds for this purpose. Although there are more hurdles for David Asper and others to overcome before any sod-turning occurs, it's hard to imagine that we are on an equally positive track towards making the playoffs. We need a general manager, coach, not to mention some player personnel changes.

BARNARD: I'm fully expecting that the sod will turn on the new stadium. And I look forward to watching playoff games for both the Bombers and the Bisons there when it is completed.

SAMPERT: Or Mike Kelly returns as the coach? Honestly, none of those seems to be a reality in 2010.

RALLY / DAKAR 2010
Globe Megawheels
Canadian hopes third time will be the charm; Montrealer Patrick Trahan's pursuit to complete the world's most dangerous motor race has come at a steep price
PHILIPPE DEVOS
1442 words
31 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
G8
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

More than 380 motorcycles, quads, cars and trucks will be at the starting line tomorrow for the start of the annual Dakar Rally, and no one can remember a year when there were more Canadians among them.

Seven competitors from the Great White North have registered for the gruelling, 17-day off-road race in the South American wilderness.

Some have tried it before, but none has attempted it more times than Montrealer Patrick Trahan, who's lining up for his third start, 10 years after he first tried to conquer the most dangerous motor race in the world.

The man

He left a career as an IT consultant to devote himself full-time to motorcycling some 12 years ago. “When I'm passionate about something, I don't wait; I do.” In 1998, he showed up for his first motorcycle rally in Morocco with 10 hours total riding experience and a new Kawasaki KLR650. That's like showing up for a NASCAR Sprint Cup race with a learner's permit and a Chevy Impala straight off the lot. But he didn't let naivety get the better of his passion. Despite repeated crashes, he managed to finish by MacGyvering his tools into spare parts (a vise grip replaced a cracked gear shifter), and at one point getting towed out of the sand by a camel. “There's two kinds of people,” the 42-year-old says. “The people who hate the Dakar: They do one, and it's so painful you'll never see them again. The other kind of people, they like the Dakar. It's a virus and they'll do everything in their life to go back again.”

The mission

The Dakar Rally has been run yearly since 1979. Originally an African rally, this is only the second year it's been held in South America, after terrorist threats caused the 2008 event to be cancelled. This year's route follows a 9,000-kilometre loop from Buenos Aires through Chile and back again over dirt tracks, towering dunes and gravel roads, with speeds reaching 150 km an hour. Each day consists of a timed section as long as 600 km, bracketed by connections as long as 400 km to get from the previous night's camp to the day's race course, and then to the night's rest stop. If you miss the start of the timed section or take too long to finish, you face disqualification. If you're delayed reaching the day's destination, there will be little time for rest and repairs. Only a handful of elite racers are vying for a podium spot; most are just trying to be among the 40 per cent or so who finish. You can follow Trahan's progress at dakartrahan.blogspot.com and dakar.com

The machine Trahan's 2009 Honda CRX450X has been modified with a carbon fibre and Kevlar fairing, engine guard and auxiliary gas tanks, reinforced Excel racing wheels, steel valves more durable than the original titanium, emergency water supply, a tool kit and electronics that include GPS tracking, telecommunications, emergency response and a warning system when faster vehicles are passing. A road-book holder carries a scroll of hieroglyphics that indicate the route. His team's support vehicle carries a bike's worth of spare parts and several fresh tires.

The inspiration

The pilot light was ignited at age 15, when Trahan was in Switzerland for a ski competition and a man clad in motocross gear arrived at the airport in the middle of winter. The rider had just dropped out of the Dakar , and his stories sparked something in the teenaged boy. In the years that followed, the Dakar flame grew into an unquenchable inferno.

The preparation

After his first unlikely rally finish in Morocco, he sought instruction and off-road riding opportunities. He entered the Dakar for the first time in 2000 and again in 2001, failing to finish both times due to crashes and breakdowns. Since then, he made two more unsuccessful attempts to enter the rally, and finished smaller rallies in Egypt, Dubai and Mexico. He's spent several years guiding off-road motorcycle tours in Morocco, Tunisia and Italy, and this past summer ran riding events for Honda Canada while arranging logistics and organizing more than $80,000 in funding and sponsorship to mount his latest Dakar challenge.

The sacrifices

The quixotic pursuit of a Dakar finish has led to personal bankruptcy and divorce from his wife of 14 years. “It was the Dakar or me,” she told him when he wanted to try the rally a second time. Now, the Dakar is his long-term relationship.

The risks

Forty-nine competitors have died in the rally's 30-year history, most of them on motorcycles. It's a statistic that weighs on Trahan's mother, whose husband and only other son died in a vehicle accident when Patrick was age seven. “When the Dakar is on TV,” he said, “she doesn't look.” Trahan is more worried about not finishing.

The rewards First prize isn't enough to cover the entry fee. Like climbing Everest or running with the bulls in Pamplona, the rewards come from overcoming fears and obstacles to achieve what few have even tried.

******

The other Canadians

Since the Dakar rally started in 1979, no one can recall a year with more Canadians entrants. These are the other Canucks who will be on the starting line in Buenos Aires tomorrow.

Rick Hatswell After keeping his riding habit a secret from his motorcycle-hating father for 10 years, Hatswell finally had a reason to come clean. The 34-year-old from Vancouver wanted the family's car repair chain to sponsor his Dakar dream. “This was the biggest challenge,” he says of the many obstacles he had to overcome to make it to the starting line. Follow him at roadtotherally.com and dakar.com

Dirk Kessler The seed was planted early for Kessler and after more than 20 years following the Dakar as a fan, he's entered for 2010 as a competitor. “If I keep on waiting, how old am I going to be before I'm too old to do it?” says the 44-year-old software developer from Vancouver, who now lives in San Francisco. “If I get the red shirt for the last guy who comes in, at least I made it.” Follow him at dakar.com

Donald Hatton Forget about finishing the Dakar, Hatton has had a hard enough time getting started. The 51-year-old motorcycle shop owner from Duncan, B.C., was ready for his first Dakar in 2008, when it was cancelled due to terrorist threats. He regrouped for the 2009 event, but crashed at 140 km/h on the fourth day, leaving him with a lacerated lung and multiple broken bones. After eight months of convalescence, his first training ride left him with a broken hand that needed surgery. When he recovered from that, he caught H1N1. But he isn't deterred. “Rallying has just gotten into my blood,” he says. Follow him at destinationdakar.ca and dakar.com

Andrew Scott Scott, a 43-year technical specialist for Barrick Gold, has been working out four to five hours a day to be strong enough for the gruelling event. “I've never done something quite this big,” says the Australian native, who's lived in Canada for six years, the last four in Toronto. “The butterflies aren't going away.” Follow him at ajsdakar.com and dakar.com

Christian Girouard

and Lawrence Hacking

Girouard had his mind set on entering the 2012 Dakar on a motorcycle, and sought advice from Hacking, the only Canadian to have finished the race. The two struck up a friendship, and Hacking soon asked the 38-year-old media relations manager from Ottawa to be his co-driver for 2010. Hacking finished the Dakar on his first attempt in 2001 on a motorcycle, and recently released a book about the feat: To Dakar and Back. Now the 55-year-old from Georgetown, Ont., is trying to repeat the feat in the car class. “You learn a lot about yourself when you attempt the Dakar,” says Hacking, who has a long involvement in off-road motorcycle racing and event organizing in Canada and currently runs World Enduro Canada. Follow the two at lawrencehacking.com and dakar.com.

As the city at the centre of the universe never tires

of boasting about itself: We're world-class jackasses

A 39-day outside workers strike; five professional sports teams that failed to make the play-on cut; a lame-schmuck mayor; a slew of cops charged, then de-charged, then re-charged; loopy judges who should be bench-pressed out of court and a cavalcade of hapless, hopeless crooks, though some even had MBAs.

Avid gamblers, we won and lost and often lost even when we won.

Our politicians taxed our patience, and just about everything else.

We had sex on the brain, of course, and, endlessly, online.

A lousy annum also for animals: Tasered, poisoned, electrocuted, raided, mummified and eaten by coyotes. Coyotes? Yes, critters of the wild increasingly encroached on the metropolis. But the really nasty creatures still walked on two legs. Herewith, a final backward glance at the 2009 Follies in Toronto and its ever-expanding environs. We crack me up.

Endorsed by Tiger Woods, though: TTC nixes "Life is Short, Have an Affair" pro-adultery ads on streetcars, but the advertiser's website gets megapublicity anyway.

Bare-Faced cheek: Playboy cover model Anissa Holmes of the GTA is kicked off Facebook for posting nude photos of herself and other sexually explicit material.

For whom the bill tolls: Seven years after her death, a Brampton woman is billed $178 by operators of private toll Hwy. 407.

Hoisted whilst heisting: A man trying to break into a Bloor St. business is left hanging upside-down for several hours after getting stuck in a basement window.

Policing under the influence of stupidity: Durham cops hand out $65 public intoxication tickets to a couple waiting outside a bar for their designated driver service.

Scratch 'n' stiffed: Ontario Lottery and Gaming Corp. settles with 27-year-old man after a printing error on scratch tickets led him to believe he'd won $135,000.

(pictured) apologizes for his "F - Toronto" slagging of the city's bungled funding application for new streetcars.

Travel
Where do we go from here? 2010's hot spots
967 words
31 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
T02
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

This time of year you can hardly swing a hamster without running into somebody's list of what's hot for the coming travel season. As my boss keeps telling me, people love lists.

There are good reasons almost every year for identifying certain countries or cities as hot spots. This coming year is more notable than many, however, what with Vancouver/Whistler hosting the Olympic and Paralympic Winter Games, Shanghai's Expo 2010 and the World Cup of soccer heading to South Africa for the first time. Toss in the once-a-decade return of the Passion Play in Oberammergau, Germany, and a couple of Latin American anniversaries, and you've got a pretty good gumbo boiling in the global pot.

Jerome Guidollet of www.travelalerts.ca[http://www.travelalerts.ca] says searches for all-inclusive, seven-day packages are at an all-time high, as people seek out deals for Cuba, the Dominican Republic and Mexico.

"Cuba is by far the big winner," said Guidollet. "Prices are really cheap and Cuba has a great variety of destinations, from Varadero to Holguin, Cayo Coco, Cayo Largo, Havana, Santa Clara and more."

In the D.R., he says Punta Cana has picked up steam and has become "the place to go" in the Dominican.

Guidolett says searches for Mexico are heavy and don't appear to have been influenced by the 2009 H1N1 scare.

The numbers are still small compared to the big three of Mexico, Cuba and the D.R., but he says Central American destinations such as Costa Rica and Panama are gaining ground. There were political problems this year, but Nicagarua appears to have settled down. And one major publication, Lonely Planet, recently singled out El Salvador as a place to check out.

Florida remains the favourite American getaway spot for many Torontonians. Things will pick up this spring when the new Harry Potter ride opens at Universal Studios in Orlando, while Busch Gardens will open the Sesame Street Safari of Fun. And Frommer's named the Florida panhandle beaches as one of its top 2010 destinations.

Guidollet says Las Vegas and Hawaii are the top spots for Western Canadian searches at travelalerts.

The Star's Richard Ouzounian was recently in Vegas to check out the new City Center development and the early reviews are outstanding as a destination that doesn't feel like traditional Vegas but still offers up great hotels, shops and restaurants.

Hawaii had a tough time in 2009, notwithstanding the election of Hawaii native Barack Obama to the U.S. presidency. But a recent survey by the Hawaii Visitors and Convention Bureau said the airline industry plans to add almost 500,000 more passenger seats on flights to the islands for 2010. A huge percentage of that, nearly 310,000 seats, are expected to come from Canada. It remains to be seen if U.S. destinations will be hurt by the new security rules brought in after the attempted bombing of an airplane over Detroit on Christmas Day. But it doesn't look as if air fares will be quite as attractive in 2010, what with rising fuel prices and general consolidation moves by the airlines.

"It doesn't mean you won't still see cheap fares but you'll have to shop more carefully to find the cheapest fares," Air Canada chief Calin Rovinescu said just before Christmas.

Cruising also should be big, with tons of new ships with wild features and plenty of good deals. Stuart MacDonald of www.tripharbour.ca[http://www.tripharbour.ca] says Caribbean and European sailings for 2010 are selling for less than $100 a day, and some for as little as $50 a day.

"And since the price of a cruise includes accomodation, meals, entertainment and transportation from the point of embarkation, it's far less expensive than a comparable vacation on land."

Keeping all that in mind, here's my Top 10 destinations for 2010 (in alphabetical order). We've got another 10 spots to watch on our website, www.thestar.com/travel[http://www.thestar.com/travel].

1. Antarctica: Not to be a climate change downer, but there appears to be an increase in interest from cruisers who want to see it while it lasts.

2. Colombia: The country is making a concerted public relations push. It has a wealth of geographic blessings, from towering green mountains to Caribbean and Pacific beaches.

3. Cuba: With the U.S. talking about finally opening up the border, this is an ideal time to take advantage of the generosity towards visiting Canadians.

4. Las Vegas: Tons of new hotels could spark another price war. Some rooms in December were going for as little as $14.

5. Germany: It's the 20th anniversary of reunification. It's also a year in which the Passion Play will be staged again.

6. Mexico: It's the 200th anniversary of independence. And the deals are strong as they try to deal with crime scares and the flu situation.

7. New Zealand: Filming could start soon on The Hobbit, which likely will spark tourism interest. Also, the kiwi dollar is down around 75 cents versus the loonie.

8. Shanghai: If the 2010 Expo, which opens May 15, is half as impressive as the Beijing Olympics, and you know they want to outdo their northern brethren, this will be special. A recent survey said China will edge out Spain for the third most popular global travel destination by 2020; putting it behind the U.S. and France.

9. South Africa: Stepping into the spotlight with the World Cup, starting in June.

10. Vancouver/Whistler: As much as B.C. will be front and centre in February, it'll be interesting to see if there's a boom later on.

The Spectator
Sports
Tiger, Tiger burning bright
Ian Hamilton
The Leader-Post
2743 words
31 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
C4
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

We've reached the end of 2009, which forever will be remembered as the year Tiger Woods' erratic driving -- off the course more than on -- caught up to him in a big way.

Seth Meyers, the host of Saturday Night Live's Weekend Update segment, may have put it best when he said: "Last week, Tiger Woods hit a tree and a bunch of women fell out."

After Woods had a minor traffic incident, women came out of the Woods-work to claim they'd had a fling with Tiger. It eventually reached the mid-teens -- that's the number of women, not their ages -- and Woods now faces the prospect of losing much of his fortune in a divorce settlement.

From Woods' "transgressions" to baseball stars being accused of using performance-enhancing drugs to disgraced NFLer Adam (Pacman) Jones pondering a stint in the CFL, there was something for everyone.

Here are some of The Spectator's favourite lines and quotes from 2009.

Lost in the Woods

- "Apparently when he told his wife, Elin Nordegren, he was going out to play a round, he was really going out to play around." -- The Spectator.

- "He must have really sweetened the deal. Elin is now third on the career money list." -- Dan Daly of the Washington Times, after Woods reportedly redid his prenuptial agreement with Nordegren in the hopes of convincing her to stay with him.

- "Even NBA players are saying, 'Dude, give it a rest.' " -- Comedy writer Alex Kaseberg, after he heard the list of women with whom Woods allegedly had flings had reached double digits.

- "That makes him a cereal eater and a serial dater." -- Gary Loewen of the Toronto Sun, after the London Sun reported Woods was sitting around watching cartoons and munching on cereal.

- "Woods was picked for his domination, performance and stamina. He didn't do too badly on the golf course, either." -- Comedy writer Jim Barach after the Associated Press named Woods its athlete-of-the-decade.

- "That seems fitting: Sweden is part of Scandalnavia, y'know." -- The Spectator, after reports suggested Woods and Nordegren were thinking of moving to her home country.

A Shot in the Dark

- "Suspicions were raised when he became the first player to hit for the menstrual cycle." -- Saskatoon StarPhoenix reader Jeff Funnekotter, after Los Angeles Dodgers slugger Manny Ramirez was hit with a 50-game suspension for taking female fertility drugs.

- "Had Manny Ramirez been traded to Minnesota during his suspension, then gone on a tear upon returning, the headline would have been: Manny Carrying Twins." -- Scott Ostler of the San Francisco Chronicle.

- "The crusade sounds like an exercise in fertility, uh, futility." -- The Spectator, after an online campaign was launched to put Ramirez onto the National League all-star team even though he was suspended.

- "Publishers are said to be trying to decide whether they would classify the book as fiction or non-fiction." -- David Thomas of the Fort Worth Star-Telegram, after hearing that pitcher Roger Clemens was thinking about writing a book to refute claims that he used performance-enhancing drugs.

- "Apparently they did, because the next day Kate tested positive for steroids." -- Kaseberg, after the New York Post's gossip page suggested Yankees third baseman Alex Rodriguez was spotted kissing actress Kate Hudson.

- "That explains the A he got in chemistry." -- The Spectator, after a book alleged A-Rod may have started using steroids in high school.

Diamond Mines

- "Welcome to today's economy, George Steinbrenner. Put in $201 million, get out $7,750." -- The Spectator, after a website estimated the melt value of the Yankees' World Series trophy.

- "Wendell is 41. And a joker. And, some might say (given the franchise's track record), also an optimist." -- Elliott Harris of the Chicago Sun-Times, after ex-Cubs reliever Turk Wendell -- asked by sportingnews.com to fill in the blank "When the Cubs win the World Series, I'll ..." -- replied "Be 99 years old."

- "On the bright side, though, the Nats just found a spray hitter for the meat of the lineup." -- Dwight Perry of the Seattle Times, after foil-wrapped hot dogs launched into the stands by Washington Nationals mascot Screech came apart in flight, raining chunks of wieners and buns onto fans.

- "Apparently, no one has had the heart to tell McCain that he's been Twittering with his garage door opener." -- Tonight Show host Conan O'Brien, after hearing that 72-year-old Republican presidential candidate John McCain was using Twitter in an attempt to get Arizona Diamondbacks slugger Mark Reynolds into baseball's all-star game.

- "On the other hand, there is no explanation why Sosa keeps saying Billie Jean is not his lover." -- Reggie Hayes of the Fort Wayne News-Sentinel, after a friend of Sammy Sosa said the former major-leaguer was undergoing a skin-cleansing process -- which explained why his face looked lighter in photos.

- "The culprits apparently did it without batting an eye." -- The Spectator, on reports that the employees of the cryonics company that froze the body of Boston Red Sox legend Ted Williams took swings at his disembodied head.

Blue Lines

- "Playoff hockey is not exactly hockey. It is hockey's older, snarling brother who just got out of prison." -- John Keating of Fox Sports Detroit.

- "That's good news for the Maple Leafs, who will have somewhere to watch the playoffs." -- The Spectator, after hearing that a new 30-foot-by-50-foot HD video screen was being erected on the exterior of the Air Canada Centre.

- "In keeping with Burke's nature as a hockey exec, let's assume: a) the meat will be tough; b) the buns will be crusty; c) there will be no room for any brats." -- Loewen, reporting on the opening of Burkie's Dog House -- a hot dog stand named in honour of Toronto Maple Leafs GM Brian Burke -- at the ACC.

- "This is like running into an ex-girlfriend a month after she dumps you. She's holding hands with a Tom Cruise-looking fighter pilot, and you're asking if they'd like fries with that." -- Robert Tychkowski of the Edmonton Sun, commenting on a visit to Edmonton by San Jose Sharks sniper Dany Heatley, who nixed a deal to Edmonton before being traded to the Sharks.

- "Once again, a group of men suffers the embarrassment of premature inoculation." -- The Spectator, after hearing the Vancouver Canucks had jumped the queue to get their H1N1 shots.

Gridiron Greats

- "They'll even have the Amish betting." -- Leader-Post political columnist Murray Mandryk, on what would happen in Pennsylvania if both the Pittsburgh Steelers and Philadelphia Eagles were to reach Super Bowl XLIII. (The Steelers got there; the Eagles didn't.)

- "Hide your beagle, Vick is an Eagle." -- The headline in The Trentonian of Trenton, N.J., after Philly signed Michael Vick, who had been in prison for funding a dogfighting operation.

- "From what I understand, he's the first player to win the Ed Block Award and the Cell Block Award in consecutive years." -- Daly, after Eagles players voted to give Vick the team's nomination for the Ed Block Courage Award.

- "Recently retired Brett Favre has waffled on intentions to tentatively cancel a news conference to announce plans to possibly change his mind." -- Greg Cote of the Miami Herald, commenting on the NFL quarterback's indecision about his career -- before Favre un-retired to join the Minnesota Vikings.

- "The Texans are thinking long and hard about the offer." -- The Spectator, after a company that deals in adult videos asked the NFL's Houston Texans if it could advertise on their practice jerseys.

- "They called us for everything but adultery." -- Montana Tech head coach Bob Green, after his team took 11 penalties for 111 yards during a game against Eastern Oregon.

- "If (Lingerie Football League) players are in skimpy outfits, what do the teams' cheerleaders wear?" -- The Spectator.

North of the Border

- "I wanted to go up to Jessica Simpson so bad and throw her my CFLPA card and say 'Hey, ditch the kid and come with me. I'm in the CFL, babe. Third-string defensive end.' But I didn't." -- Winnipeg Blue Bombers' Pat MacDonald, who once found himself at a party with Simpson and her then-boyfriend, Dallas Cowboys quarterback Tony Romo.

- "A question arises after watching Thursday's contest between the Winnipeg Blue Bombers and Edmonton Eskimos: Who has the weaker left arm, Edmonton tailback Jesse Lumsden or Winnipeg quarterback Stefan LeFors?" -- The Spectator, after Lumsden's wonky shoulder popped out in a game against the Bombers, who were quarterbacked at the time by the CFL's worst passer.

- "Somebody must have convinced him the North Pole was a strip club." -- A line in USA Today's "Game On!" section after the Bombers reportedly had signed troubled NFLer Adam (Pacman) Jones.

- "With a jailbird like Jones involved, shouldn't it be Pros vs. Cons?" -- The Spectator, on the news that Jones had accepted a spot on Spike TV's Pros vs. Joes reality show.

Court Cuts

- "(Signing with the Los Angeles Lakers) was a no-brainer, which is good, because I didn't finish college." -- Forward Ron Artest, explaining why he joined the defending NBA champions as a free agent.

- "Why is the Bryant jersey so popular in China? An hour after buying one, a fan feels like buying another." -- Mike Penner of the Los Angeles Times, after hearing reports that Lakers star Kobe Bryant had the top-selling replica jersey in China.

- "As part of the $700-million deal, the team will agree to become the New Jersey Nyets." -- Loewen, after it was announced that Mikhail Prokhorov, Russia's richest man, was negotiating to become a part-owner of the NBA team.

- "It's not like he can spend his days feeding pigeons at the park, because that would mean sitting on the bench." -- Detroit Free Press columnist Steve Schrader, after Allen Iverson said he was thinking about retiring because he wasn't happy in his role as a reserve with the Memphis Grizzlies.

The Top 10

- "It's fascinating seeing how players from other countries scratch themselves," "You'll be part of a cherished baseball tradition dating all the way back to 2006," and, "What else are you going to watch, hockey?" -- David Letterman's "Top 10 Reasons to Watch the World Baseball Classic," as read by pitcher Roy Oswalt, slugger Adam Dunn and shortstop Derek Jeter, respectively.

- "Cool! MapQuest found a route that's only five miles," "Why can I run 26 miles in less time than it takes to play a World Series game?" and, "I really hope that was Gatorade." -- Letterman's "Top 10 Thoughts That Go Through Your Mind When Running the New York City Marathon," as read by marathon winner Meb Keflezighi.

- "Spent the previous night with John Daly," "Would you practise if you had a hot Swedish wife?" and, "What do you expect? Y.E. Yang wins everything!" -- Letterman's "Top 10 Tiger Woods Excuses" after Woods lost the PGA Championship in stunning fashion.

- "Replaces your seven-iron with a garden weasel," "Says he needs weekends off," and, "Likes to pick up divots, put them on his head and say, 'Welcome to Celebrity Apprentice.' " -- Letterman's "Top 10 Signs Your Caddie Is Nuts," delivered during the U.S. Open.

Odds and Ends

- "Competitive eater Joey Chestnut ate 68 hot dogs in 10 minutes last weekend. By Monday, his bathroom had been declared a natural disaster area." -- Cote.

- "Parliament Hill in Ottawa should be the mecca for those who favour marijuana. After all, what sound does the bell in the Peace Tower make every time it tolls? Why, 'bong,' of course." -- The Spectator, after reports surfaced that Canadian Olympic snowboarder Ross Rebagliati -- who once was linked to weed -- was planning to run for the Liberals in a federal election.

- "Three members of the Nippon Ham Fighters baseball team are suffering from swine flu. Hopefully they will be cured soon." -- Loewen.

- "It's sad. That place cost Holyfield an arm and a leg and two ears." -- Ostler, after it was reported that boxing legend Evander Holyfield was facing foreclosure on his Atlanta mansion.

- "The test is fairly standard. She is handed a beer and a TV remote. They then toss a flower vase at her (and) if she drops either the remote or the beer to catch it, she is not a male." -- Kaseberg, describing the gender testing that was to be conducted on women's 800 metres world champion Caster Semenya.

- "Three riders in five weeks. They may have to change the horse's name to Paris Hilton." -- Janice Hough, after it was announced that someone other than Calvin Borel (who rode Mine That Bird in the Kentucky Derby) or Mike Smith (who was aboard in the Preakness) would have to ride the horse in the Belmont because those other two jockeys weren't available.

- "Injured cheerleaders looking to launch lawsuits reacted by saying they were getting a rah-rah deal. The jilted cheerleaders also will have to stick to their current mode of making money -- pyramid schemes." -- The Spectator, after the Wisconsin Supreme Court ruled that high school cheerleading is a contact sport, meaning participants can't be sued for accidentally injuring others.

Futbol Follies

- "Now that's effective Glock management." -- The Spectator, reacting to reports that two officials from the Brazilian side Portuguesa stormed into the team's locker room and berated the players while waving guns in the air following a 2-1 loss.

Last Words

- "I spent a lot of time on the floor with your mother." -- Exercise guru Jack LaLanne, 95, after he was told by Ostler that Ostler's mom used to watch LaLanne's televised exercise show every day in the '50s.
