SOUTH AMERICA

EX-ARCHBISHOP JAILED FOR ABUSE

BUENOS AIRES -- The Catholic former archbishop of Argentina's Santa Fe province has been sentenced to eight years in prison for "aggravated sexual abuse" against a seminarian, a court source said Wednesday.

Judge Maria Mascheroni said Edgardo Storni, now 73, took advantage of his access to and authority over the young man when the crime was committed in 1992, while he was archbishop.

Under Argentine law, Storni will serve out his sentence under house arrest because he is more than 70 years old.

NORTH AMERICA

MODERATE QUAKE JOLTS BORDER CITY

TIJUANA, Mexico -- A moderate earthquake of 5.9 magnitude rattled the Mexican city of Mexicali just south of the California border on Wednesday, the U.S. Geological Survey reported.

Mexican civil defence officials said they were investigating the quake but there were no immediate reports of damage or injuries.

Mexicali, east of the city of Tijuana, is home to a number of factories that assemble goods for export to the U.s.

EUROPE

TWO DIE IN POLISH TATRA AVALANCHE

WARSAW -- Two people were killed and three injured Wednesday in an avalanche in Poland's Tatra mountains, the country's rescue service said.

The service's chief, Jan Krzysztof, told the Polish news channel TVN24 that the three injured individuals were in hospital, one of them in critical condition.

The avalanche occurred in the 2,499-metre Rysy range, which draws large numbers of winter tourists. A similar accident there killed eight people in January 2003.

POLAND SEEKS HELP IN SIGN THEFT PROBE

WARSAW -- Poland said Wednesday it was poised to ask Sweden for judicial assistance in its probe of the theft of the infamous Nazi German "Arbeit Macht Frei" sign from the former Auschwitz death camp.

Polish Justice Minister Krzysztof Kwiatkowski spoke with his Swedish counterpart Beatrice Ask, the ministry said in a statement.

"Their discussion focused on a request for judicial assistance being drawn up by regional prosecutors in Krakow," the southern Polish city where the probe is being steered, it said.

The relatively rare step of involving ministers came "because of the importance of the case," it added.

Last week, Polish investigators said the mastermind of the Dec. 18 theft lived abroad.

But they have consistently refused to confirm media reports that the individual is in Sweden, that his or her name is known to Polish justice authorities, and that they plan to ask the Swedes for an arrest.

ALCOHOL INVOLVED IN BRIT'S DEATH

GENEVA -- The 23-year-old English tourist who fell to his death from a 100-metre high cliff, was under "strong influence of alcohol" at the time of the incident, Swiss local police said Wednesday.

Based on findings to date, investigators believe that the incident was likely to have been an accident.

"The young English tourist ... was under strong influence of alcohol at his time of death.

"He fell off a 100-metre high cliff, while he was on a steep and difficult terrain... He died due to his serious injuries," said police from the canton of Bern in a statement.

Autopsy results have shown no indication that a third party was involved in the incident, they added.

Police did not specify the name of the tourist, but local media had earlier identified him as Myles Robinson.

INDIAN ARRESTED FOR HUMAN TRAFFICKING

ROME -- Italian police have arrested an Indian man sought by Belgian authorities for alleged human trafficking and helping illegal migrants, an Italian newspaper reported Wednesday.

Pal Devinder, 32, who was being hunted on a European arrest warrant issued by Belgium, was picked up in northern Italy as he tried to leave a building with his bags packed, according to the Piacenza Sera paper's website.

AUSTRIA RELEASES WEAPONS SMUGGLER

VIENNA -- An Austrian court released on Wednesday a fugitive sought by the United States for supplying mustard gas ingredients to Iran in the 1980s, two days after his arrest in an Alpine ski resort ended 20 years on the run.

Peter Walaschek, a German citizen, had pleaded guilty in a U.S. court in 1988 of shipping to Iran 115 tonnes of thiodiglycol, a chemical used to make mustard gas, police said. Mustard gas was used during Iran's war with Iraq in the 1980s.

Walaschek fled the United States while awaiting sentencing. He was held for several months in Croatia in the mid-1990s but Croatian courts did not find his extradition justified under Croatian laws at the time, Austrian police said.

12,200 SWINE FLU DEATHS IN 2009

GENEVA -- Swine flu, which first emerged in Mexico in March 2009, has killed at least 12,220 people worldwide, the World Health Organisation said on Wednesday.

It said the Americas, especially the United States and Canada, has been the worst affected with at least 6,670 victims this year.

The WHO said that eastern and central Europe were the most active areas for transmission.

It said there had been at least 2,422 confirmed fatal cases in Europe.

AFRICA

NIGERIAN AIRPORTS TO GET 3D SCANNERS

LAGOS -- Nigeria is to install 3D body scanners at its international airports to step up security after a failed attack on a U.S. airliner by a Nigerian, civil aviation chief said on Wednesday.

"Nigeria will be upgrading its security screening system to 3D total body imaging scanner," Harold Demuren, director general of the Nigerian Civil Aviation Authority told reporters.

Nigerian Umar Farouk Abdulmutallab, 23, is accused of trying to blow up a Northwest Airlines Airbus as it was landing at Detroit on Dec. 25 from Amsterdam's Schiphol airport.

SIX DIE IN CONGO MINE COLLAPSE

LUBUMBASHI -- Six miners were killed and five others injured when a commercially abandoned mine collapsed in southeastern Democratic Republic of Congo, the mayor of the local town said.

The collapse occurred in a former copper mine of state company Gecamines, burying the clandestine miners.

"Six diggers have been killed and we count five injured, including one seriously," Charlotte Mabansime, the mayor of the mining town of Kolwezi, 300 kilometres northwest of provincial capital Lubumbashi.

31 PRISONERS IN SA JAILBREAK

JOHANNESBURG -- Thirty-one prisoners broke out of a South African police station overnight by sawing away the roof of their cell, a police spokesman said Wednesday.

"It is alleged that 31 suspects, most of them illegal miners, sawed their way out by cutting the secured metal roof of the ... cell" in the eastern town of Barberton, Captain Leonard Hlathi told AFP.

Five of the men were caught by police, who also arrested an accomplice, said Hlathi, who added that a manhunt had been launched for the other escapees.

MIDDLE EAST

SOMALI PIRATES FIRE AT OIL TANKER

KUALA LUMPUR -- Heavily armed Somali pirates fired on a Kuwaiti oil tanker near the Arabian Sea on Wednesday in an attempt to hijack the vessel, a global maritime watchdog said.

Noel Choong, head of the International Maritime Bureau piracy reporting centre in Kuala Lumpur, told AFP pirates armed with automatic weapons and rocket-propelled grenades launched the attack from a skiff off Somali waters.

"The Somali pirates chased the tanker and sprayed bullets early Wednesday in a bid to hijack the ship," he said.

"Fortunately, the tanker managed to escape the pirates," Choong said, adding there were no reports of injuries to the crew.

SUICIDE BOMBSKILL DOZENS

RAMADI, Iraq -- Twin suicide bombs killed at least 27 and wounded more than 100 in Iraq's Sunni Arab heartland on Wednesday and a roadside bomb killed seven pilgrims returning from a major Shi'ite Muslim religious festival.

The attacks underscored the tenacity of the insurgency despite a steep drop in overall violence and reflected Iraq's vulnerability as it prepares for national polls in March and for local troops to take over from U.S. forces.

U.S. forces flew Qassim Mohammed, governor of Anbar province west of the Iraqi capital, to Baghdad for treatment after he was wounded in the attacks outside the provincial government headquarters in Ramadi, Anbar's provincial capital.

Hospital and police sources said Sadoon Khraibit, a member of Anbar's governing council, and its deputy police chief were wounded in the attacks, which left charred cars, their chassis buckled from the explosion, and bloody wreckage on the street.

TALIBAN CLAIM PARADE ATTACK

PESHAWAR -- Pakistan's feared Taliban network claimed responsibility Wednesday for a suicide bombing that killed 43 people at a Shiite parade in Karachi and threatened further attacks.

The claim was made by one of Pakistan's most wanted commanders in the Tehreek-e-Taliban movement, which for two months has been targeted by a military offensive near the Afghan border.

Monday's attack in Pakistan's biggest city reduced to carnage events marking the Shiites' holiest day, Ashura, and sparked riots, underlining the security challenge faced by the nuclear-armed Muslim country.

Colour Photo: Ted Aljibe, Agence France-Presse, Getty Images / DANGEROUS TOURIST ATTRACTION: Motorbikes travel along a road with the backdrop of the Mayon volcano in the city of Legazpi in Albay province, 330 kilometres southeast of Manila on Wednesday. Thousands of tourists have been flocking to the Philippines' restive Mayon volcano with many even risking their lives to get close to the spectacular flowing lava, authorities said.

News
Anti-viral H1N1 kills
New Brunswick Telegraph-Journal
152 words
31 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A9
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

A 27-year-old New Brunswick man is the first in the province to die after contracting an antiviral-resistant strain of H1N1, health officials in the province say.

Michael Thomas Neill of Quispamsis died at the Saint John Regional Hospital on Monday. Neill's death is the eighth and youngest fatality associated with the H1N1 virus in the province, said Dr. Paul Van Buynder, New Brunswick's deputy chief medical health officer.

Neill was also the first person in the province to have an H1N1 virus resistant to Tamiflu, the most common antiviral used to fight the infection, Van Buynder said.

Drug-resistant cases of H1N1 are rare and have only occurred several dozen times across the world, Van Buynder said.

Neill had a history of asthma and pneumonia but did not get the H1N1 vaccine, his parents say.

H1N1 clinic to reopen
The Leader-Post
167 words
31 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A4
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

The Regina Qu'Appelle Health Region will reopen its mass H1N1 immunization clinic on Monday.

From Monday to Jan. 8, H1N1 shots will once again be available at the former Robert Usher Collegiate from 9 a.m. to 4 p.m. each day.

Those who wish to get the shot need their Saskatchewan health card and are encouraged to fill out a consent form (available at www.rqhealth.ca[http://www.rqhealth.ca]) in advance.

After Jan. 8 clinics will no longer be held at Usher and the shots will only be available at public health offices in Regina beginning Jan. 11. Clinics are offered on Tuesdays at the north office (204 Wascana St.) and Wednesdays at the east location (1911 Park St.).

Call 766-7700 ahead of time to book an appointment.

More information on locations, dates and times of clinics is available on the RQHR's immunization information line at 1-888-354-8111 or at the health region's website.

Issues & Ideas
How well do you remember the '00s?
National Post
2484 words
31 December 2009
National Post
FINP
National
A14
English
(c) 2009 National Post . All Rights Reserved.

ROGER HALL

So, how do you like the 21st century so far? The first 10 years have been, to say the least, a little rough -- terrorism, wars, natural calamities and financial collapse have marked their passage, yet that same period has also seen major advances in science, medicine and technology that hold potential benefits for all humanity. The following quiz chronicles this confusion and asks readers to dip into their memories to recall terrors and triumphs of the last 10 years.

Score one point for each correct answer, including for bonus questions. And no cheating by consulting that ubiquitous tool launched on Jan. 15, 2001. Not even an extra point for knowing it is called Wikipedia. Answers and scoring are on the following page.

2000

1. The decade was bracketed by two financial collapses, one in 2000 and one ongoing. What is the name of the bubble that burst in 2000?

2. An era of a different sort ended with the death of Charles M. Schulz on Feb. 13. What was his claim to fame?

3. Two submarines made news in August: In the United States the H.L. Hunley was raised after 136 years underwater and the K-141 Kursk sank mysteriously in the Barents Sea with a loss of 118 lives. To what respective navies did these ships belong?

4. On Nov. 7 the term "chad" entered the American political lexicon and became a lively issue. What was the occasion and exactly what was (or is) "chad"?

5. There are nights at the theatre and theatrical knights. Of the latter, which two bowed out in 2000?

(a) Sir John Geilguid

(b) SirAlecGuinness

(c) Sir Peter Ustinov

2001

1. Fill in the blank. The event of the year -- and probably the decade -- was 9/11, but additional international panic was triggered a week later when contaminated letters containing _______ were sent to news outlets in the United States.

2. In June a crown prince shot his father the king and several other members of the royal family before turning the gun on himself. In what country?

3. Financial chaos continued through the year and became mixed with corruption with the fall in December of what financial giant?

(a) Enron

(b) MontgomeryWard

(c) Hollinger

4. American Airlines Flight 11 and United Flight 175 crashed into the Twin Towers and American Flight 77 hit the Pentagon. But United Flight 93 went down in a field near Shanksville, Pa. What was its presumed target?

5. Match the following whose deaths occurred in 2001 with the phrases most associated with them.

(1) George Harrison

(2) Douglas Adams

(3) Ken Kesey

(4) Sir Fred Hoyle

(5) Mordecai Richler

(a) Hitchhiker

(b) My Sweet Lord

(c) Cuckoo's Nest

(d) St. Urbain

(e) Blackholes

2002

1. On Jan. 1 it was decre ed that after Feb. 28 it would be au revoir franc, auf wiedersehen deutschmark, adios peseta, etc. What was happening?

2. From Feb. 8-24 the Winter Olympics was held in:

a. Turin

b. Salt Lake City

c. Lillehammer

3. Although not a resident, she maintained a long-standing love affair with Canada which began when she first visited the country on the eve of the Second World War. She died to universal mourning on March 30. Who was she? ( Bonus point: What was her age?)

4. Turkish-born, he became world famous for his photographic portraits, of which this one was possibly the most iconic. Who was he?

5. On May 26, this robotic spaceship discovered ice on one of Earth's sister planet -- an event confirmed in 2008 with subsequent data. Where there is, or was, water there might have been life. What planet?

2003

1. Fill in the blank. On Feb. 1 the homeward-bound space shuttle _________ was destroyed during re-entry over Texas. The seven astronauts aboard were killed.

2. It is May Day, a time for celebration, and the banner behind a certain American politician proclaims "Mission Accomplished." What were the circumstances and who was the politician?

3. Though known for her culinary ability, this television figure was also accused of "cooking the books" and would end up in the clink for obstructing justice. Who was it?

4. An era that started in Nazi Germany came to an end on July 30 in a factory in Puebla, Mexico. What happened?

5. Gone but not forgotten. Match the photos of these prominent individuals who died during 2003 with their names and the phrases most associated with them:

(1) Leni Riefenstahl

(2) Bob Hope

(3) Carol Shields

(4) Sheb Wooley

(5) Fred Rogers

(a) Neighbourhood

(b) Purplepeople

(c) Willpower

(d) Diaries

(e) Memories

2004

1. First there was Juan and then there was White Juan. What are we talking about?

(a) Latin American singers and their racist copycats

(b) storms

(c) Legendary lover Don Juan and his albino brother

2. The marque, which was started in 1897, took its name from its founder. More than 35 million of the product were produced before the last one rolled out on April 29. What was it? ( Bonus point: give the title of the catchy song of 1905 that dramatically enhanced sales.)

3. On Oct. 5 a fire broke out on what Canadian navy ship resulting in the death of a crew member and raising suspicions about the quality of this class of vessel?

4. The day after Christmas saw the worst natural disaster in terms of human lives lost of modern times -- perhaps of all time. What was it?

5. Two days after her death on Aug. 8, the lights of New York's Empire State Building were dimmed for 15 minutes in her memory. Who was she and why would the lights have been extinguished in her honour?

2005

1. Facebook, Twitter and Google are all benchmarks of online history, but what popular Internet site specializing in video first went online on Feb. 15?

2. In February the season was cancelled -- the first time a labour dispute had caused a North American professional league to pack it in. What was the sport?

3. Near what Alberta town were four Mounties shot and killed on March 3?

4. Terrorist aftershocks of 9/11 continued on July 21 when 56 were killed in four separate explosions (three underground and one on a bus) in what city?

(a) Madrid

(b) Moscow

(c) London

5. On Aug. 29 nature again struck a savage blow when a storm killed at least 1,800 people in huge floods. Where did this occur? ( Clue: unscramble the letters to give the name of the storm -- TINRAKA)

2006

1. He was born 250 years ago -- on Jan. 27, 1756 -- but is more popular now than during his lifetime and is credited by many with invigorating plants and animals as much as human beings. Who is he?

2. March 17 marked the end of an era: The United States navy decommissioned the last two vessels of this type. What were they?

3. Their plan: alleged targets were the CN Tower, the Toronto Stock Exchange and the Toronto

CSIS office. Reports suggested they also wanted to storm Parliament in Ottawa and behead

PM Stephen Harper. Who were they?

4. During the dog days of summer (unsubtle hint) a certain sphere in the solar system was downgraded to "dwarf planet." Name it.

5. Unscramble the name of the former Middle Eastern leader who was executed on the penultimate day of the year: DADAMS NEISUHS

2007

1. Released on July 21, it sold 11 million copies in 23 hours. What was it?

2. Famous Toronto has-beens: On Sept. 13 of this year the CN tower was replaced as "the world's tallest free standing structure" by:

(a) Guangzhou TV and Sightseeing Tower in China

(b) Taipei101inTaiwan

(c) Burj Dubai in the United Arab Emirates

3. Robert Dziekanski was welcomed to Vancouver on Oct. 14 by being Tasered five times before dying by what police force?

4. Unscramble the name of the former Asian leader assassinated by a bomb blast on Dec. 27. RIZANEB OTTUHB

5. Match the following whose deaths occurred in 2007 with the word most closely assocated with them.

(1) Denny Doherty

(2) Bobby "Boris" Pickett

(3) Evel Knievel

(4) Ed Mirvish

(5) Oscar Peterson

(a) Mayhem

(b) Merchandise

(c) Music

(d) Mamas (and Papas)

(e) Monsters

2008

1. What was Northern Rock?

(a) Scottish rock band known for raunchy lyrics

(b) Britishmortgagebanknationalizedbecauseofbadinvestments in the sub-prime mortgage crisis

(c) A volatile Swedish Vodka

2. On Feb. 24 Cuba unanimously elected President Castro -- but what is his first name?

3. Many would argue Prime Minister Stephen Harper has much to apologize for -- what was the occasion for the public apology of June 11?

4. New words and phrases like "sub-prime," "housing bubble" and "shadow banks" gained currency because of the global recession which began in 2007. But the term "greed" has a more substantial explanatory power. Which of the following institutions collapsed in 2008?

(a) Lehman Brothers

(b) BearStearns

(c ) Wachovia

(d) Alloftheabove

5. On Dec. 5 DNA testing positively concluded that human remains found in 1991 were those of what European ruler assassinated in 1918?

2009

1. On Jan. 20 Barack Obama was inaugurated as the 44th President of the United States and is the first African American to hold that office. From what country do his African ancestors come?

2. It is called popularly -- if inaccurately, "Swine Flu" -- but H1N1 on June 11 was labelled a global pandemic -- the first to be awarded this designation since what outbreak?

3. Two American pop cultural figures died within hours of each other in June -- one after a long and public struggle with cancer and the other suddenly and unexpectedly. Who were they?

4. Fill in the blanks. "The deal had been struck in September 2008 but it wasn't until February 2009 that ___________ became Prime Minister of ____________ in a power-sharing arrangement with President ___________.

5. What global undertaking, which occurred in December 2009 with much fanfare and promise but little concrete result, has been called "Emission Impossible"?

SPECIAL BONUS QUESTION

Is the world a better place than a century ago? The 20th century was the bloodiest and most destructive on record. And that first decade was also marked by its share of terrorism, wars, natural calamities and financial collapse as well as more sanguine events. It would seem, as the American playwright Tennessee Williams said, "Humanity is a work in progress" -- and progress is slow. Put the following events in chronological order by year starting with 1900.

1. Death of Queen Victoria

2. Russo-Japanese War

3. Model "T" Ford introduced

4. Sigmund Freud publishes The Interpretation of Dreams

5. Baden-Powell forms the Boy Scouts

6. Austria annexes Bosnia and Herzogovina

7. Entente Cordiale between Britain and France

8. John Galsworthy publishes The Man of Property

9. Treaty of Vereniging ends Boer War

10. Mutiny on Potemkin and aborted revolution in Russia

---

ANSWERS

1. Dot-com bubble

2. He drew Peanuts, the comic-strip, from 1950 until his death

3. The Kursk was in the Russian Navy whereas CSS H.L. Hunley belonged to the Confederate States of America

4. "Chad" were bits, in various stages of attachment, left by a voting machine punching ballot paper. During the Florida recount in the American election they were assessed as "hanging," "swinging" or "dimpled," interpretations that were instrumental in awarding George W. Bush the American presidency over Al Gore.

5. a. and b. Ustinov died in 2004

2001

1. anthrax

2. Nepal

3. a.

4. Either the U.S. Capitol Building or the White House

5. 1. b., 2. a., 3. c., 4. e., 5. d.

Youth Editorial Board
EDITORIAL
Risks of H1N1 vaccine have been exaggerated
Connor Henderson
550 words
30 December 2009
Waterloo Region Record
TKWR
Final
A11
English
Copyright (c) 2009 Kitchener-Waterloo Record.

I have heard many people saying that they don't want to get the H1N1 vaccine because of the risks involved with it.

These risks include allergic reactions with some of the ingredients in the vaccine or physical soreness from the shot.

Many of these fears stem from the stories we, now regularly, hear in the media about people who have had reactions and are now sick. There's even a Facebook group supporting the idea.

What we don't as regularly hear about is that the majority of the people who get the shot don't have anything happen to them. Remember all those long lines people were complaining about when the vaccine was first released? Compared to those stories, you probably have only heard of a very small number of people who have experienced negative reactions to the shots.

A few weeks ago I went to get the vaccination with my family. The biggest reaction any of us had was soreness the next day. The doctor's office we went to didn't see any reactions to the shot since the office started offering it. Honestly, it barely hurt more than a regular flu shot. The comfort from knowing that I most likely wasn't going to get the virus more than offset that little amount of pain.

In my opinion, everyone should get the shot, even if you aren't in the problem zone. All the ideas and paranoia that you will get about receiving the shot should be expelled. The only thing that they succeed in doing is causing fewer people to get the shot, further advancing the intensity of the virus.

Yes, there is a small chance that you could have a reaction, but the chances that you won't are much higher, and the chances that you will get the virus if you don't get the shot are also higher.

The virus by the way is a killer, let's not forget. So your options are getting the shot and having the small chance of getting ill but not dying, or not getting the shot and possibly getting the virus and dying.

So basically, the pros outweigh the cons. If everyone were to get this shot it could keep a lot of people from dying by halting the disease's progression and evolution. And wasn't that the main goal the government first had when releasing this vaccination to the public?

That is why you should stop thinking now, weighing the decision and just go out and get the shot before the new yearwhen you'll probably have less chance of going out and getting it and probably feel more reluctant to go anyway.

There is also speculation among the medical community that we could see another surge of H1N1 cases in January. Just remember as you go out and get the shot , you may be saving your own life and others.

Also that any soreness is worth the small risk of actually getting a reaction.

Or you could not go and get it done and take the risk of getting the virus and possibly dying. I know what I would do but, hey, I guess it's your choice.

L. Ian MacDonald
Editorial / Op-Ed
Harper is the political player of the decade; PM has had a few stumbles, but he's grown into the job
L. IAN MACDONALD
Freelance
927 words
30 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A21
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

There's no doubt that Stephen Harper is the player of the year in Canadian politics, and a very strong case can also be made that he's the player of the decade.

And this is a guy who, as the decade began, was out of politics, working as a lobbyist as head of the National Citizens' Coalition. Harper, who always saw himself as more of a backstage strategist, went on to become the leading political actor of the decade.

Consider: When Stockwell Day invited a challenge to his leadership of the Canadian Alliance in 2002, Harper stepped in where no one else would - and prevailed.

Then in 2003, Harper and Progressive Conservative leader Peter MacKay successfully completed a merger that united the right, a prerequisite for winning an election. More than a united right, Harper also got the PCs' most important asset - the Conservative brand, the one Canadian voters always turn to when they get tired of the Liberals.

That was the moment, just as Paul Martin was becoming Liberal leader, that a fourth consecutive majority slipped away from the Grits.

In the 2004 election, only a late surge of strategic voters to the Liberals saved Martin a minority government. It was also a good lesson for Harper. His team wasn't ready to govern, and the voters sensed it in the closing days of the campaign, in which the Conservatives allowed the Liberals to define their leader as a scary guy with a hidden agenda. It wouldn't happen again.

In 2005, the Conservatives staged a big policy convention in Montreal, where they took a lot of scary stuff like abortion, and Reform-era initiatives like recall of MPs, off the table.

In the 2006 election, Harper actually went into the campaign behind, but over the course of eight weeks, proved the point that campaigns matter. He announced five priorities for government - including a health-care guarantee and child-care allowances for parents - that got the attention of the middle class. In Quebec, his "open federalism" speech just before the holidays gave Quebecers a respectable alternative to the Liberals and the Bloc. It was no accident that his breakthrough in the polls in Quebec, resulting in 10 seats, occurred after Quebecers had discussed it over the Christmas holidays.

In 2008, Harper had only himself to blame for a missed rendezvous with a majority, as well as for the self-inflicted parliamentary crisis that nearly cost him his government.

But in 2009 he has been the Comeback Kid, thoroughly dominating politics and public policy on the federal stage. Aside from an unforced error by his office, when it stupidly suggested Brian Mulroney was no longer a member of a party he twice led to massive majorities, Harper has enjoyed a rise in the polls that has taken him once more to the threshold of majority territory.

Of course, he has also been fortunate in his opponents, first with Stéphane Dion in the 2008 election, and now with Michael Ignatieff giving a very good impersonation of Hamlet. To be or not to be in an election, that was the question for Iggy.

First Ignatieff said he had the government on a short leash, demanding quarterly updates as a condition of supporting the January budget. That only allowed Finance Minister Jim Flaherty to put out a quarterly statement, complete with graphs and charts, on what a great job the government was doing steering the economy through the recession to recovery.

Then Ignatieff famously said, "Mr. Harper, your time is up," a foolish declaration that left him no room to climb down except in the humbling way he finally did, taking it all back. Now his position is that there's no way he'll force an election in 2010. There doesn't seem to be much of a middle ground in Ignatieff's world, only extremes. Meanwhile, he has handed Jack Layton the balance of power, and the NDP leader, who enjoys being visible and relevant, is coming off his most effective session in the House.

Ignatieff can take some comfort in that a year ago Harper was in a bad place. Flaherty's listening tour, and his January budget, were instrumental in saving the government and reversing the political course of the year.

Subsequently, the government's message was twofold - first a steady hand on the wheel through the recession, and then staying in Ottawa to run the country's affairs through to an economic recovery. The core attribute of government is competence and the government has passed it, from the GM bailout to the H1N1 rollout.

But as the year and the decade end, the biggest change has been in Harper himself. From the February bilateral meeting with Barack Obama to a series of international summits, Harper has developed a comfort zone on the world stage, a role he will grow into more next year as he hosts the G8 and G20 summits.

At home, in the House and on the hustings, he has grown into the role, as well as the job, of prime minister.

All of which makes him Canada's political player of the year and, indeed, the decade.

Anti-Liepert site must go: province
Archie Mfilean
Edmonton Journal

30 December 2009
Edmonton Journal

English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

The legislature speaker's office has ordered the Alberta Liberals to take down a website calling for Health Minister Ron Liepert's firing or resignation.

Speaker Ken Kowalski said the site " liepertmustgo.ca"is an inappropriate use of caucus funding, since it singles out one MLA for personal criticism.

"It is an important function for any caucus to communicate its positions on policy, including providing criticism of government policies," Kowalski said in a letter sent last week to the Liberals. "However, this communication goes beyond what is acceptable as it singles out and personally criticizes an individual member."

But the Liberals argue the website is no more personal than attacks in the legislature or in news releases. "We're not attacking anything personal about (Liepert), only his job performance and the direction he is taking," Liberal Leader David Swann said.

Liepert and Kowalski both declined to comment on the issue.

Premier Ed Stelmach has confirmed there will be a cabinet shuffle in the coming weeks and much speculation has centred on Liepert.

The Liberals took aim at Liepert during the fall sitting of the legislature and demanded his resignation over his handling of the H1N1 vaccination campaign and what they say is turmoil in the health care system.

The site was launched this month as a way for Albertans to share stories about the health care system.

Swann made no apologies for the harder edge to the party's communications. The Liberals say they will take their concerns to the member services committee, which has final say on such issues.

The Liberals could host the website from their parity site, which receives no public funding. But Swann said they're fighting on principle.

"Anything the government can do to suppress dissent and criticism is fair game for them," Swann said. "We're trying to give Albertans a voice."

Sports
Hockey community there for Brodie; Money being raised to help family of boy with cancer
Chris Yzerman
The Ottawa Citizen
600 words
30 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
B7
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

It's the most important assist anyone will make this season in the Kanata Minor Hockey Association.

After 13-year-old Brodie Rafter was diagnosed with Hodgkin's lymphoma in the fall, his Kanata Blazers bantam minor B team and the KMHA decided to lend a hand to help out Brodie and his Glen Cairn family while the defenceman undergoes treatment for the disease.

"When we found out, the team was really worried about his health and wanted to do what they could to help him and his family get better," Blazers coach Rob Levesque said. "He's a great kid and always has a smile on his face. He's very serious about his sports -- his hockey and his baseball.

"We're waiting for him to get better so he can get back on the ice with us."

Brodie, who competed in the Bell Capital Cup last year, had been playing with the Blazers in November when struck with a lingering illness. By the end of the month, he'd been diagnosed.

"At first, they thought it was the flu, with H1N1 going around, but he wasn't getting any better," said Dave Farnes, the KMHA's AA league rep.

Despite the devastating news, Levesque said the bravery, strength and sense of leadership Brodie showed in his own family following the diagnosis made him a natural choice for team captain because the team had yet to elect a captain.

The Blazers had the C put on his jersey, and it has hung behind the team bench for every game.

"Looking at that type of attitude was a really good source of inspiration for our team," Levesque said.

Brodie's teammates presented him with an iPod so he could stay connected while he undergoes chemotherapy at the Children's Hospital of Eastern Ontario.

His mother, Tracey, was forced to give up the day-care facility she operated in order to look after Brodie, whose chemotherapy treatments last until spring, when he'll begin further radiation therapy.

"It's a pretty aggressive treatment," Farnes said.

In the meantime, the Kanata hockey community is pitching in to help out Brodie, Tracey, his father, Scott, and older brother Kyle.

KMHA president Charles Saunders declared the week of Jan. 10 Blazers for Brodie Week.

The Kanata Stallions of the Central Junior Hockey League will donate all gate receipts from their Jan. 12 game against Pembroke at the Kanata Recreation Complex to the family, the same day that A&B Sports will donate all of its skate-sharpening proceeds.

The association's Winter Classic tournament at the Bell Sensplex Jan. 15-17 will also feature various fundraisers. A Blazers for Brodie T-shirt and wristband are in the works and the KMHA is also accepting donations for the family.

The Ottawa Senators donated 25 pairs of 200-level tickets for Thursday night's game against the New York Islanders for the association to sell.

As of Tuesday, nine pairs of the tickets, with a face value of $228, were still available for $150, or $75 for singles, with all the money going to the Rafters. Tickets can be arranged through Bonnie MacLean at the.macleans@rogers.com .

"I'm optimistic," Farnes said. "(But) we're looking at a bit of a road getting through the treatment and then rest, and getting him back on his feet."

Document OTCT000020091230e5cu0001n


HEALTH CARE
Report on Business: International
FLU INC. How vaccines became big business; Making flu vaccines used to be a money-losing business. But that all changed with a flu pandemic on the other side of the world. Inoculating the global population against a growing list of illnesses has since spawned a $24-billion market for a few pharmaceutical companies. Now the trick isn't wringing profits from vaccinations, it's meeting the demands of people and countries fearful of new strains of influenza
PAUL WALDIE AND GRANT ROBERTSON
3993 words
30 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
B1
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

It was not the talk Dr. Michael Ossi planned to give when he was summoned to Washington in the fall of 2003 to brief health officials on Capitol Hill.

Dr. Ossi, an infectious disease expert with British pharmaceutical giant GlaxoSmithKline PLC, had been asked to meet with government officials on how to respond to a deadly problem unfolding halfway around the world.

In Asia, entire flocks of birds were dying from a virus called H5N1, better known as avian flu. A particularly virulent strain of influenza, H5N1 had for years been confined mostly to birds because their higher body temperatures provided an ideal environment for the bug to proliferate.

But pockets of people were becoming infected in Asia, setting off alarm bells for health officials around the globe. When it spread among humans, the consequences were unusually fatal. Roughly 60 per cent of those infected with H5N1 died, making it three times deadlier than the 1919 Spanish Flu, according to the World Health Organization.

Dr. Ossi came to the hastily arranged gathering of health officials and academics expecting to talk to them about his company's research into anti-viral drugs and flu vaccine. But the health experts clustered around a handful of tables were not interested in hearing about the science behind such products. They had much more pressing concerns.

They only had one question, Dr. Ossi recalls: “How much can you make and how fast can you make it?”

It was a watershed moment for him. For most of his career, working in vaccine research – indeed, most research related to the prevention and treatment of infectious diseases – was a ticket to obscurity at a big drug company. The high stakes and big profits for companies like Glaxo were not in vaccines, but in multibillion-dollar blockbuster drugs: the Wellbutrins, the Zantacs and the Valtrexes.

But the world was changing. Shoehorned into that meeting room in Washington were mathematicians from top universities, charged with calculating how many people could be protected in a pandemic; ethicists who would debate who should get medicine first in the event of a shortage; and public health officials struggling to find a way to cope with potentially tens of millions of infected people. Their urgency made it clear to Dr. Ossi that the quiet, unprofitable world of vaccines was about to be given more prominence in government than it had in decades.

H5N1 would alter government approaches to pandemic planning. But it would also create a new and unprecedented opportunity for the global pharmaceutical industry. It was, as Dr. Ossi recalls, “an obvious commercial opportunity” for the drug companies – one that is reshaping their businesses.

In a matter of a few years, flu shots have gone from being a marginal, money-losing business to a massive profit generator for a small number of global companies, as governments and the public hasten to protect themselves from getting sick.

Between 2004 and 2007, vaccine sales across the industry soared an average of 32 per cent each year, with flu vaccine leading the way. That is roughly four times faster than any other pharmaceutical product.

In a year that will be remembered for widespread public worry about the H1N1 virus, or swine flu, vaccines have become a $24-billion business. Analysts predict the global vaccine industry will top $40-billion by 2012. For companies like Glaxo, Sanofi-Aventis, Merck & Co., Novartis AG and Pfizer Inc., the fear of a pandemic has translated into a financial windfall that has been years in the making. Worldwide, nearly 1 billion doses of H1N1 vaccine have been ordered in 2009.

This is the story of how that happened – how Flu Inc. grew out of nowhere, transforming a once struggling business characterized by lab closures and lawsuits into a high-profit industry in less than a decade, and of the steps the pharmaceutical industry has taken to ensure the dollars keep flowing.

The change is driven by a new way of thinking in government about how to approach future threats of a flu pandemic. Health officials have begun to see merit in pursuing a strategy of stockpiling vaccines, even at a much higher cost per dose than they paid in the past.

Behind all this, of course, is a public that demands to be protected from the worst strains of influenza. This year, about 40 per cent of Canadians got the vaccine for H1N1 – the highest adoption of a flu shot the country has ever seen. Those shots are responsible for limiting the spread of the disease and, most likely, for saving lives. But even as the H1N1 threat subsides after killing more than 400 people in Canada, the business of fighting flu is arguably still in the early years of its growth.

“It was a new market, created by this pandemic scare,” Dr. Ossi said.

How the flu vaccine

industry was built

Vaccines have always been a business. They've just never been a very good business.

“We were losing money,” said Claude Vezeau, former chief executive officer at Montreal-based IAF BioChem International, Canada's largest maker of vaccines in the 1990s. “All vaccines were losing money.”

The problem has been rooted in the cumbersome process of vaccine production and in the public's complacency. Alan Davies, the head of Canada's famed Connaught Antitoxin Laboratory at the University of Toronto, once lamented: “People are willing to pay more for a bottle of headache tablets than they are for a vaccine that will protect them for many years.” Connaught, too, was a money-loser.

The idea of inoculating people against disease dates back to 1796, when British doctor Edward Jenner noticed that milkmaids exposed to cow pox appeared to be immune from smallpox.

More than two centuries later, making vaccines on a large scale remains challenging. Vaccines are still made primarily from tiny bits of pathogens, the disease-causing agents of a virus. It can take months to get the formula right, and flu viruses are always changing. The manufacturing process is also clunky and relies on techniques developed 50 years ago in which a virus is injected into chicken eggs to multiply.

Heavy regulation and a lack of a broad customer base – governments are the primary buyers – left many drug companies with little incentive to make vaccines. Why spend time on such a low-margin business as vaccines when a company could make a fortune developing a new blockbuster drug?

In Canada, where vaccine making was once largely left to public institutions, the picture was no brighter. The country's largest facility, the Connaught lab, earned fame as the site of Frederick Banting and Charles Best's insulin discovery. But it struggled financially for years before being bought by French drug giant Sanofi-Aventis.

Canada's other large vaccine facility, the Institute Armand-Frappier in Quebec City, didn't fare much better. By the mid 1980s, its owner, the University of Quebec, could no longer shoulder the financial burden, and the facility was sold to Mr. Vezeau's IAF BioChem – which also struggled.

How the world changed for vaccines

But all of that began to change with the first hint of a pandemic on the other side of the world.

The outbreak of H5N1 in Hong Kong in 1997 was relatively small – just 18 people who handled birds – but six of them died. Such a high death rate drew immediate attention from medical experts around the world. Fears of a much bigger death toll began to percolate at government levels.

Like many countries, Canada began working on a pandemic preparedness plan. Led by John Spika, a senior infectious disease official at Health Canada, federal and provincial bureaucrats began drawing up models of a potential outbreak that could kill up to 58,000 Canadians and do $30-billion worth of damage to the economy. The key to fighting such an outbreak, they believed, was securing an abundant supply of vaccine.

Dr. Spika and others in the vaccine community warned that if a serious pandemic broke out, countries would close their borders and hoard vaccine. Governments began setting aside money in federal budgets for pandemic preparations. Ottawa also began negotiating a long-term vaccine supply agreement with BioChem, which had the only flu vaccine facility in Canada.

For Mr. Vezeau at BioChem, pandemic planning was a godsend. Suddenly, he had the attention of government officials. “We were losing money, so we went to the government and said, ‘Listen, for this to be a viable manufacturer, we need to increase the price of our vaccine,” Mr. Vezeau said. They got a higher price.

The government pursued contract negotiations with BioChem on a deal that was potentially so lucrative it began to push the value of the company higher. Before it was even signed, Shire Biologics stepped in and purchased BioChem in 2001. Soon after, the company signed a 10-year pact with Ottawa that would see the Quebec facility responsible for producing flu shots for every Canadian in a pandemic.

Dr. Spika called the deal “a cheap insurance policy” for the country. At a price tag of $300-million, it was more money than the vaccine maker had ever seen before.

Other vaccine companies began to negotiate similar large-scale contracts in dozens of countries, from Switzerland to France and even such smaller nations as Iceland.

However, one problem remained for the vaccine manufacturers as governments around the world began doling out money for contracts. Like any virus, flu will only grow in living cells. One of the best places to grow it for the purposes of vaccine making is in fertilized chicken eggs. But the process is slow and hard to use on a mass scale; it can take as many as three eggs to make one shot of flu vaccine. “They have to use chicken embryos and there isn't enough supply of those readily available to be able to make tons of vaccine,” Dr. Ossi said.

Fighting pandemic flu with vaccine made from eggs alone would be a losing battle, many believed. Companies needed to find a way to increase the amount of vaccine that could be produced in order to capitalize on the growth. And that particular discovery was already in the works.

The problem with

making vaccines

For years, scientists had tried to find a faster way to make vaccines. They chased a variety of theories, including isolating the DNA of a virus, which many researchers believed would unlock new ways to fight infections. But at its main vaccine facility in Rixensart, Belgium, Glaxo had found a way to make vaccines more potent using another kind of technology: adjuvants.

Adjuvants are like superchargers for vaccines. They are mild contaminants, such as salt or oil, that cause the body to respond with a more intense immune response. When paired with antigens, the adjuvant liquid can make the vaccine's impact stronger. This allows for more doses to be produced from less antigen.

“It allows us to decrease the antigen content, which allows us to multiply the capacity,” said Philippe Monteyne, senior vice-president of global vaccines development at Glaxo. “And of course, multiplying the capacity has some impacts on the business side,” boosting profits.

Adjuvants allowed companies to pump out more, but it is also a higher-margin business than antigens.

Significantly more than half the price of a dose of flu vaccine is attributable to the adjuvant, though Glaxo doesn't disclose the exact figures. “That's why vaccines became so attractive,” Mr. Monteyne said. “Most of the value in our case is put on the adjuvant technology.”

For the drug companies, the new interest in vaccines by governments looking to get as many flu shots as they could buy, and the scientific advances of adjuvants, came at an opportune time.

Many drug makers were starting to worry about the long-term viability of megadrugs like Lipitor, a cholesterol fighter, and Zantac, an ulcer treatment, that have a finite period of patent protection. When the patents expire, the market is flooded with cheaper generic versions. The big drug companies needed a new source of revenue, and the advent of large-scale vaccine manufacturing looked promising.

With the value of vaccines on the rise with the fears of avian flu, the drug companies also liked something else about the resurgent business: they had it to themselves.

Because vaccine making is so expensive – a new plant can cost up to $1-billion (U.S.) – companies like Glaxo, Novartis and Sanofi-Aventis didn't have to worry about a rash of new entrants cutting into the flu vaccine business as governments ordered millions of doses.

“The barriers to enter the market are extremely high,” said Mr. Monteyne in Belgium. “You don't become a vaccine maker over night. That's why we have a few big players, and very few only.” That meant the giants could push hard to increase prices. And they did.

Sanofi-Aventis expects to earn close to $6-billion (U.S.) in vaccine revenue next year and double its sales by 2013. This quarter, sales of H1N1 vaccine alone will top $500-million. It is suddenly a good time to be a flu shot maker.

“Vaccines, vaccines, wonderful business,” Chris Viehbacher, the Canadian-born CEO of Sanofi-Aventis, told investment analysts on a conference call a few months ago.

It didn't take long for such calculations to be made in other boardrooms around the globe. The companies began acting quickly to expand: Novartis spent $5-billion to buy U.S.-based flu vaccine maker Chrion. Britain's Astra Zeneca paid $15-billion for Medimmune, and Glaxo purchased the old BioChem vaccine operation in Quebec for $1.4-billion.

With 22 per cent of the market, Glaxo was now the global leader in vaccines. And with that clout came the power to influence governments, who were already fearful about not having enough vaccine supply in the event of a pandemic.

How vaccines are sold and prices are set

In the summer of 2008, Jim Flaherty's staff prepared a briefing note for an upcoming meeting with the head of Glaxo's Canadian operations, Paul Lucas.

Glaxo had been lobbying several governments around the world to get higher vaccine prices and access to massive cash reserves countries were setting aside to cope with a pandemic threat.

“We understand that Mr. Lucas would like to discuss how GSK could further contribute to Canada's pandemic preparedness, including [Ottawa] setting aside $400-million as a contingency fund,” the note said, according to documents obtained by Ottawa researcher Ken Rubin.

Mr. Flaherty held the purse strings on any national pandemic plan and subsequent vaccine purchase from Glaxo. The company had a message for the minister: “GSK has been critical … contending that the proposed vaccine price is too low,” Mr. Flaherty's staff told him.

Indeed, the company had already been lobbying governments well before then. Since the late 1990s, prices for flu vaccine in North America have soared from $2 per dose to as high as $12 in 2007. The price has recently fallen back to about $8 as buying volumes increased in the face of H1N1. But that's still a healthy margin, as some analysts estimate it costs about $1 to make each dose.

The company does not discuss its costs, but Mr. Monteyne said the cost of a flu shot is flexible depending on whether the buyer can pay more. “We have a tiered pricing strategy,” Mr. Monteyne said. “It is mainly based on the level of income of the country.”

Beyond just selling crates of vaccine, Glaxo also wants to sell full-service protection – pandemic readiness packages. And the industry's desire to build a stable business out of vaccines, along with the emergence of adjuvants, has led to perhaps the most significant shift the industry has seen in decades: the creation of vaccine stockpiles.

Switzerland was the first country to jump in. In October, 2006, with fear over H5N1 at fever pitch, the Swiss signed a contract with Glaxo on a stockpiling deal that called for 8 million doses of avian flu vaccine, slightly more than one shot for every citizen.

Then, in a new kind of move for the industry, the Swiss reserved future space on the production line for another 8 million doses if needed. That meant Swiss health authorities had priority in line at Glaxo's factories if a second dose were needed.

This emerging business – pre-pandemic treatment – was rounding into shape. Glaxo began trade-marking the names of vaccines along those lines, registering its vaccines as Prepandrix.

Other nations soon followed the Swiss with deals of their own, locking up huge sales for Glaxo.

There was just one problem: the H5N1 pandemic never happened. The virus stayed mostly with animals. The Swiss were left with one of the world's largest stockpiles of unused H5N1 flu vaccine. Glaxo's sales of avian flu vaccine fell 54 per cent in 2008, as countries realized their stockpiles weren't needed.

This was a problem for the vaccine makers, who were looking to build an enduring revenue stream rather than simply capitalizing on flu fears to gain a temporary spike in sales. Glaxo's new CEO, who had been elevated from the ranks of the company's vaccine division, understood this.

How the new vaccine market was born

Barely a year into his tenure as Glaxo's chief executive officer, Andrew Witty outlined the company's achievements during a conference call in October. Quarterly profit was up 30 per cent from a year earlier, orders for H1N1 flu vaccines had topped 400 million doses and the company was close to becoming the world's largest vaccine maker.

“I think the momentum of this business is going in the right direction,” Mr. Witty told analysts. In short, Glaxo's big bet on vaccines was bearing fruit. And perhaps most surprisingly, the Swiss were buying again, despite having spent millions on H5N1 vaccine it never used.

This, Mr. Witty explained, was the brilliance of the new vaccine market. Glaxo had turned the Swiss situation into an opportunity to sell more vaccine.

Because adjuvant could be paired with any antigen, the leftover stockpiles could be used for other outbreaks by simply plugging in a different antigen, depending on which virus looked most threatening.

Once a country bought a large supply of adjuvant, it was locked in as a buyer for Glaxo's antigen for years to come. Countries were not just vaccine buyers now; they were subscribers, coming back annually to the company for more and different types of shots.

“If you go with an adjuvanted technology, you can actually keep a stockpile of your adjuvant and then simply rotate a new antigen in at the last minute,” Mr. Witty said.

That is basically what the leading countries like Switzerland did.

“The real business is a business of stockpiling. It is not a reactive market. It is something that is proactively being built with governments,” Mr. Monteyne said.

Other countries, including Canada, took notice of what the Swiss were doing. In the past 12 months, the number of countries using such stockpiling methods has grown to 60 from less than 10. When H1N1 broke out and temporary vaccine shortages occurred, Canadians learned that the federal government has become a big buyer of adjuvants.

As far as the vaccine makers are concerned, adjuvants are the future because they encourage countries to stockpile what they've bought from the drug companies. Asked by an analyst whether the company could keep generating the record vaccine profits it saw in 2009, Mr. Witty didn't hesitate: Yes, it could.

“What's been created this summer is essentially a stockpiling marketplace, which over a period of time will have to be refreshed,” Mr. Witty told analysts. “And that's exactly where we see there being some kind of steady state” for the vaccine industry.

New fears over H1N1 were the catalyst to a completely new way of buying and selling vaccines that many countries are expected to follow. Flu shots are now a product for the masses. Non-seasonal flu shots such as H1N1 could become a yearly norm.

Soaring vaccine sales are also pushing companies to chase profit in other types of shots. The race is now on to develop blockbuster vaccines, defined as those that bring in more than $1-billion annually. Two recently developed vaccines – Prevnar for pneumonia and Garasil for cervical cancer – have become blockbusters, selling close to $2-billion a year.

For now, though, the flu is the driver of all of this.

Flu Inc. is fed by the worry of the unknown, the fears of government that the next pandemic lies just around the corner, or tucked away in some far off area of the world. In the case of H1N1, the threat of illness for 40 per cent of the country was real enough that they went for the shot, sometimes lining up for hours to get it. And that has spawned a new business.

“We are just at the early days of a new era which is just exploding,” Mr. Monteyne said. “The whole vaccine industry is changing.”

Dr. Ossi sees it as a permanent shift in habits. “The volume of vaccine that countries are asking for has gone up exponentially,” he said.

“How many people got flu shots five years ago? Even the very high-risk people out there – the elderly, with chronic lung disease – only 60 per cent of those were vaccinated. Otherwise, many healthy people never thought of getting the flu vaccine.

“Now, all of a sudden, there's a huge demand and a lot of people are jumping on the bandwagon. It's something that will continue for good now. It's a new marketplace.”

***

MAKING FLU VACCINE WORK

How it's made

Like any virus, flu will only grow in living cells. One of the best places to grow it is in fertilized chicken eggs. (Pigs and humans are also good, but less practical.) Eleven days after the egg is fertilized, a hole is drilled into the eggshell and the virus is injected into the fluid surrounding the embryo. After the virus infects the embryo, it multiplies. Machines then crack open the eggs and the virus-filled fluid is removed.

The virus is chemically inactivated, usually with formaldehyde, and used as the “antigen” of a flu shot, which is short for antibody generator. When injected into the body, it's the antigen that provokes an immune response that remembers the code of the virus it encounters.

The antigen-making process can take months because the eggs have to be at the right maturity. The virus also grows slowly and it can take as many as three eggs to make one shot of flu vaccine. Then there are the chickens that produce the eggs – they have to be kept in steady supply, and healthy, since producing enough vaccine for an entire country can take millions of eggs.

What is adjuvant?

The word itself comes from the Latin “adjuvare” which means to help or aid. Adjuvant is a liquid substance used to help boost the flu vaccine and make it work better. Adjuvant also makes it possible to manufacture more doses with the same amount of antigen. Adjuvants can include oils, salts and virosomes, which are bits of influenza virus that cannot replicate.

A time for hope
Viewpoint: The Corner Brook (N.L.) Western Star (excerpt)

HMSP

Copyright (c) 2009 The Hamilton Spectator.

It crept upon us and now it's gone in what seems a flash.

Christmas Day is over, the preparations, the gift buying, the extra food for family and friends who came to visit.

It was a lot of work. But the big question remains: was it all worth it?

For many it's far from over yet as we are still in the midst of the Yuletide season, with New Year's festivities yet to come. Many have holidays and take advantage of them to visit and receive others. It's a great time of the year to reconnect with family and friends.

For most, the memories of the turn of the century are still fresh in our minds. Remember the fear of the millennium bug and how there were global fears of computers failing, lights going out and a multitude of other problems? It's now a decade behind us. The new fear is the H1N1 pandemic.

Going into a new year and a new decade requires a positive attitude with the hopes things will get better, the economy will bounce back and life will be a little better. We've survived Christmas, we survived the millennium bug a full decade ago, and while the H1N1 pandemic was devastating for some, the majority has survived it.

Let's enter a new year full of hope that things will improve for everyone.

Columns
Political shocker put Selinger on top - Landed on his feet amid seismic shift
By Dan Lett
1110 words
30 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A5
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

In his wildest dreams, Greg Selinger never saw it coming.

Manitoba's former finance minister started 2009 preoccupied with the global recession. There was a budget to consider, negotiations on a federal-provincial stimulus package and preparations for an H1N1 pandemic.

But a leadership race? With Premier Gary Doer still enjoying unbridled popularity, there was no hint change was coming.

"I thought we were going to work for a fourth mandate with the team we had," Selinger said in a recent interview. "I think that was the feeling of the majority of us. With the recession, (thought of a leadership change) wasn't anywhere near the top of my mind."

Politics is a volatile game but the events that unfolded in Manitoba this year were nothing less than seismic. Doer's resignation and his decision to take a position as Canada's ambassador in Washington hit the reset button on Manitoba's political economy.

What seemed to be certain is now decidedly uncertain. This includes the province's economic prospects and the outcome of the next provincial election, scheduled by law for 2011.

Notwithstanding the flu and the recession, the pre-eminent provincial political story of 2009 was the story behind Doer's resignation. The exact details remain largely unknown although there are hints he was contemplating an exit as early as the spring.

Political sources from different stripes began picking up on rumours of Doer's departure over the summer. However, the circle of those who knew for sure was kept small and tight by Doer in Winnipeg and Prime Minister Stephen Harper in Ottawa.

NDP insiders confirmed Doer was incredibly discreet about his master plan. His most senior political staff was kept in the dark until the day before or the morning of his resignation.

Few considered Doer a candidate for the post in Washington. In fact, most national media continued focusing on a wide array of the usual suspects. This was the case even after Doer announced his resignation; most national reports hypothesized the Manitoba first minister would take a run at federal politics.

One of the biggest unintended consequences of this strategy was Doer's colleagues in cabinet and caucus, and the party itself, had virtually no warning and no chance to prepare.

Lamentably, the NDP did not react well to Doer's departure. With no immediate prospect for an election, the party inexplicably designed a rash and expedited leadership process that lasted less than 60 days. In the end, it had little democratic integrity.

Leadership candidates scrambled to raise money, secure support from the caucus and wage ground wars to capture delegates in all 57 ridings. Organized labour impaired the process, insisting on a delegate-selection system that awarded automatic delegates to unions. In the end, the unions could not find enough members who wanted to attend the leadership convention, and were forced to return delegate spots to the party. Allegations of cheating and counter-allegations of character assassination bloodied the campaign.

In the end, Selinger ran the best campaign and captured the premier's office. However, it may be that winning may turn out to be one of those "be careful what you wish for" triumphs.

This past week, the province's second-quarter fiscal update was released. It showed that what had been projected to be a $40-million or so surplus this year may turn out to be a $600-million deficit. Despite posting perhaps the best economic performance this year among the provinces, Manitoba is facing the same precipitous drop in revenues that has ravaged treasuries all over Canada.

Just a few weeks ago, Selinger was trumpeting the fact that Manitoba's economy was technically flat, but that "flat was the new up." Selinger suggested the next year was likely to be very tough, but following the throne speech, he specifically ruled out cuts to core services or layoffs. The Q2 update is certainly food for rethinking that.

In an interview, Selinger denied soft-pedalling the economic predicament now facing the province. However, while just a few weeks ago he did not see severe deficit-fighting measures, he is now more circumspect about the strategy for next year's budget.

That does not mean he appears panicky about the growing deficit. Selinger understands that notwithstanding the efforts of critics and enemies to blame the deficit on mismanagement, this is a condition brought on by issues much bigger than Manitoba's per capita spending on health care.

Under Manitoba's new and improved balanced-budget law, Selinger has four years to return the budget to surplus.

If there is a flaw in Selinger's strategy, it may be that a four-year window to slay the deficit will be interrupted in two years by the next provincial election. And that means Tory Leader Hugh McFadyen will have his best chance, maybe ever, to topple the NDP machine.

Tory rank and file can smell opportunity with Doer gone and Selinger managing a growing deficit. But it will take more than just a budget deficit to defeat the NDP, and that appears to be what the eager McFadyen has anticipated.

No Doer, no surplus and a more focused Tory campaign will make the next election more competitive, that is a certainty. But if Selinger does not go early, and he has more or less indicated he will not, he has one more year to get a handle on the treasury and solidify his profile as leader of his party and province.

Two years could be enough time, or it could expire in the blink of an eye. Either way, the next two years will be the most important of Selinger's political life.

dan.lett@freepress.mb.ca

Byelection for

Doer's old seat

A provincial byelection to fill former premier Gary Doer's old seat in northeast Winnipeg will likely be held before the legislature resumes sitting March 23, a provincial spokesman said Tuesday.

But first Premier Greg Selinger has to call a voting date for the Concordia constituency and the Progressive Conservatives and Liberals have to select candidates. The government spokesman said ideally the vote would be held before the end of March so that the new MLA could take his or her seat when the province tables its new budget.

The seat was left open when Doer became Canada's ambassador to the United States.

In November, longtime party insider Matt Wiebe was selected at a nomination meeting as the NDP candidate for the Concordia riding. Wiebe worked as Doer's constituency assistant.

Editorial
Post mortem on H1N1 flu
618 words
30 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
A16
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

With the H1N1 flu virus now fading, Canada's public health experts, scientists, and political leaders should shift their emphasis from fighting the pandemic to analyzing the vaccination campaign, with an eye to what went wrong and how to do better next time.

Frontline doctors, nurses and public health officials worked extraordinarily hard and managed to immunize more than 40 per cent of Canadians. But some weaknesses in the system stood starkly revealed.

Response to this pandemic was distressingly uneven, with access to potentially life-saving shots varying greatly from one region to another and even between health units in the same province. For example, while crowds forced Toronto to shut down its two initial H1N1 vaccine clinics just hours after opening them, Sault Ste. Marie was immunizing residents with military precision. That's because the Soo had set up an efficient registry in advance.

Distribution flaws in several provinces saw small children and other high-risk persons stuck in long line-ups while sports stars and hospital insiders received priority treatment. And the federal government opted to contract with one pharmaceutical manufacturer for the vaccine rather than spread the task among several. That led to serious production delays.

It is little wonder, then, that the H1N1 virus was chosen by Canada's newspaper editors and broadcast news directors as the top news story of 2009. But it wasn't all bad news.

Unless the H1N1 virus mutates in some unexpected way and manages a rebound, Canadians, as a group, should consider themselves lucky. While more than 400 H1N1-related deaths have been reported in Canada, even an average flu season typically kills thousands.

The pandemic appears to have peaked in mid-November. Canadian hospitalizations, intensive care unit admissions, and deaths have dropped steadily since then. There are similar results elsewhere, with the World Health Organization reporting just over 11,500 global fatalities related to the H1N1 virus. That's a relative pittance compared to the toll of past pandemics.

In retrospect, the H1N1 virus served as a useful dry run, sharpening our pandemic response. To take advantage of it, however, we need an in-depth and unbiased look at what went wrong, coupled with a commitment to fix the problems.

Some steps seem obvious, such as signing contracts with more than one pharmaceutical company to produce the vaccine. Others will be more difficult to implement. For example, Canada clearly needs a centralized, efficient and truly national public health system, but bureaucratic and constitutional barriers stand in the way.

Even without a national system, however, Ontario could take some meaningful steps toward correcting problems within its own borders. The H1N1 vaccine was delivered by the province's 36 public health units, which were clearly overwhelmed by the task. Other provinces managed better with fewer delivery units. Next time, Ontario should consider handing the assignment to its 14 Local Health Integration Networks (LHINs), which can call on the resources of hospitals and clinics to assist in distribution.

The best practises deployed in various parts of the country, such as in the Soo, should be studied and borrowed. After a crisis, the various health authorities would do this sort of analysis as a matter of course. But Canada's response to the H1N1 pandemic should be subject to a broader review, perhaps by an expert panel struck for that purpose.

Time is of the essence. Pandemics can erupt and spread with startling speed. It is worth noting that the current H1N1 outbreak only began in March. As this pandemic wanes, it is prudent of us to start preparing for the next one. Because next time we might not be so lucky.

Potash earnings drop a big story in '09

Editor's Note: This is the second instalment of a two-part series

- - -

From potash to pork, agriculture to assets, the 2009 year in business saw highlights and lowlights in the industries that affect the Saskatoon region every day.

Here's a look at the top business stories that took place during the second-half of the year:

JULY

- TD Economics downgraded its 2009 economic forecast for Saskatchewan, estimating the province would see a one per cent drop in gross domestic product this year instead of the 0.4 per cent increase it had forecast in March. The drop was caused by a less bullish outlook on potash, agriculture and oil.

- After PotashCorp released second-quarter earnings $700 million lower than the same period in 2008, the provincial government said it would rework its 2009-10 budget as it became clear the $1.9 billion it once expected from potash sales this year would not be realized. The government had expected to take in nearly $2 billion on sales of potash at $550 US per tonne.

- Stomp Pork Farms Inc. sold off its inventory after it and Stomp Pork Farm USA Inc. went into receivership earlier in the summer. Court documents show the inventory was sold as a result of depressed hog prices, a high Canadian dollar and the effects of the H1N1 or "swine flu" virus on the industry.

- Real estate: 440 homes sold at an average price of $283,619. (All residential real estate figures are from the Saskatoon Region Association of Realtors. The association finds the average residential price by dividing the dollar volume of homes sold by the number of homes sold.)

AUGUST

- The Saskatchewan Environmental Society (SES) called on the province to immediately pause the development of Saskatchewan's oilsands reserves pending a thorough environmental assessment of the northwestern oilsands zone and a public consultation on future development. The SES said it didn't want the province, which is in the early stages of developing the oilsands industry, to make the same mistakes that have "plagued the oilsands development in Alberta."

- The University of Saskatchewan's Edwards school of business announced the opening of a downtown campus. The K.W. Nasser Centre, named after the man who made the campus a reality, is home to the school's master of business administration program.

- A pre-feasibility study prepared for Shore Gold Inc. indicated the Saskatoon company's Star diamond project could produce 20 million carats at a weighted average price of $265 per carat over 12 years for a total of $5.3 billion. The study said construction on an open-pit mine at the site in east-central Saskatchewan could start as early as 2010.

- Real estate: 393 homes sold at an average price of $281,871.

SEPTEMBER

- Saskatoon's Urban Playground organization, which is dedicated to making the city an attractive place for youth to live, learn and work, released a report showing the region stands to lose $417 million during the next two years if the flight of young talent from the city continues. The report, Attracting and Retaining Talent to Saskatoon, indicates 12 per cent of residents under 40 plan to leave the city within the next 24 months.

- The shareholders of Athabasca Potash Inc. (API) voted in a new board of directors at the company's annual meeting. The board that won the vote was led by company founder Dawn Zhou, who was ousted from her position as president and CEO by the former board during the summer.

- A conference on innovation, networking and creativity made its debut at TCU Place. Backed by the W. Brett Wilson Centre for Entrepreneurial Excellence at the University of Saskatchewan, the Sask INC Innovation Forum attracted between 200 and 250 participants.

- Real estate: 351 homes sold at an average price of $279,457.

OCTOBER

- Karnalyte Resources Inc. announced plans to develop a $287-million US solution mine and processing plant near Wynyard to gain access to carnallite, a mineral that contains the same type of potassium potash firms mine, as well as magnesium. The company, which is in the early stages of the process, said the project could be up and running within two years.

- The Canadian Wheat Board (CWB) opened a grain testing lab at Innovation Place. The farmer-controlled organization explained it is moving to test Prairie grain itself rather than send it to third-party laboratories.

- PotashCorp announced a third-quarter drop in net earnings of nearly $1 billion from the same quarter last year. Company president and CEO Bill Doyle said at the time drop was a result of an economic crisis that "cut deeper and lasted longer" than the company initially anticipated.

- Real estate: 311 homes sold at an average price of $274,759.

NOVEMBER

- After announcing in August it had placed Saskatoon on its shortlist of candidates to host a $150-million processing plant, Fortune Minerals Ltd. confirmed the Saskatoon region is its best choice for the facility. The company said the plant, which will process ore from its Nico project in the Northwest Territories, will be located near Langham.

- Big Sky Farms Inc. filed for creditor protection after a lengthy downturn in the hog market led Saskatchewan's largest hog producer to take the financial action. The move, which gives the company time to create a restructuring plan, caused outrage among local producers owed money by the company, resulting in a blockade at one of Big Sky's facilities and a rally at the legislature. Documents show the company owes its creditors $96 million.

- Local farmers, especially those north of Saskatoon, take advantage of balmy weather in the race to finish harvest before winter arrived. Harvest had been delayed by cool, wet weather through October.

- Real estate: 254 homes sold at an average price of $278,885.

DECEMBER

- BHP Billiton, which has its eyes set on developing the Jansen potash project in east-central Saskatchewan, announced plans to move from its Innovation Place office to a new home in downtown Saskatoon's Discovery Plaza early in the new year. The company opened an office in the city in 2008.

- Cameco Corp. moved to sell its 48.5 per cent stake in Centerra Gold Inc. for net proceeds of $872 million. The Saskatoon-based company said it is selling off its gold asset in order to better focus on its uranium business.

- A lack of uranium ore will lead to an indefinite shutdown of Areva Resources Canada Inc.'s McClean Lake mill in July 2010, the company announced mid-month. The move to put the mill into a "care and maintenance mode" will result in 140 layoffs, 120 at McClean Lake and 20 in the company's Saskatoon office.

- Real estate: Figures not yet complete
