U.S. registers increase in birth rate after decline

Birth rates in the United States are climbing after years of significant declines, according to an annual report in the journal Pediatrics.

Researchers from the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention found that the number of U.S. births for 2007 had jumped 1 per cent to 4,265,555.

The new number represents "the highest number ever registered for the United States," they said.

Almost 1.9 million people joined the population due to "natural increase," which is basically the net birth rate.

As the birth rate rose, the age-adjusted death rate dropped 2.1 per cent, a record low for the United States.

Life expectancy at birth reached a record high of 77.9 years.

One shocker: The U.S.'s infant death rate is 6.83 per 1,000 births. Compare that with Malaysia (6.2) or Korea (3.8).

And in the U.S. capital, the District of Columbia? It's nearly double the national rate, at 12.22.

Younger mothers, particularly adolescents, pushed the birth rate higher.

From 1991 through 2005, teen birth rates dropped 34 per cent. Since 2005, though, they've popped back up 5 per cent.

In terms of teen-pregnancy prevention, the study's authors conclude, "it could be that new messages and strategies are needed to reach the teenagers of today."

Click off the TV and burn more calories

Just push yourself away from the television. You will burn more calories.

Overweight adults who watched three hours of TV a day and cut their viewing in half for three weeks used about 120 more calories a day than a similar group of viewers, who continued watching five hours a day on average, a research trial has found.

While 120 calories may not sound like much, it is equivalent to the number of calories burned in a 1.6-kilometre walk, said Jennifer J. Otten, lead author of the paper published in the Archives of Internal Medicine.

"If you add it up over time, it's equivalent to walking eight miles (12.8 kilometres) a week.

"Over a year, it might help prevent weight gain of 12 pounds."

Unlike children whose television time is cut, the adults did not eat less. But they spent more time in light physical activities or sedentary activities that burn more calories than watching television does -- reading, playing board games or scrapbooking.

Body language unmasks racism in TV shows

A new study suggests one doesn't have to say a word to communicate racism.

Researchers at Massachusetts' Tufts University found white characters in scripted television shows display far more negative body language toward their black characters than to members of their own race.

The researchers examined 11 television programs, including Bones, Grey's Anatomy, CSI and Scrubs. They say the scenes they examined portrayed the black characters as social and intellectual peers to the white characters.

Still, researchers found positive body language such as smiling, nodding and leaning forward while talking is far less common when white characters engage with their black co-stars.

The study is in the Dec. 18 edition of the journal Science.

Swine flu is a threat to new moms

A new study finds swine flu is not only dangerous to pregnant women, but it's also a threat to new mothers.

An analysis of pregnant women and new mothers who were hospitalized with swine flu in California found that those who had a baby in the previous two weeks were at higher risk of severe flu complications.

The new report, released last week by the New England Journal of Medicine, is the first to look at the risk to women who recently gave birth. The study was done by the California Department of Public Health and the U.S. Centers for Disease Control.

Out of his comfort zone and into the hot seat - Year-end interview with Premier Greg Selinger
By Bruce Owen and Larry Kusch
1265 words
29 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A4
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

FIVE months ago, Greg Selinger was comfortably tucked away in a first-floor Manitoba legislature office as finance minister, a job he'd held for 10 years.

The recession was about a year old, but Manitoba's economy was humming along compared with other provinces.

Coming off a summer break, Selinger's only focus was staying the course.

Then, on the morning of Aug. 27, everything changed.

Gary Doer stunned almost everyone by announcing he was stepping down as premier. (A day later, he was appointed Canada's ambassador to the United States.)

Within seconds, the methodical Selinger felt that tingling in the gut you get when a big decision has to be made.

It took him a while -- it's his style, he never rushes into things -- but the former university associate professor of social work decided to throw his hat into the ring. It was a three-way race for premier, but looking back, few had any doubt Doer's second-floor office was Selinger's for the taking.

Now, more than two months into the job, the 58-year-old sat down with the Free Press to talk about what's ahead. Here's an edited version of that discussion:

FP: What have you learned about being a premier that you didn't know before?

Selinger: I'm not sure there's something I've learned that's new. But the reality is it's always different actually doing it versus being close to the person doing it. In my case, I've just been out more, less in the cabinet room doing, say, the nitty-gritty work of budget and more being out around Manitoba visiting various communities and various groups.

FP: Is perhaps that your greatest challenge, introducing yourself to Manitobans, getting them to know you?

Selinger: You know what? A lot of them know you already, but it's actually been enjoyable to have more direct contact with Manitobans and all the various communities I've visited. It's been quite positive. The bottom line is I will be out more interacting with various communities around things that we can do together.

FP: Some people within the opposition are still talking as though you will go to the people in 2010. Will you rule that out now?

Selinger: As I've said, we plan to serve out the mandate (until October 2011), I plan to serve out the mandate. There's lots of worthwhile things to do. There's lots of challenges. And that's my plan.

FP: In your throne speech you said you want to have an economic summit in the new year. Why?

Selinger: We did it 10 years ago. It just seemed time to do it again. It's just a good time to take a look at where we want to go in the future. There has been a very significant jolt to the global economy. It has impacts on all of us. We've always said we're not immune to that. We're starting to see some of the effects of that in terms of issues around Manitoba and different workplaces. We think it's a good time to take stock and refocus where we want to go in the future.

FP: At the same time you also want to create a premier's advisory council on poverty and education. Why?

Selinger: We do have an opportunity in Manitoba to improve the way we work together, to get educational outcomes, to address issues of social housing, to address a variety of issues, many of which we've taken initiatives on. But we've heard from people in the community that they'd like to have more opportunities to dialogue with the government on that and this is one way to do it. We'd like to get that up and running early in the first quarter of 2010.

FP: When are we going to be sitting in the new football stadium?

Selinger: As you know, the proponent for the new stadium asked for a year delay on that. The reality is that we will be having conversations with all the proponents and see what's possible to move that project forward.

FP: Is there a chance Mr. (David) Asper could have ownership of the Blue Bombers prior to a new football stadium being built?

Selinger: That hasn't been proposed by anybody at this stage of the game, that I'm aware of. But the reality is that I think we would want the idea of a new stadium to have some solid possibilities. As I understand it, the existing arrangement was that he has to build the new facility before he gets ownership of the team.

FP: The Queen is coming (in 2010). Are you looking forward to that?

Selinger: Absolutely. It's Manitoba's 140th birthday. It's a great honour to have the Queen here. It's a recognition of the province's role in Confederation. She's coming to three provinces. We're glad she's chosen us to be one of them.

FP: Have you met her before?

Selinger: Yes, when she was here last time (2002).

FP: One of the things you prided yourself on as finance minister is that you used to walk to work every day. Are you still walking to work?

Selinger: Not as frequently. I've been a little busy travelling around to various locations, so that routine has been broken by a necessity to be out and around more.

FP: Is there anything in your first 50 days as premier where you've asked yourself, 'What have I gotten myself into?'

Selinger: You know what? I think we knew we are in a global recession. This places challenges on governments. Governments have unique roles in recessions. They have greater demands for spending to solve a variety of issues and they have less revenue. That creates some unique challenges for a government. All governments are experiencing that right now. Our challenge is to keep the momentum going, to build and grow the economy, and at the same time address the essential service needs of Manitobans. That is a big challenge for every premier in the country right now as well as the prime minister.

FP: So you made the right decision?

Selinger: Absolutely. We all knew that there were going to be challenges. If the job was easy, why would you want to do it, quite frankly?

FP: What specific challenges do you see in the year ahead?

Selinger: We had a pandemic this fall and we'll see what the outcome of that is. So far, Manitoba has been able to respond in a pretty organized fashion in providing the (H1N1) vaccine to people, but there are always new surprises coming down the pipe. I think the economy will remain central, keeping the economy going and stimulated. There is a broad consensus among all the premiers and the prime minister that we have to continue the stimulus program well into early 2011, maybe all the way through 2011, depending what happens with the American economy. One thing that I think is good is that the country is working together on that. There's been good co-operation for the most part between the federal and provincial governments to make things happen to keep the economy buoyant.

News
THE YEAR OF WAITING, AND WAITING AND WAITING; Do you get the feeling we spent most of 2009 fuming in long lines?
Katie Daubs
Toronto Star
741 words
29 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
A01
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

Angrier than usual as 2009 draws to a close? Probably residual lineup rage.

In the past year, Torontonians have stood around waiting for H1N1 vaccines, faced garbage strike queues, lined up to buy condos, shuffled to buy TTC tokens and have been subjected to the daily traffic jam.

And in the last week of 2009, thousands waited up to eight hours at Pearson to board planes to the U.S., after an attempted terrorist attack led to more security screening.

Yes, this was a year spent in line.

"Queues have been with us ever since humans have had semi-organized society," said Richard Larson, the director of the Center for Engineering Systems Fundamentals at Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

"I think in general the trend is for queues to go away."

Larson, who is also known as Dr. Queue, said such new technologies as ATMs and the Internet have caused lineups to die off a little.

But in Toronto, the line continues to thrive, enraging those who must submit to its unwieldy size to make it through the day. Myron Hlynka, a queuing theorist at the University of Windsor, said 2009 was probably worse than other years.

On Oct. 29, at the North York Civic Centre, thousands came with their children for H1N1 vaccinations. Many were turned away. Those who remained decried the Third World conditions in the line.

"Older people were waiting outside in the cold. I thought, something was wrong with this," Hlynka said.

So did many others.

Similar to the rage felt by a commuter who waits dutifully in the correct lane while a late merger zips up the right, queue rage is the indignation that arises when hospital board members and sports stars jump ahead for H1N1 vaccinations. Or it might be the anger felt by realtors who waited in a long line for days to buy a condo, only to be told they had waited in the wrong line. Queue rage is what happens in the absence of fair play: it elicits exaggerated sighs, rolled eyes and shouting matches.

"Doesn't that make people furious when you hear those athletes get (the vaccine)?" MIT's Larson said. "That's line jumping in policy, even if you can't see a physical line."

Although the H1N1 vaccine clinics soon had lineup systems in place, Larson said the original scene was reminiscent of Vladimir Sorokin's The Queue. In the 1983 fictional book, people waited in a three-day-long line in Moscow without knowing what they were waiting for.

Larson said people experience a common fear that if they don't join a line, whatever is at the end will be depleted.

The 24-hour news cycle, with updates about the status of lines, hasn't helped.

"When there's a perceived shortage, long lines become attractive," he said.

"It's a complete perversion."

Lines have been around since the dawn of time. The history of queue rage is more recent.

Did Cro-Magnon man weasel his way to the front of the food line by asking Cro-Magnon woman about the weather?

Did all those animals lining up for salvation on Noah's Ark wait patiently in pairs?

Larson said academics started studying the psychology of lines in the 1950s, when Disneyland opened in California. The company decided to leave out sidewalks during the first weeks so they could see where people walked before they paved.

That same decade, on the opposite coast of the country, people were complaining about long elevator delays in a high-rise apartment in New York City.

A man sent to study the problem suggested mirrors next to the elevators.

The complaints dropped to zero.

"People are distracted, they adjust their tie and makeup," Larson said.

The mirrors of the '50s have become the TV screens on TTC subway platforms and the trendy music at Canada's Wonderland. Other advancements have been made - the express and self-checkout lines at the grocery store try to make shopping more enjoyable.

No technology can ever fully eradicate rage, so Larson suggests people do something absurd, and try to make conversation.

"It depresses me when I see 14 people in line all face down checking stock prices," he said.

"The line is a lonely place."

Arts & Life
Remembering the pop culture moments of the year
Doug Camilli
Canwest News Service
729 words
29 December 2009
Saskatoon Star Phoenix

Copyright © 2009 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

What? Huh? December again, already? Time to take stock and sum up, you say? Well, somebody's got to do it, so I sat down with the tools of my trade.

While cutting the lime into wedges, I gave 2009 the mental once-over: Recession. Afghanistan. Swine flu. Bernie Madoff. The Michael Jackson industry. Iran. The climate change issue. Olympic marketing overkill. The coalition government fuss. The New York Yankees winning the World Series. Michael Ignatieff. Tiger Woody. Ugh.

2009 is surely the Year of the Vampire. The lifeless undead were ubiquitous this year, first in teen fiction, then in teen movies, teen TV and everywhere: True Blood, The Vampire Diaries, Twilight, etc., etc.

Why are we obsessed with vampires? Sociologists droned on about anxiety in the culture. Novelist Anne Rice calls vampirism "a powerful metaphor for the outcast and monster in all of us."

Vampires were not, however, the only absurd thing about 2009. Here are some of my other favourites:

- Those wacky Belgians: Belgium's national body-building championships were cancelled after government anti-doping inspectors showed up -- and all 20 competitors ran away. They should have expected some testing: Last year three-quarters of competitors tested positive for steroids and other bad stuff.

- Pickup line of the year: "I love you -- and I have a condom!" Yeah, that oughtta work. (It's the first line of a justly forgotten movie called The Uninvited.)

- Tweet of the year: Singer Lily Allen: "People say I'm egocentric. But enough about them."

- Toughest review of the year: Deborah Ross in The Spectator mag: "There really isn't a lot to say about Lesbian Vampire Killers apart from this: Don't go anywhere near it. Just don't see it. Do something else instead. Do anything else instead. Catch up with your ironing . . . at 87 minutes, it's probably a good 87 minutes too long."

- What will they call their kids?: Kelly Hildebrandt of Florida married Kelly Hildebrandt of Texas. The first one's middle name is Katrina; the second one's middle moniker is Carl. They met online, after she was searching for her own name on Facebook. After making sure they aren't closely related, they started dating.

- What could possibly go wrong?: Solange Magnano, Miss Argentina of 1994, died from complications after buttock-enhancement therapy. She was 38.

- Wedding of the year: In Suvereto, near Livorno in Italy, a bride and groom had arranged for the bride's bouquet to be tossed to the bridesmaids and single female guests from a small plane. But the flowers somehow got sucked into the engine and the plane caught fire, exploded and plunged into a hostel, Corriere della Sera reported. One person was badly hurt.

- You can see she's got a future in politics: Paula Abdul, when asked about a report that Simon Cowell had asked her out on a date, answered this way: "There's some truth to that, and no truth to it."

- Teacher of the year: Crystal Defanti, a Grade 5 teacher near Sacramento, Calif., gave each of her students an end-of-year gift: A DVD she made herself, with footage of school events, outings, prize-givings -- and her on a sofa, naked and intimate with a boyfriend.

It was an accident, she said, apologizing. This being California, the school board didn't fire her.

- "Men are pigs" dept.: Mel Gibson, Jon Gosselin, David Letterman, Ronnie Wood, Gordon Ramsay, John Edwards, Mark Sanford, Eddie Cibrian and, of course, Tiger Woods.

My favourite is Sanford, governor of South Carolina, who was supposedly "hiking the Appalachian trail" when, in fact, he was in South America with a mistress. All over South Carolina, guys in bars started getting slapped in the face just for inviting girls to go hiking.

- This never happens to Horatio Caine: When German cops found the same female DNA at 39 different crime scenes, over two years, they were sure they had a serial killer who also dabbled in other types of crime. But it turned out that all 39 samples matched the factory worker who had made the cotton swabs cops use to collect samples at crime scenes.

Editorial
Bitterness and venom in Ottawa
National Post
675 words
29 December 2009
National Post
FINP
National
A20
English
(c) 2009 National Post . All Rights Reserved.

Parliament Hill is understandably prone to partisan extremes, but 2009 has been a particularly bilious year. Liberal party members will blame the atmosphere of chronic hostility on the Conservatives and the aggressiveness of their tactics in bringing the never-ending election campaign to Canada, complete with negative ads and the targeted denigration of opponents.

Conservatives can respond by citing the Liberals' continuing refusal to accept the validity of their rejection by voters, and their relentless efforts to demonize Prime Minister Stephen Harper and his government. What, after all, was the coalition crisis last winter but an attempt to undo the election of 2008 and install as prime minister a Liberal leader who had been overwhelmingly rejected by voters just weeks before?

Whichever side you prefer, there's no denying that whatever accomplishments the government can claim have been overshadowed by the bitterness and venom that characterized the year.

Just a quick run through the past 12 months' events offers a taste of how rancid federal politics have become. The coalition crisis was quickly followed by the economic crisis, during which the opposition mounted a furious effort to convince Canadians, against all evidence and common sense, that Mr. Harper's government was somehow to blame for a downturn that was sweeping the globe (and which Canada, in fact, weathered far better than most).

That was followed by efforts to somehow link the government to the unappetizing spectacle of former prime minister Brian Mulroney defending himself against the ever-changing accusations of the diminutive German arms merchant Karlheinz Schreiber; which was followed in turn by Liberal leader Michael Ignatieff insisting the government had betrayed the unemployed by failing to enrichen the employment insurance program, even though the Liberals had considered it perfectly satisfactory when they were running the show.

We were treated to two efforts to force the resignation of Natural Resources Minister Lisa Raitt (once over some private remarks she'd made to a press aide and once over fundraising activities in a previous job); accusations the Prime Minister had surreptitiously slipped a Communion wafer into his pocket; claims that the Tories had secretly manipulated Olympicwear designers into using a C (for Canada) that looked like the C (for Conservatives) on party literature; efforts to blame the government for a shortage of H1N1 vaccine that was being felt world-wide; allegations the Tories were trying to link Liberal MPs to anti-Semitism; and the year-ending hysteria over the Copenhagen climate change conference, which saw Canada, which produces less than 2% of global greenhouse gas emissions, portrayed by overheated activists as an international pariah even though it has more aggressive reduction targets than China (which produces 21%) or the U.S. (which produces 20%).

No wonder the Conservatives habitually react to critics with a mix of derision and contempt, which nonetheless does nothing to improve the atmosphere. Defence Minister Peter MacKay only managed to inflame a debate over Canada's treatment of Afghan detainees when he chose to belittle whistleblower Richard Colvin rather than address his allegations. Over their four years in power the Conservatives have grown increasingly fond of secrecy and obfuscation, boycotting committees they don't like when they aren't stuffing the Senate with partisan supporters.

Minority governments carry with them the constant possibility of an election, though the danger this year looks to be less than in the recent past. Liberal leader Michael Ignatieff is looking to recuperate from self-inflicted wounds, and needs time. Mr. Harper is in a position of relative strength in the polls, which should provide a measure of comfort. Both men would do Canada a service if they would disavow the cheap tactics and low blows of the past 12 months. The government needs to get over its love of secrecy and distrust of anyone and anything that doesn't come with a Conservative party membership card; the opposition has to abandon the cheap, scandal-a-day approach to challenging the government.

Nobody wins in trench warfare.

News
Hospital battling superbug; VRE outbreak; Lakeshore limiting visits to patients
CHARLIE FIDELMAN
The Gazette
485 words
28 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A13
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

Lakeshore General Hospital posted a guard on its fourth floor west wing to limit visits to patients because of an outbreak of hospital-borne bacterial infections that are resistant to antibiotics.

But while patients and relatives complained of an outbreak of C. difficile (Clostridium difficile) illnesses, which causes abdominal cramping and diarrhea, Lakeshore hospital officials said they are actually fighting an outbreak of another superbug, Vancomycin-resistant Enterococci, known as VRE.

Commonly found in people's intestines, Enterococci does not usually cause illness in the healthy, but can cause urinary and wound infections in those with a weakened immune system.

"I was going to go visit today, but changed my mind because I don't want to bring anything home. I'm concerned about the rest of my family," said Rod Demarais, who regularly visits a friend on the fourth-floor medical wing.

Ten days ago, hospital staff moved patients onto gurneys in the hallways while walls were washed and rooms were disinfected, and then they moved patients back into their beds, said a patient, who asked that his name not be published because he feared reprisals from the nurses.

Hospital spokesperson Marie Josée Labrosse confirmed the hospital is dealing with an outbreak of VRE in eight patients.

"What we do is close the floor, clean as much as we can, and isolate the patients," she said. "We have some cases (of C. diff) but no outbreak.

Visitors are allowed into patients' rooms one at a time if they wash their hands thoroughly and wear a surgical gown and gloves.

Since 2003-2004, when a hyper-virulent strain of C.diff in Quebec killed hundreds of patients, hospitals are required to report outbreaks or clusters of C.diff cases to public health authorities.

According to the latest public health data available only as of the end of November, two Montreal hospitals are reporting outbreaks of

C. difficile, said Terry-Nan Tannenbaum of the Montreal Health and Social Services Agency's department of public health.

Maisonneuve Rosemont and St. Luc hospitals reported 14 cases of C. difficile. There have been no deaths.

"This is the time of year when C. diff occurs," Tannenbaum said. "But hospitals are aware of what they have to do - basic control measures that have to do with cleaning and rapidly isolating symptomatic people."

At least 13 people died at Charles LeMoyne Hospital in the Montérégie region as of August in connection with an outbreak that sickened 43 patients.

"We can't say it's over. As of Friday, we had 15 cases," said hospital spokesperson Annie Laroche, but no new deaths.

Quebec health officials had reported a small drop in hospital-acquired Clostridium difficile illnesses in November because of extra hygiene measures adopted because of the threat of the H1N1 virus.

Issues & Ideas
Canadian politics pretty dull in 2009
Rob Roach
Special to the Sun
743 words
28 December 2009
Vancouver Sun
VNCS
Final
A15
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Sun

I have been asking my fellow policy wonks what stands out for them in Canadian politics in 2009 and the answer seems to be "not much." Despite this, it is worth putting together a list that helps sum up the year. Here goes:

1. The election that wasn't: Remember when Ignatieff told Harper that his "time was up" and promised to bring down the government at the first available opportunity? Yeah, that didn't happen.

2. Canada gets a black eye in Copenhagen: There was so much spin coming out of the United Nations Climate Change Conference in Copenhagen that it was hard to tell what was going on let alone what it will mean in the years ahead. In an unusual twist, Canada was cast against type as a villain. It's unclear what this achieved other than making students think twice about sewing Canadian flags onto their backpacks before travelling in Europe. On the domestic front, the real work is yet to take place as the provinces and federal government need to design and implement a workable national plan that does not hamstring the economy.

3. Alberta runs a deficit: It turns out that money does not grow on trees in Alberta as the province endured its first deficit in 15 years. The final amount will likely be smaller than the $7 billion figure forecast back in June, but it is still a dramatic shift from the multi-billion dollar surpluses of previous years. Boom and bust--that's life in Alberta.

4. Cool sporting event that takes place in British Columbia between 2009 and 2011: It's not clear if I am allowed to use the word Olympic without a license, but I will risk it. While Canadians were stocking up on Olympic mittens and commemorative glasses, tension was brewing in Vancouver over attempts by the organizers to control guerrilla marketing and what others see as a totalitarian-esque crackdown on free speech.

5. The feds start selling cars: It was back in December 2008 that the federal government and the province of Ontario announced loans to GM and Chrysler totaling about $4 billion. Fast forward to summer 2009 and the bailout figure was over $10 billion and the federal government owned an 11.7 per cent stake in GM.

6. Even more unelected Senators: Stephen Harper is the only Prime Minister that has taken Senate reform seriously and we need to wait and see if he can manoeuvre this file through the sludge that is Canadian politics. After appointing 18 unelected Senators at the end of 2008, Harper appointed nine more in August 2009 including former Tory campaign chair Doug Finley. Democracy and the Red Chamber will remain distant strangers for some time to come.

7. Canada stays in the inner circle of richest countries: Some wonks were wondering when Canada would get the tap on the shoulder and be asked to make room in the G8 for China or some other country. This was avoided when the G8's role as "the pre-eminent council on global economic cooperation" was taken over by the G20 in the fall.

8. Swine flu: Known for a while by the unseemly moniker swine flu, H1N1 brought tragedy to many individuals and families in Canada and around the world who lost loved ones to the virus. At the same time, the federal government and the provinces launched a mass immunization program. It is difficult to assess the success of the program at this time, but its ups and down should provide lots of fodder for improving how we respond to future challenges of this sort.

9. The Great Recession: The claim that capitalism was dead was refuted in 2009. Yes, the domestic and global economy limped along and many people were hit hard, but the economy lives to grow another day.

10. The War in Afghanistan: Last, but not least, and whether you think Canada should be in Afghanistan or not, our soldiers continue to put their lives on the line in the service of Canada and for that they deserve our deepest thanks. The war dragged on this year and will likely do so in 2010 as well. Let's all pray for peace and the safe return of our service men and women.

Robert Roach is director of research for the Canada West Foundation.

The End of the 00s: A decade in review

Robert Sibley examines the themes that marked our world, starting with the Age of Anxiety. The decade was riven with uncertainty and the fear of asteroids, BSE, SARS, avian flu, swine flu, nuclear war, global warming and countless other doomsday scenarios

CAUTION SIGNS
Comment
Slippery road ahead after a decade of fear
TODD HIRSCH
745 words
28 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
A17
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Calgary-based senior economist at ATB Financial

All Canadian drivers know the terror of skidding on black ice. It's one of those great national unifiers, like hockey and Tim's double-doubles.

If we're lucky enough to avoid a crash, we breathe an enormous sigh of relief, but we don't return to driving as normal. We shut the radio off. Talking and laughing cease. We slow down, squeeze the wheel and drive with exaggerated focus. It's instant sobriety.

There is a resonance as we look forward to the decade starting in 2010.

Recent decades can be loosely defined by a word or a phrase. Social upheaval in the 1960s. Flash and entitlement in the 1980s. Rage and disillusionment in the 1990s.

The 2000s? Fear.

From the first few minutes, when we panicked about lights shutting off and planes dropping out of the sky, fear defined the 2000s. Y2K proved to be a non-event, but it set the stage for a decade of terrifying events.

Consider the big news stories of the past 120 months: Collapsing tech stocks. Planes slamming into the World Trade Center. Bombings in Bali, Madrid and London. Anthrax. The Beltway sniper. The Indian Ocean tsunami. Eerie videotapes of a bearded man named bin Laden. Hurricane Katrina. SARS. Drowning polar bears. The near collapse of the global financial market. H1N1.

Even when economic times were good, there was fear. In the United States, people feared missing out on the housing market and piled crazily in to whatever real estate they could. China's rise as an economic powerhouse has been, on balance, tremendously beneficial for the global economy. But this has stirred irrational fears of the Chinese taking over the world. (The host's medal count at the Beijing Olympics may have been a harbinger of things to come!)

Fortunately, fear has a way of clearing and sharpening the mind. A near-collision on black ice brings an adrenalin rush that heightens our senses and refocuses our attention. And, at its best, fear is an essential survival instinct as it helps us avoid legitimate dangers in the future.

If fear marked the 2000s, maybe a post-panic sobriety will mark the next 10 years. Focus, caution and greatly lowered expectations might be what we look back on in December, 2019.

While a decade of caution and lower expectations may not sound like much fun, we're due for one – particularly around public finance. Government debt levels around the world have ballooned nearly out of control, and credit-rating agencies are fearful (there's that word again) that some governments may be rendered insolvent in the coming years. While small countries such as Iceland and Greece have already been smacked down pretty hard, even the giant U.S. and British governments have had their fiscal knuckles rapped. Japan is hanging by a thread.

A decade of sobriety and lower expectations will begin with a realization that governments cannot keep stimulating forever. Through higher taxes, lower spending or a combination of both, public spending must be brought under control. None of this will be welcome news to voters, but we will have no choice.

On the bright side, we can hope that the decade of fear has handed us a few lessons – lessons about excess, entitlement and greed. During the two most fearful events of the decade, 9/11 and the market meltdown of 2008, we learned that we actually don't know very much at all. We were shaken and humbled. Like a hard slap in the face, we were taught that our governments, our economies and our environment are much more fragile than we thought.

However, being handed a lesson and learning a lesson are different things. Will the coming decade bring about the sobriety we need? Or will our memories be short? If excess, entitlement and greed are not curbed, the world can anticipate more horror in the decade starting in 2010. And if we do not accept that government deficits and debt must be controlled, we will only prolong a much worse fate later on.

We say goodbye to the decade of fear, but we won't miss it. When we ring in the new year, we will remember to tread lightly. The economic and political road signs are still warning us: Caution! Icy Conditions Ahead.

The opinions are Todd Hirsch's own.

Third Page
Sask. response to H1N1 a team effort: McKinnon
Pamela Cowan
Saskatchewan News Network; Regina Leader-Post
961 words
28 December 2009
Saskatoon Star Phoenix
SSP
Final
A3
English
Copyright © 2009 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

An aboriginal map hangs opposite Dr. Moira McKin non's desk -- a reminder of the native land of Saskatchewan's chief medical health officer.

"It reminds me of home; it reminds me of the importance of country and communication," McKinnon said of the painting from the desert in the north part of Western Australia. "It just grounds me."

Preparing for the global influenza pandemic, McKinnon had to be grounded as she mapped out the province's H1N1 strategy -- a plan that became the envy of many other Canadian jurisdictions.

Some clinics, such as those in Alberta, opened their doors to the general public when the H1N1 vaccine was available, but the vaccine supply either ran out or people waited in line for hours to roll up their sleeves.

"There's a feeling so strong in Canada of equity, but in that case, equity went by-the-by because it was the strongest and the fittest who got the vaccine, really," McKinnon said. "Equity is sometimes making decisions about prioritization -- so you can get those who are most vulnerable vaccinated first."

From the beginning, Saskatchewan kept to the nationally agreed-to sequencing protocol, which identified priority groups -- the first being health-care workers, followed by high-risk individuals with underlying medical conditions.

McKinnon pointed out the province's pandemic response was a team effort involving many medical and non-medical staff who organized and delivered more than 500,000 doses of H1N1 vaccine.

"There was something like 20 or 30 people involved day-to-day on the H1N1 -- it was quite an event, really," McKinnon said. "It went across ministries as well. There was a lot of inter-ministerial departmental meetings with Corrections and Justice and Education. It was really a cross-disciplinary response to the pandemic. I was just the one that was in front of the cameras."

Her calm demeanor at numerous news conferences was accompanied by her soft-spoken, but clear, updates about the virus activity in the province. Her wealth of public health experience was evident.

Not only has McKinnon worked in western Australia, but also in the United Kingdom, Russia and China.

As the key advisor for communicable diseases and emergencies for Australia's federal government, she dealt with many crises including the 2002 Bali bombings, anthrax, avian influenza and the 2004 Asian tsunami. Those experiences taught her that before a public health decision can be made, it's important to gather all of the evidence and information.

"It's never enough -- but you have to make a decision," she said. "It's very important to keep calm and assess all of that information and make the best decision on the information that you've got."

The voice of the community helped health officials decide who should be immunized first.

"In Saskatchewan, it came back to us in many forms -- through HealthLine, through direct e-mails and phone calls," McKinnon said. "Saskatchewan wanted the children immunized, and that came from the seniors as well. It's important to respect and understand that community voice on top of all the information. It's a big part of the decision-making."

While the H1N1 virus was milder than expected, it could have had a huge effect, she said.

"It was a very valuable event to go through because we're going to have another one like it," McKinnon said. "There's no doubt."

Infectious disease threats are inevitable because of increasing world travel and the close interaction between the planet's nine billion people and two billion animals.

Most of the viruses from which humans get clinical diseases come from animals. "That interface continues with pets at home and farming, and the increasing need for animals for food," McKinnon said.

While the pandemic grabbed headlines around the world, work continued on the province's HIV strategy.

"We have the highest incidence rate of HIV -- in fact, it's twice the national average -- and the biggest increase is in young aboriginal women, so that's the next thing that we need to look at now," McKinnon said. "The things that are driving this HIV epidemic in Saskatchewan are the same things that will make it occur anywhere else in Canada, so we need to get a national approach; the strategy won't work unless all the parties are part of it, including the community."

She believes the lessons of collaboration learned during the H1N1 outbreak can be used to address the HIV crisis.

"The other thing I learned from H1N1 is the public want to listen and be part of the decision making," she said. "They do care."

Looking ahead to the new year, McKinnon rates environmental concerns high on the agenda as more industry is poised to come to Saskatchewan. Also on her radar are the growing problems of diabetes, obesity and Saskatchewan's high injury rate.

She's pleased with the smoking legislation that was proclaimed while H1N1 was prominent. The next challenge, she said, is ensuring it's enforced.

Despite 2009's many challenges, the year is ending on a high note for McKinnon.

"Saskatchewan has got such a history with the introduction of medicare, which Australia now has. So many good things have come out of Saskatchewan and I'm very impressed by the people here and the spirit of collaboration -- it's a pleasure to work here and a pleasure to be here," she said. "I think Saskatchewan can lead in health and has led in health. I'm pleased to be part of the team."

News
H1N1: Anatomy of a pandemic
Sharon Kirkey
Canwest News Service
1941 words
28 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A8
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

On April 26, Canada announced its first cases of swine flu -- two in B.C., and four in Nova Scotia among private school students home from a school trip to the Yucatan Peninsula. The U.S. declared a health emergency after confirming a total of 20 swine flu cases in the country. In Mexico, more than 1,000 people were placed under observation, and Mexico City went into virtual lockdown, shutting schools, museums and theatres.

In Canada, the government issued a travel advisory for Mexico. School trips were cancelled. Catholic priests in Edmonton told worshippers to stop shaking hands in peace and just nod their heads instead. The Funeral Service Association of Canada issued an updated pandemic planning guide that suggested funeral homes set aside a six-month supply of gloves, masks, face shields, gowns, embalming chemicals, body bags "or other impervious shrouds," and to prepare for a possible increased demand for cremation caskets and "economically-priced caskets, especially since there may be multiple deaths in a single family." Funeral directors were advised that, while there were no conclusive studies on the flu's survival rate in a dead body, "it is a best practice to assume infection up to 48 hours after death." Masking or covering the mouths and noses of the deceased was suggested.

Canadian intensive-care doctors and researchers, working with colleagues in Mexico, moved quickly to collect, analyze and disseminate data on the sickest patients, because doctors were being forced to go on precious little. Who was it attacking? Who was getting sick? What therapies seemed to work? What were the danger signals that someone was getting very sick and needed aggressive treatment?

All the evidence for treating influenza had been on "walking, talking" patients with typical flu -- "the old influenza we used to talk about, the kind that makes you sick for a few days, and you get better," says Dr. Anand Kumar, an intensivist with the Winnipeg Regional Health Authority and associate professor of critical care and infectious disease at the University of Manitoba. There was virtually no evidence on critically ill patients.

Soon, Kumar and other critical care physicians were coming up against the limits of "evidence-based" medicine, which dictates doing what the best evidence says works. But the Canadians were discovering some key assumptions were wrong.

"The national guidelines said that certain tests for this condition are 100 per cent sensitive. If it's negative, it's negative. If it's positive, it's positive," Kumar says. Except doctors were seeing cases that were coming up negative on testing that, clinically, were screaming they were real cases.

"There was a lot of confusion. Do you treat them? Do you not treat them? Those of us in the ICU said, `We're just going to treat them."' The guidelines also said to treat for five days with antivirals. "But we're looking at these guys and they're still sick after five days. We think we need to treat longer. A lot of us said, `To heck with that, we're going to treat for 10 days, because that seems more reasonable.'

"Now that we've had a chance to actually study it, it turns out we were right."

Research by Kumar and other members of the Canadian Critical Care Trials Group, a 200-plus-member group of critical care researchers working to improve the care and outcomes of critically sick children and adults, was shared with countries worldwide.

"I've seen the written recommendations, like the English recommendations, for the management of severe H1N1 disease," Kumar says. "They are virtually verbatim what we told them."

Back in Ottawa, Earl Brown, virologist and director of the University of Ottawa's Emerging Pathogens Research Centre, was anxious to study this new viral threat as well. But Health Canada initially declared H1N1 a Level 3 pathogen -- an infectious agent transmitted by an airborne route that can cause serious or life-threatening disease. The plague is a Level 3 pathogen. So are SARS and yellow fever.

Around the same time, federal legislation was passed that made it illegal to research substances of this type, unless in a certified, biosafety Level 3 facility, labs that are in short supply in Canada.

"We couldn't work on it, because our new Level 3 facility wasn't yet certified, and hardly any labs could work on it, because they were saying this is a real scary virus, it's containment Level 3," Brown says.

"We were sitting on our hands, and most other people were too, and everybody was getting nervous, there was all this hype about it."

What Brown found most bizarre was that, at that time, many schools in Ottawa had decided not to notify parents if a case of swine flu was identified, because the level of risk of severe disease in infected schoolchildren wasn't considered severe enough to warrant notifying parents.

Brown said the pathogen should not have been deemed level 3. He remembers sitting in federal advisory committee meetings, thinking, "They don't trust scientists enough to work on this thing, but it's not important enough to notify the parents of children when schools have the infection? It was getting a little bit Kafkaesque."

But as the spring wave slowed, and H1N1 activity died down, so too did public sense of urgency and seriousness. The virus wasn't cutting a swath across Asia and South America. Horror fantasies about the flu are always about the possibility of a repeat of Spanish flu, of horse-drawn carriages carrying corpses, but people weren't dropping dead in the streets. When Canada announced it was buying an adjuvanted vaccine, some feared the vaccine more than the virus itself. An adjuvant is a chemical added to the vaccine to boost the body's immune response.

"You had the steady drumbeat of instant experts who were saying how the adjuvant is going to kill way more people than the disease," says Dr. Brian Ward, an infectious-disease physician and associate director of the Research Institute at McGill University Health Centre.

Then Evan Frustaglio died on the very day the H1N1 flu shot clinics opened, and everything changed. "All of a sudden, everybody wanted it," Brown, of the University of Ottawa, says. "You had people swarming the ERs, and the second wave was on."

Frustrations grew as the vaccine was released in fits and starts. Clinics opened to chaos before organizers learned to handle the crowds. In the early days, many clinics had hundreds of people standing in line for hours, often outdoors, and often learning after a four- or five-hour wait that the day's supply of vaccine had run out. Parents had to take care of preschoolers for hours without leaving their spots in line. Some outdoor lines had no washrooms nearby. Many had nowhere to get food or drink.

There were complaints of vaccines being delivered to clinics after office hours, of not enough syringes or alcohol swabs, and the logistics of recruiting the sheer numbers of nurses and doctors needed to give the injections.

The flu made it impossible to do some other public health work.

Restaurant and water inspections couldn't stop. "But the primary (non-flu) immunizations for young kids, the first series had to keep going, but the booster shots for 18-months and four-year-olds, some of those were delayed," says Dr. Cory Neudorf, chair of the Canadian Public Health Association and chief medical health officer with the Saskatoon Health Region.

Many of the screening programs used to pick up problems, such as failure to thrive, vision or hearing problems, had to be delayed. "It basically touched every part of public health."

"There will be certain new moms who aren't visited, babies who may end up never getting that screening appointment, immunization rates even. We've seen in past crises like this there's a cohort of kids that just never catch up," he says. "That's nagging a bit."

Experts say it's too early for H1N1's post-mortem, that a third wave of infection could hit in 2010. We're only now entering what is normally the height of flu season. The worst-case scenario? A complete re-assortment of the virus, where H1N1 swaps genes with another flu virus and a more lethal pandemic emerges.

Ward, of McGill, says that, in retrospect, officials overestimated the impact of H1N1.

But "they would have been crazy not to have reacted the way they did. They would have been crazy, because there was no way that you can predict what's going to happen. In public health, people are always going to get slammed after the fact. If it turns out to be worse than you said, you're going to get yelled at. And if turns out to be better than you said, people are going to say 'Didn't you overreact?' "

The news coming out of Mexico was that this was a bad and different bug, that it killed nearly half of those who became critically sick. The genetics of the microbe had everybody frightened, Ward says, because it was a virus like no one had seen before. In May, the experts were still arguing about whether or not older people were actually protected against infection, or whether the ones that weren't getting sick just hadn't visited Mexico.

What's clear is that no computer simulation could have given the country, or the world, as much information as we now have about how to respond to a public health emergency.

"We've basically had our bluff called," Ward says. "Nature came along and said, 'OK, you say you're ready, are you?' In many instances we were, and in some instances we weren't. This simulation has brought us a huge distance toward being able to respond far more competently."

Where public health officials fell hardest, he and others say, was in communicating risks. The deaths of 13-year-old Evan Frustaglio and 10-year-old Vanetia Warner within days of one another shook a nation.

"If those poor children hadn't died, I think we would be looking at a 15 per cent vaccine uptake," Ward says. "Those deaths galvanized a lot of people. But we should not have to rely on that kind of serendipity to be able to communicate these kinds of risks."

In Toronto, in the intensive care unit at Sunnybrook Health Sciences Centre, critical care physician Dr. Robert Fowler has treated patients so ill with H1N1 that not even turning them on their stomachs to ventilate improves oxygen flow, and often the only option is to insert tubes into large arteries, and circulate their blood through a machine that adds oxygen, bypassing the lungs completely.

H1N1 has consumed Fowler and his colleagues for months.

"It's the kind of thing you don't go through very often, especially in the early stages of this, when you're not sure where this is going to go. You kind of say to yourself, 'This is a pandemic, people are getting sick and dying because of it.' This is sort of what you do for a living" -- trying to keep people alive, he says, when their bodies are trying to die.

Keeping a patient with severe H1N1 disease on a ventilator for months is a substantial response to try to save one life.

"But these people are generally young, and otherwise healthy. And if they can be brought through this eventually, well, they'll go back to being themselves."

Facebook's top 15 status terms
Amy Minsky
Canwest News Service

Who knew there was room for anger in the kingdom of Internet fads ruled by LOLCats?

"FML," a digital slang used to express frustration, ranked second among Facebook's top 15 words and phrases used in American status updates in 2009.

The acronym, which stands for "f-- my life," came blazing onto the Facebook lexicon scene, moving from a relatively low usage rate to one of the most mainstream status updates in 2009, according to the social networking site's data team.

The month of May -- a time when many students are wrapping up their final exams and hunting for summer jobs -- saw the biggest spike in statuses containing the acronym. After a relative lull in usage during the summer, FML crept back toward chart-topping levels throughout the first semester of a new school year.

Facebook's data team also noticed a weekly trend in the number of frustrated status updates. "As we'd expect ... Mondays and Tuesdays are particularly rough," the data team said in a Facebook note. "Surprisingly, it is not Mondays that rate highest, but Tuesdays."

In order to make the list more comprehensive, the data team used keywords to group similar items.

Keywords "Michael Jackson," "Patrick Swayze" and "Billy Mays" were used to map the frequency for status updates mentioning celebrity deaths (fourth place); movie titles, such as New Moon, Transformers and The Hangover were used to track discussion of movies (sixth place); "Easter, "Lord" and "God" were used to track the prevalence of religion in Facebook statuses (14th place).

Twitter, the micro-blogging site, came in a respectable 10th place, even though activity peaked in April and has been decreasing steadily ever since.

Twitter also released a list of most-tweeted topics of the year. Its list was broken down into categories of news events, people, movies, TV shows, technology, sports and hashtags. While Harry Potter, the Super Bowl and American Idol each topped one of the top 10 lists, Facebook was nowhere to be found.

RAPTORS ON ROLL

Chris Bosh had 25 points and 16 rebounds, Jarrett Jack scored 17 points and the Toronto Raptors extended their winning streak to a season-high four games, beating the Detroit Pistons 102-95 Sunday in Toronto. Even with Richard Hamilton, Tayshaun Prince and Ben Gordon all back in the lineup, the Pistons couldn't stop their losing streak from reaching seven games. Detroit has not topped 100 points in any of those defeats. Hamilton and Gordon each scored 15 and Jonas Jerebko and Rodney Stuckey both had 14 for the Pistons. Ben Wallace had 10 points and nine rebounds.

HOLIDAY CLASSIC

The OUA champion Windsor Lancers host two CIS women's basketball teams in the four-day Holiday Classic today through Thursday at the St. Denis Centre. Today, the No. 2 nationally ranked Lancers (8-0) face Toronto (4-4), currently sixth in the OUA East conference, at 7 p.m.

BOWL HISTORY

Sarah Thomas made history at the Pizza Bowl Saturday, becoming the first woman to officiate a bowl game when she worked the matchup between Ohio and Marshall at Ford Field. Thomas is one of five women officiating in major college football, but Pizza Bowl spokesman Tim Moore said she was the first to draw an assignment for a Bowl Subdivision post-season game. "It was an honour," Thomas said after Marshall's 21-17 win.

CHANGE OF HEART

Florida coach Urban Meyer is taking an indefinite leave of absence instead of resigning and expects to be on the sideline leading the Gators when next season opens.

"I do in my gut believe that will happen," Meyer said Sunday at a Sugar Bowl news conference. Offensive co-ordinator Steve Addazio will run the team during Meyer's absence, but Meyer will be the coach against No. 4 Cincinnati New Year's Day.

SPARTANS TO FACE POTTS

Texas Tech coach Mike Leach announc-ed junior quarterback Taylor Potts will start over junior Steven Sheffield against Michigan State in the Jan. 2 Alamo Bowl. Potts started nine games for the 8-4 Red Raiders this season. Michigan State is making a third consecutive bowl appearance for the first time since 1995-97, and it is the second-longest post-season streak in school history.

ROONEY REBOUNDS

Wayne Rooney went from villain to hero as Manchester United won 3-1 at Hull Sunday to move within just two points of Premier League leaders Chelsea. Rooney put United in front with his sixth goal in as many games on the stroke of half time but his poor back pass to goalkeeper Tomasz Kuszczak led to Craig Fagan's penalty equalizer. The England striker ensured his mistake did not prove costly by going on to set up the champions' two other goals.

LAST-DITCH EFFORT

Promoter Bob Arum delivered what he said would be his last proposal Sunday to salvage the fight between Manny Pacquiao and Floyd Mayweather Jr., which has been on life support over a blood testing dispute. Under Arum's proposal, the Nevada Athletic Commission would have the final say in how much testing there would be for the fight and when it would take place.

ESCOBAR CLOSE TO DEAL

A person familiar with the negotiations told The Associated Press that injury-plagued pitcher Kelvim Escobar and the New York Mets have reached a preliminary agreement on a US$1.25-million, one-year-contract.

CREW MEMBER DIES

A crew member for Richard Childress Racing has died of complications from the H1N1 virus. Donald (DJ) Richardson died late Friday after a lengthy hospitalization for acute respiratory distress syndrome.

Jack Knox
Monitor
15 memorable moments; We learned a new language in 2009, phrases like 'unadjuvanted vaccine,' 'bascule bridge,' 'stimulus package' and 'bleeping HST.' Jack Knox looks back at stories -- some significant, some definitely not -- that you'll remember.
JACK KNOX
Times Colonist
2693 words
27 December 2009
Victoria Times Colonist
VTC
Final
D1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2009 Victoria Times Colonist

1 H1N1: Bitten by the bug

Victoria's Michelle Bossi was front-page news in April: Vancouver Island's first confirmed case of the H1N1 flu, which she contracted on vacation in Mexico, where the pandemic broke out this spring.

The global panic (or prudence, depending on your perspective) spread faster than the flu itself. Metchosin's Hans Helgesen Elementary closed for a week in May after students became sick (they tested negative). A woman was asked to leave a Victoria Transit bus because other passengers feared her coughing. We learned to sneeze in our elbows, eschew the handshake, pump hand sanitizer like Texas crude.

In mid-September, a woman from the Beecher Bay reserve became the first of 10 people on Vancouver Island to die of the disease.

By October, it was a race to see which you would get first, the flu or the flu shot, which became 2009's Tickle Me Elmo, the must-have gift in short supply. Victorians waited patiently in three-hour queues reminiscent of Soviet bread lines (or Boxing Day at Future Shop) as the scarce vaccine was meted out to the most vulnerable (and the Calgary Flames).

The pandemic wasn't as bad as we feared; by mid-December it had killed 51 British Columbians (the seasonal flu typically kills 400 to 800) and about 10,000 worldwide. That raises the question: Have we overreacted to H1N1, or has all that elbow-sneezing and vaccinating prevented it from being worse?

2 MICHAEL JACKSON DIED

Judging from the response, the overdose death of the 50-year-old King of Pop was the worst thing to happen in the world ever, even though there's a strong suspicion that Wacko Jacko was what would normally be called a child-molesting pervert. Apparently his celebrity/talent upgraded his status to "troubled genius," which is what we call famous people when we want to make excuses for them.

Not since Princess Diana had we seen this kind of global grief, or at least ghoulish fascination. Yahoo.com reported "Michael Jackson" was the top search term of 2009 (followed by "Twilight"). His name appeared in the Times Colonist more than 100 times in 2009. A flash mob danced Thriller in Centennial Square.

Jackson's death celebutrumped that of Farrah Fawcett, who died of cancer the same day, much to the sadness of anyone (not that I'm naming names here) who had her swimsuit poster pinned to the wall in 1977.

Both deaths came within days of that of John Houghtaling, inventor of the Magic Fingers coin-operated vibrating bed. The world was shaken.

3 THIS WASN'T IN THE BROCHURE

Jeez, I must be blind. I'm looking through the provincial Liberals' election platform and try as I might I can't find anything about the HST, or axing public servants, or cutting health care, or throwing non-profit groups under the bus. What I can find is Gordon Campbell drawing a line in the sand and insisting that the budget deficit won't be higher than the $495 million announced in February.

Anyhoo, just two months after winning another majority in May, the Liberals thank the voters by -- surprise! -- announcing the 12 per cent HST, which will take effect July 1, 2010, adding to the cost of a whole range of goods that are currently free of provincial sales tax.

And, oh, that cast-in-stone budget deficit? It's suddenly $2.8 billion.

If there's good news for the Liberals, it's that the next election isn't until May 2013 and voters have the collective memory of a fruit fly.

4 GUNS AND DRUGS

A series of high-profile murder trials and major drug busts signalled that we're going to have to dig a deeper moat if we want to isolate ourselves from the bloody narco-wars on the Lower Mainland.

Drug dealer Daniel Aitken was actually convicted of two separate murders; he shot his friend Alex McLean in Esquimalt in 2003 and Adan Merino in Victoria in 2004. Both McLean and Merino were involved in the drug world. Aitken had earlier done time for killing yet another Victoria dealer, Samir Shamoon, in 1995.

A teenager tied to the Red Scorpions, a gang trying to make inroads on Vancouver Island, was convicted of killing UVic student Philbert Truong, whose only crime was to try to save a friend after a minor dispute outside the Red Jacket nightclub in 2008.

Late in the year, former Highlands councillor Ken Brotherston and his two sons went on trial for the slaying of Keith Taylor at a West Shore house frequented by crack addicts.

In September, eight people are arrested in the region's biggest-ever drug bust. Close to 100 cops raid five locations in Saanich, Colwood and Langford, netting $2.25 million in cocaine, $420,600 in cash and four high-powered handguns. Also in September, Victoria-based RCMP revealed that a two-year investigation had resulted in charges against 11 people and the seizure of millions of dollars worth of cocaine, ecstasy, GHB and crystal meth.

Yes, this is last year's story, but the effects were felt in 2009.

5 THE MELTDOWN

Victoria's unemployment rate doubled to seven per cent. The implosion of the Traveller's Inn chain was the most visible sign of trouble in the hospitality industry in this, the year of the staycation.

The half-built Spencer Road interchange, the bridge to nowhere, became a symbol of stalled development, particularly on the West Shore, where projects like Silkwind, the $1-billion Colwood Corners renewal and the $1.4-billion Capella condo towers at Bear Mountain were shelved. (The trials of Bear Mountain's Len Barrie were a yet-to-be-finished chapter in themselves.)

In town, the $160-million Radius mixed-use development is on hold, but work on the Uptown Centre, the Falls, the Hudson and local hospitals bucked the construction trend. Perhaps the biggest head-scratcher was the way real estate rebounded.

Yes, this is next year's story, but the effects were felt in 2009.

6 THE OLYMPICS

Thousands of people cheered when the Olympic torch relay began in Victoria on Oct. 30, a succession of jubilant runners holding aloft what appeared to be a giant flaming doobie. Thousands also jeered the most counter-productive demonstration ever: Anti-Olympics activists (including someone who threw marbles in the path of police horses) disrupted the relay, ruining the day for disabled kids and other would-be participants. (What's next, shooting puppies to protest homelessness?)

There is, to say the least, ambivalence about the 2010 Games, the uplifting nature of the event itself tempered by the associated cost, inconvenience and inaccessibility. The $900-million security measures will trip up travellers and all but shut down B.C.'s criminal court system for the month of February as 6,000 police officers from across Canada are seconded to the Games.

7 MEDIA MUDDLE

The good news story as Canadian media struggled to stay afloat in a sea of red ink: CHEK didn't drown. Employees and local investors bought the station at the beginning of September just as the plug was about to be pulled by debt-heavy Canwest Global.

Other local outlets were wobbly, too. Canwest also owns the Times Colonist, which dropped its Monday edition in June. At Black Press, which owns Monday Magazine and the various community papers around Victoria, employees were pushed to take unpaid days off work; Torstar, which owns a 19.4 per cent stake in Black Press, declared the holdings to have no value. In March, CTVglobemedia Inc. cut 118 jobs -- 28 per cent of the total workforce -- at 'A' News stations across Canada, including 18 of 100 jobs in Victoria, where they also cancelled the morning show. Several jobs were also axed at CFAX radio, owned by CTVglobemedia subsidiary CHUM.

8 STIMULUS SPENDING

To those who shrug and say, "I get my news from the Internet," where do you think the Internet gets its news?

Here's a new drinking game: Every time you hear the words "stimulus package," "shovel ready" or "infrastructure" on the TV news, knock back a beer. You'll be gooned before the weatherman comes on. In the capital region, the feds threw millions at everything from a McTavish Road interchange that we're not sure is the highest highway priority to a blue bridge replacement that we're not sure we need at all. The most important municipal construction project completed in 2009: The permanent outdoor urinal erected at Government and Pandora.

9 THOW AND HERTEL

The long arm of the law finally nabbed Victoria's two most infamous financial fugitives by their soiled white collars.

Investment adviser Ian Thow, who fled his Central Saanich waterfront estate in 2005, was arrested Feb. 17 in Portland, Ore., where he had been living in a high-end condo. He now languishes in the North Fraser Pretrial Centre in Port Coquitlam, charged with 25 counts of fraud. He is accused of bilking clients of $32 million.

Frank Hertel, once lauded as a high-tech visionary who would turn Victoria into Silicon Valley North, took off for Venezuela rather than face tax-evasion charges laid in 1986. The onetime CFAX Citizen of the Year, now in his 70s, has been jailed in England, awaiting word on extradition, since being arrested at Heathrow airport May 9.

10 MIGRANTS 2.0

It's deja vu all over again: Gun-toting authorities shepherd a rusty shipload of migrants into Ogden Point on Oct. 18, a day after the ship is intercepted off the West Coast. Except unlike 1999, when the arrival of four boatloads of Chinese set Islanders aflutter, the 76 men aboard the Ocean Lady, seized in Juan de Fuca Strait, were Tamils from Sri Lanka.

The men remain in custody in Vancouver, but Canada will have to decide soon whether to free them as legitimate refugees or send them home.

11 THE INTERWEB

This was the year your grandma unfriended you on Facebook, then Tweeted about it.

We all became so connected in 2009 that we couldn't hear anything beyond our ear buds, see anything beyond our screens. Your new handheld device does so much ("There's an app for that") that to call it a cellphone is like calling the Swiss army knife a knife.

B.C. finally decided to ban phoning/Googling/texting/sexting while driving as of Jan. 1, 2010. The Google Street View cameras came to Victoria in July, blurring faces and licence plates to satisfy privacy concerns. The dashboard GPS, the best marriage-saving device since monogamy, became common. This month, a viral video (another term you just learned) showed a groom pausing in mid-wedding to change his Facebook status to "married."

12 ROYAL VISITORS

It was a republican's nightmare: Visiting royals stacked up like 747s circling O'Hare in a blizzard. You couldn't set foot in downtown Victoria without having it run over by a speeding motorcade, somebody's crowned head poking through the sunroof of the limo.

In July came Emperor Akihito of Japan, returning with Empress Michiko to the city he first visited in 1953 while travelling to the coronation of Queen Elizabeth. Their visit was sandwiched between that of Elizabeth's youngest son, Prince Edward, and her eldest, Prince Charles, who was heard whispering excitedly to Camilla, "I think I just saw Jack Knox." Or maybe not.

It was like something out of CSI: Vic West, except this story was real, which made it sad.

13 SONGHEES SKELETON

In late May, a skeleton found sitting on a box in the back of a five-tonne truck abandoned in a Roundhouse project parking lot turned out to be that of Neil McLean, a homeless man in his late 60s. McLean, who had mental health issues, had been reported missing in January by his Alberta family, who had not seen him for two years. He hadn't been seen around Our Place for about a year. It appears McLean chained the truck shut from the inside, which he was known to do as a security measure.

14 THE RUMBLES

Homelessness continues to eat away at Victoria like a cancer.

It sounds like an earthquake, or God bowling, or James Earl Jones clearing his throat. Whatever it is, all summer long Victorians report this loud, unexplained rumbling noise that continues for up to 30 seconds at a time. Since many of the reports come from Oak Bay and Cordova Bay, the most logical explanation has something to do with a new type of fighter jet taking off from Washington state's Whidbey Island Naval Air Station.

Either that or your bad decisions from the '60s are coming back for a visit, Cheech.

15 OH, DEER

Sure, there's nothing new about deer in residential areas. But deer roaming Oak Bay village, cruising Yates Street, swimming in a Saanich pool, browsing in a Government Street toy store, or hogging the comfy seats at Starbucks? (OK, I made up that last one.) Urban-born deer were all over the City of Gardens (or, as they call it, Buffet By The Sea) this year.

Bunnies were on the move, too, spreading beyond UVic, where it was proposed that some of the 86 million feral rabbits be trapped, given vasectomies and adopted out to appropriate homes. This wasn't done, but nor did Victorians go for the bunny-control measures used in Helsinki (feeding them to zoo animals) or Stockholm (burning them as heating fuel).

We were confounded by critters in 2009. It took several levels of government to deal with a dead cow that washed up at Ogden Point. (They ended up shipping it to Alberta, which could explain why our taxes are so high). Suzy the Tiger finally left the Highlands for a ranch in the Interior.

And of course, we can't forget the biggest animal story of 2009: Tiger Woods turned out to be a cheetah.
